KEY POINTS

The physician’s initial encounter with an infertile couple is the most important one because it sets the tone for subsequent evaluation and treatment. Factors from either or both partners may contribute to difficulties in conceiving. Therefore, it is important to understand male and female reproductive physiology and consider all possible diagnoses before pursuing invasive treatment.

1 The main causes of infertility include male factor, decreased ovarian reserve, ovulatory

disorders (ovulatory factor), tubal injury such as blockage or paratubal adhesions (including endometriosis with evidence of tubal or peritoneal adhesions), uterine factors, systemic conditions (including infections or chronic diseases such as obesity, autoimmune conditions, or chronic renal failure), cervical and immunologic factors, and unexplained factors. 2 The basic investigations that should be performed before starting any infertility treatment

are a semen analysis, testing to confirm ovulation, and documentation of tubal patency. Ovarian reserve screening is often also performed. 3 Male factor is the sole cause of infertility in approximately 20% of infertile couples, but it

may be a contributing factor in as many as 50% of cases. Within this category are same- sex female couples desiring to conceive (they would not necessarily be considered traditionally infertile, but they would need donor sperm). Treatment of reversible anatomic, endocrine, or infectious causes of subfertility, such as a varicocele repair, cessation of suppressive medications or toxic behaviors, and treatment of sexually transmitted diseases and thyroid disorders, may be efficacious. Intrauterine insemination (IUI) is the best studied and most widely practiced of all the insemination techniques. Intracytoplasmic sperm injection (ICSI) has allowed couples with male factor infertility to achieve assisted reproductive technology (ART) pregnancy outcomes that are comparable with those of couples with nonmale factor infertility using conventional in vitro fertilization (IVF) treatment. 4 An association between the age of the woman and reduced fertility has been well-

documented. The decline in fecundability begins in the early 30s and accelerates during the late 30s and early 40s. Chemotherapeutics may hasten loss of ovarian reserve and may prompt fertility preservation counseling. Similar counseling should be undertaken for male cancer patients. 5 Disorders of ovulation account for about 30% to 40% of all cases of female infertility.

These disorders are generally among the most easily diagnosed and most treatable causes of infertility.

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1862"></a>

صفحه 1862

6 The most common cause of oligo-ovulation and anovulation—both in the general

population and among women presenting with infertility—is polycystic ovarian syndrome (PCOS).

7 Tubal and peritoneal factors account for approximately 30% to 40% of cases of female

infertility. Cervical factor is estimated to be a cause of infertility in no more than 5% of infertile couples. Uterine pathologies contribute to infertility in as many as 15% of couples seeking treatment. Leiomyomas have not been shown to be a direct cause of infertility. If the evaluation is negative (i.e., unexplained), then superovulation with IUI may be considered, though with less success than ART. 8 ART techniques include utilization of gametes from autologous or third-party sources.

Such techniques include IVF, ICSI (using ejaculated or surgically retrieved sperm that is either fresh or cryopreserved), gamete intrafallopian transfer (GIFT), zygote intrafallopian transfer (ZIFT), oocyte and embryo cryopreservation, and the use of gestational carriers. Because of improved success rates associated with IVF embryo transfer, the performance of GIFT and ZIFT has declined dramatically. Embryo screening using morphokinetic methods, PGD, and PGS are also utilized. 9 Multiple gestation, especially higher-order multiple gestation, is a serious complication of

infertility treatment and has tremendous medical, psychological, social, and financial implications. 10 Other complications of ART can occur, but stress is the most common reason for patients

to discontinue fertility therapy. Improved protocols can minimize ovarian hyperstimulation syndrome (OHSS) risk, and recent studies have not shown an increased risk for breast, uterine, or ovarian cancer secondary to medications used for superovulation in the treatment of infertility. Although concerns have been raised about congenital abnormalities in offspring, absolute risk seems to be low. 11 Information on the Society for Assisted Reproductive Technology (SART) and registered

ART clinics are accessible by the public at https://www.sartcorsonline.com/rptCSR_PublicMultYear.aspx?reportingYear=2021. The Centers for Disease Control (CDC) by law collects ART data and this is publicly available at ftp://ftp.cdc.gov/pub/Publications/art/ART-2015-Clinic-Report- Full.pdf#page=4.

Assessing the scope of infertility is hampered in part by the lack of standardized definitions (1). Infertility is variably defined as no pregnancy with unprotected intercourse for 1 year (2) or 2 years (1). The American Society for Reproductive Medicine (ASRM) recently released and expanded the definition of infertility as “a disease, condition, or status characterized by any of the following (3):

. “The inability to achieve a successful pregnancy based on a patient’s medical, sexual and

reproductive history, age, physical findings, diagnostic testing, or any combination of those factors.” . “The need for medical intervention, including, but not limited to, the use of donor gametes or

donor embryos in order to achieve a successful pregnancy either as an individual or with a partner.”

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1863"></a>

صفحه 1863

. In patients having regular, unprotected intercourse and without any known etiology for either

partner suggestive of impaired reproductive ability, evaluation should be initiated at 12 months when the female partner is under 35 years of age and at 6 months when the female partner is 35 years of age or older.”

In a separate document, the ASRM further clarifies that “women >40 years of age . . . may require more immediate evaluation and treatment” (2). In addition, they recommend immediate evaluation and intervention for couples with known causes of infertility, including, but not limited to: (1) irregular menses (intermenstrual bleeding, oligomenorrhea, amenorrhea), (2) known/suspected uterine, tubal or peritoneal diseases such as endometriosis; (3) known or suspected male factors; (4) sexual dysfunction, and (5) conditions that predispose to impaired ovarian reserve such as cancer treatments and genetic mutations (2).

Infertility may be further classified as primary infertility, in which no previous pregnancies have occurred, and secondary infertility, in which a prior pregnancy, although not necessarily a live birth, has occurred (4). About 90% of couples should conceive within 12 months of unprotected intercourse (5). The term subfertility is often used interchangeably with infertility (6). Fecundability refers variously to the probability of pregnancy per cycle or to the number of cycles to achieve a pregnancy (7). Fecundity refers to the probability of achieving a live birth (8). The diagnosis of impaired fecundity has been proposed to include couples with ≥36 months without conception or physical inability or difficulty in having a child (9).

EPIDEMIOLOGY Infertility affects approximately 186 million people globally; up to 15% of couples in their reproductive years are affected by some form of infertility (10,11). In the United States, demand for assisted reproductive services has skyrocketed, doubling from 150,000 ART cycles in 2009 to over 300,000 cycles in 2019 (12). That said, utilization of infertility services in the United States remains low, often because of the high cost of services and inconsistent and incomplete insurance coverage for fertility care. Inequitable access to care is just one of the many social determinants of health that result in persistent racial disparities in the utilization of infertility treatments (13).

INITIAL ASSESSMENT The physician’s initial encounter with an infertile couple is the most important one because it sets the tone for subsequent evaluation and treatment. It cannot be overemphasized that in partnered patients, infertility is a problem of the couple. The partner’s presence, beginning with the initial evaluation, involves them in the therapeutic process. 1 This essential involvement demonstrates the physician’s receptivity to the partner’s needs and those of the patient, allowing both members of the couple an opportunity to ask questions and voice any concerns.

2 The physician should obtain a complete medical, surgical, and urologic/gynecologic history from both partners. Specifically, information regarding menstrual cycle regularity, pelvic pain, and previous pregnancy outcomes is important. Risk factors for infertility, such as a history of pelvic inflammatory disease (PID) or pelvic surgery, should be reviewed. A history of intrauterine exposure to diethylstilbestrol (DES) is significant, but is now rarely elicited, as use

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1864"></a>

صفحه 1864

in pregnancy essentially stopped in 1971 (14). In addition, a review of systems relevant to pituitary, adrenal, and thyroid function is useful. Questions regarding galactorrhea, hirsutism, and changes in weight are particularly relevant. A directed history, including developmental defects such as undescended testes, past genital surgery, infections (including mumps orchitis), previous genital trauma, and medications should be obtained from the male partner. A history of occupational exposures that might affect the reproductive function of either partner is important, as is information about coital frequency, dyspareunia, and sexual dysfunction. Finally, information should be obtained on any family history of infertility, premature ovarian failure, congenital physical or intellectual impairments, and hereditary conditions relevant to preconception planning, such as cystic fibrosis, thalassemia, and Tay–Sachs disease.

1 The initial interview gives the physician the opportunity to assess the emotional impact of infertility on the couple. It gives the physician a chance to emphasize the emotional support available to the couple as they proceed with the diagnostic evaluation and suggested treatments. In most cases, referral to a trained social worker or psychologist may be beneficial and many fertility practices can now readily access these resources, often on-site.

The physical examination of the woman should be thorough, with attention given to height, weight, body habitus, hair distribution, thyroid gland, and pelvic examination. Referral of the male partner to a urologist for examination often is beneficial if historic information or subsequent evaluation suggests an abnormality. This initial encounter provides an opportunity to outline the general causes of infertility and discuss subsequent diagnostic and treatment plans (Figs. 36-1 to 36-3).

3 The basic investigations that should be performed before starting any infertility treatment are semen analysis, confirmation of ovulation, and documentation of tubal patency (Figs. 36-1 to 36-3). If a patient with severe systemic illness such as renal failure, liver failure, or cancer wishes to conceive, careful preconception assessment and counseling is advisable because the risks of fertility treatment and pregnancy can be substantial.

CAUSES OF INFERTILITY The main causes of infertility include:

. Male factor . Decreased ovarian reserve . Ovulatory factor . Tubal factor

تصاویر استخراج‌شدهٔ همین صفحه

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1868"></a>

صفحه 1868

. Uterine factor . Pelvic factor . Unexplained

The relative prevalence of the different causes of infertility varies widely among patient populations (Table 36-1) and has changed little over the past 25 years. Worldwide, male factor accounts for 51.2% (15) and tubal blockage for 25% to 35% (16) of infertility and subfertility (conception after attempting for 1 year). In Europe, ovulatory dysfunction accounts for 21% to 32%, male factor 19% to 57%, tubal factor 14% to 26%, unexplained 8% to 30%, endometriosis 4% to 6%, and combined male and female factors 34.4% of infertility (17–19). 4 Male factor is the only cause of infertility in about 20% of infertile couples, but it may be a contributing factor in as many as 50% of cases (19–21). Very few couples have absolute infertility, which can result from congenital or acquired irreversible loss of functional gametes in either partner or the absence of reproductive structures in either partner. 2 Spontaneous conception is less likely to occur with age >42, infertility duration >4 years, severe endometriosis, or severe tubal disorder (22), so patients with those features should be strongly encouraged to consider treatment rather than expectant management.

Table 36-1 1 Causes of Infertility

Relative Prevalence of the Etiologies of Infertility (%)

Male factor 17–28

Both male and female factors 8–39

Female factor 33–40

Unexplained infertility 8–28

Approximate Prevalence of the Causes of Infertility in the Female (%)

Ovulatory dysfunction 21–36

Tubal or peritoneal factor 16–28

Miscellaneous causes 9–12

Prevalence of etiologies of infertility (1,19,23,24)

Impact of Lifestyle on Fertility Frequently, patients will ask about modifiable lifestyle factors (diet, weight, tobacco use, alcohol intake, caffeine intake) and how these factors may affect their natural fertility. Data on this topic is frequently mixed, as most of the evidence is based on retrospective or observational studies that have high risk for confounding and recall bias.

Preconception Diet There is evidence that a diet rich in fruits, vegetables, whole grains, and omega-3 fatty acids and a diet low in saturated fats may improve fertility in women (25,26). In males, a systematic review including a mix of seven observational and cross-sectional studies concluded that a diet rich in fruits, vegetables, and fish and low in meat (especially processed meat), fat, processed food, and soy improves semen analysis parameters such as motility and concentration (27).

The SCOPE study included female patients attempting to conceive without fertility treatment and found that a lower intake of fruit and a higher intake of fast food were associated with

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1869"></a>

صفحه 1869

modest increases in infertility and a longer time to pregnancy (28). Given the available data, patients should be counseled to eat a balanced diet high in fruits and vegetables and low in saturated fat to optimize their fertility.

Vitamins/Micronutrients Women attempting to conceive should take a prenatal vitamin with 400 to 800 µg of folic acid to prevent neural tube defects. The data regarding supplementation in males is mixed with no clear fertility benefit of carnitine or selenium. There is some evidence to support the use of coenzyme Q10 to improve sperm parameters (27).

Body Mass Index Obesity affects reproductive potential for both males and females attempting natural conception, but also negatively impacts the success of fertility treatments (29,30). Females with class II obesity (BMI 35 to <40 kg/m2) experience longer time to pregnancy compared to females without obesity, and female obesity is associated with lower pregnancy rates with IVF compared to women who are not obese (31–33). Furthermore, patients with abdominal obesity appear to be at higher risk for anovulation than patients of a similar BMI but a smaller waist circumference. Potential etiologies for obesity-associated infertility include disruptions in the hypothalamic– pituitary–ovary axis leading to ovulatory dysfunction, diminished fertilization rates, and impaired endometrial gene receptivity (34).

The first-line treatment for patients with obesity is weight loss, to improve a patient’s fertility and decrease maternal and fetal risks when pregnancy is achieved. In females with class 1 Obesity (BMI 30 to 34.9 kg/m2), weight loss of ∼20 lb has been shown to improve the chance of achieving a spontaneously conceived live birth (35).

While much attention is spent on elevated BMI, patients who are underweight (BMI ≤18.5) also can experience decreased fertility. Patients should aim to achieve a normal BMI (18.5 to 25), through exercise and healthy diet, prior to conception.

Men with obesity are more likely to experience infertility. This may occur through various mechanisms including abnormal semen parameters, increased sperm DNA or mitochondrial damage, or through reduced gonadotropin secretion (36–39).

Tobacco Use Studies report that compared to nonsmokers, maternal and paternal tobacco smokers have lower live birth rates with intercourse (40), lower pregnancy rates using donor IUI (41) and partner IUI (42), and higher rates of IVF cycle cancellation prior to oocyte retrieval, even among female former smokers (43). Maternal tobacco use is associated with an increased risk of infertility and miscarriage. Furthermore, tobacco use may lead to an earlier menopausal transition through accelerated follicular depletion (44–48).

In males, the evidence that tobacco use impacts fertility is less clear. However, there is some data that either smoked (49,50) or chewed tobacco (51) is associated with decreased sperm density and motility.

Alcohol Use Studies are mixed on the effect of moderate alcohol intake and fertility outcomes. One study showed that maternal low to moderate daily intake of alcohol (<12 g) and caffeine (<200 mg) in the 1 year prior to ART did not impact ART outcomes (52). A different study reported an opposite outcome in which couples where both partners drank at least four drinks per week had a

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1870"></a>

صفحه 1870

significantly lower chance of live birth from IVF (53).

Given the lack of conclusive data on the amount of alcohol needed to impact fertility and/or pregnancy, the recommendation is to avoid heavy alcohol use (>2 drinks/day) in patients attempting to conceive (54).

In males, alcohol use does not seem to negatively affect sperm parameters, but alcoholism, especially when associated with liver disease, negatively affects semen analysis parameters (49,55).

Caffeine Intake Caffeine consumption in high levels (∼500 mg) can affect female fertility (56,57). The recommendation for people trying to conceive is to limit caffeine intake to less than 200 to 300 mg of caffeine per day (54).

Other Lifestyle Factors Data on marijuana use and fertility in males and females is scarce. Some studies show no effect on semen parameters, while separate studies report an association between marijuana use and abnormal semen parameters, though these studies are observational (58–60). Animal models support a negative effect of marijuana use on male fertility (61). Concurrent use of other illicit substances, such as cocaine, further decreased sperm quantity and negatively impacted motility in these models (60).

Females who use marijuana may have a slightly higher incidence of infertility (62). Further research is needed, but at this time, the recommendation should be made that couples attempting to conceive should avoid use of marijuana.

Male Factor The concept of a global decline in sperm counts is controversial (63,64). A recent systematic review and meta-regression analysis reported a significant overall decline in sperm concentration and total sperm count in North America (as well as Europe and Australia) since the 1970s, with no indication that this decrease is dampening (65,66). This systematic review by Levine et al., published in 2017, reports more than a 50% decrease in sperm concentration and total sperm count between the years of 1971 and 2011. Although decreases in sperm parameters have been noted in fertile men (67) and in US sperm donors (68), the clinical relevance for fecundability is unknown. However, one simulation model has suggested that if sperm concentrations decline by 21% to 47%, fecundability would decrease by 7% to 15% (69).

Physiology

Spermatogenesis The male reproductive tract consists of the testis, epididymis, vas deferens, prostate, seminal vesicles, ejaculatory duct, bulbourethral glands, and urethra. Gonadotropin-responsive cells in the testes include Leydig cells (the site of androgen synthesis) and Sertoli cells, which line the seminiferous tubules (the site of spermatogenesis) (70). 1 The pituitary gland secretes luteinizing hormone (LH), which stimulates the synthesis and secretion of testosterone by the Leydig cells, and follicle-stimulating hormone (FSH), which acts with testosterone on the Sertoli cells to stimulate spermatogenesis (70). In humans, a new cohort of spermatogonia enters the maturation process every 16 days, and the development from spermatogonia stem cells to the mature sperm cells takes about 75 days (71). Spermatogonia

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1871"></a>

صفحه 1871

undergo mitotic division to give rise to spermatocytes (70). These diploid spermatocytes undergo meiosis to produce haploid spermatids, which contain 23 (rather than 46) chromosomes (70). Maturation of spermatids is called spermiogenesis and involves condensation of the nucleus, formation of the flagellum, and the formation of the acrosome (a structure derived from the Golgi complex covering the tip or head of the sperm nucleus) (72). The resultant spermatozoa are released into the seminiferous tubule lumen and enter the epididymis, where they continue to mature and become progressively more motile during the 2 to 6 days that is required to traverse this tortuous structure and reach the vas deferens (73).

Sperm Transport

1 During ejaculation, mature spermatozoa are released from the vas deferens along with fluid from the prostate, seminal vesicles, and bulbourethral glands (74). The released semen is a gelatinous mixture of spermatozoa and seminal plasma; however, this thins out 20 to 30 minutes after ejaculation. This process, called liquefaction, is the direct result of proteolytic enzymes within the prostatic fluid (74). 1 Following ejaculation, the released spermatozoa must undergo capacitation to become competent to fertilize the oocyte (75). Capacitation occurs within the cervical mucus and involves removal of inhibitory mediators such as cholesterol from the sperm surface, tyrosine phosphorylation, and calcium ion influx, all of which allow the sperm to recognize additional fertilization cues during travel through the female reproductive tract (75). After the sperm reach the tubal isthmus, they are slowly released into the ampulla, further reducing the number of sperm that reach the oocyte (75). Sperm transport from the posterior vaginal fornix to the fallopian tubes occurs within 2 minutes during the follicular phase of the menstrual cycle (54).

Fertilization

1 As the capacitated sperm pass through cumulus cells surrounding the oocyte, hydrolytic enzymes are released from the sperm acrosome via exocytosis in a process called the acrosome reaction (75). Capacitation and the acrosome reaction can be induced in vitro (72,75). Following the acrosome reaction, the sperm binds to and penetrates the zona pellucida (the extracellular coat surrounding the oocyte). This allows fusion of the sperm with the plasma membrane of the oocyte, an event that promotes changes in the oocyte that prevent entry by additional sperm (75). As the first sperm penetrates, cortical granules are released (the cortical reaction) from the oocyte into the perivitelline space. This stops the oocyte’s zona pellucida from binding new sperm and inhibits penetration by previously bound sperm, further reducing the possibility of polyspermy (76).

Environmental Exposure and Male Fertility Many associations between environmental toxins (pesticides, endocrine disrupting chemicals [EDCs], heavy metals, air pollution) and male fertility have been studied. Decreased sperm concentration and motility have been noted in areas of the United States with heavy agriculture and pesticide use (67), but occupational exposures have not been linked to infertility (77).

While many associations between environmental exposures and male infertility have been postulated, more rigorous investigation is required (78).

Medications and Male Fertility Certain medications may reduce sperm numbers or function or may cause ejaculatory dysfunction (Table 36-2). Vaginal lubricants such as Astroglide, KY Jelly, saliva, and olive oil inhibit sperm motility in vitro, while no adverse effects are seen with hydroxethyl

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1872"></a>

صفحه 1872

cellulose (Pre-Seed), mineral oil, or canola oil (54). 3 However, Astroglide and KY Jelly do not appear to affect fecundability in couples without infertility who are engaging in procreative intercourse (79).

Table 36-2 1 Drugs that Can Impair Male Fertility

Impaired spermatogenesis—sulfasalazine, methotrexate, nitrofurantoin, colchicine, chemotherapy

Pituitary suppression—testosterone injections, gonadotrophin-releasing hormone analogs

Antiandrogenic effects—cimetidine, spironolactone

Ejaculation failure—α blockers, antidepressants, phenothiazines

Erectile dysfunction—β blockers, thiazide diuretics, metoclopramide

Drugs of misuse—anabolic steroids, cannabis, heroin, cocaine

Reproduced from Hirsh A. Male subfertility. BMJ. 2003;327(7416):669–672, with permission from BMJ Publishing Group Ltd.

Semen Analysis A semen analysis is a test to identify abnormal semen parameters, which can also identify health risks for the patient, as mortality rates have been positively associated with abnormal SAs. 3 The basic semen analysis measures semen volume, sperm concentration, sperm motility, and sperm morphology (21). No individual parameter is diagnostic of infertility, but the odds of infertility increase as the number of abnormal parameters increase (21). In 2021, the World Health Organization (WHO) released updated values and confidence intervals for the lower 5th percentile of semen parameters of over 3,500 fertile men from a more diverse geographic background (than in 2010) to serve as a semen analysis guide, updating its prior parameters released in 2010. These values are listed with the previously published guidelines in Table 36-3 (74,80); values below the cutoffs may not necessarily indicate infertility (80). However, significant deviations from the reference limits are generally classified as male factor infertility (21). Given regional differences between laboratories and in semen quality, laboratories are encouraged to develop their own reference ranges (80). There can be significant variability in semen analysis outcomes from ejaculate to ejaculate; therefore, it is prudent to evaluate at least two semen analyses for a given patient, especially if abnormal parameters are noted (21).

Table 36-3 3 Semen Analysis Terminology and Normal Values

Oligozoospermia Reduced sperm numbers Mild to moderate: 5–20 million/mL Severe: <5 million/mL

Asthenozoospermia Reduced sperm motility

Teratozoospermia Increased abnormal forms of sperm

Oligoasthenoteratozoospermia Sperm variables all subnormal

Azoospermia No sperm in semen

Aspermia (anejaculation) No ejaculate (ejaculation failure)

Abstinence

3 The general recommendation for the abstinence interval before producing an ejaculate to be examined is between 2 and 7 days (80). The epididymis stores the equivalent of three ejaculations (73), and therefore short abstinence is unlikely to significantly deplete sperm quantity in most men. A retrospective US study of 1,939 oligozoospermic, subfertile men found that >2 days abstinence was associated with more samples with >20 M total motile counts and higher ejaculate volume but no statistically significant impact on concentration, motility, or morphology (81). With >5 days of abstinence, oligozoospermic men had reduced total motile sperm count and normal morphology (82). Although longer abstinence of >2 days in normozoospermic men increased sperm concentration and total count, it was globally detrimental with respect to lower ejaculate volume, total and progressive motility, viability, and tail morphology (81). With prolonged abstinence >10 days, even normozoospermic men will have reduced total motile sperm count and reduced proportions of sperm with normal morphology (82), as sperm overflow into the urethra and are flushed out into the urine (80). Prolonged epididymal storage with heightened oxidative stress negatively affects quality in terms of elevated sperm DNA fragmentation (81) and may explain the improved pregnancy rates associated with shortening abstinence times to ≤2 days prior to specimen collection for intrauterine insemination (IUI) (83).

Specimen Collection The specimen should be obtained by masturbation and collected in a clean container kept at ambient temperature (80). 3 The patient should report any loss of the specimen, particularly the first portion of the ejaculate, which contains the highest sperm concentration (80). Collection may be performed either at home or in a private room near the laboratory (80). The sample should be taken to the laboratory preferably within 30 minutes, and no more than 50 minutes, from collection time, as the specimen needs to be evaluated within 1 hour from collection to prevent dehydration and degradation (80). The sample should be kept between 20 °C and 37 °C. If masturbation into a container is not possible, condoms specially designed for semen analysis should be used rather than latex condoms which are toxic to sperm (80). Intercourse to collect the sample is discouraged because of contamination risk (80). 3 Even when the specimen is obtained under optimal circumstances, interpretation of the results of

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1874"></a>

صفحه 1874

the semen analysis is complicated by variability within the same individual and overlap in infertile and fertile reference semen parameters (80). Semen parameters may vary widely from one man to another and among men with proven fertility (80).

Sperm Volume and pH The lower limit of normal semen volume is ≥1.4 mL. Less clinical interest is placed on the pH of the sperm, but if measured, this ideally should be equal to or above 7.2 (74,80). These parameters are affected mainly by the balance between the acidic secretions of the prostate gland and the alkaline fluid from the seminal vesicles (80). Low volume along with pH <7 suggests congenital absence of the vas deferens (74,80). 3 Difficulties with collection, retrograde ejaculation, or androgen deficiency can contribute to low volume (80). Consider further workup with urine voided immediately after ejaculation in patients with documented low semen volume <1 mL, after verifying with the man that no spill occurred during collection (21). Calculate the total sperm in the urine divided by (the total sperm in urine plus total sperm in ejaculate) multiplied by 100. A value >7.1% to 8.3% will be found in patients with partial retrograde ejaculation (84).

High volumes >5 mL suggest inflammation of the accessory glands (80).

Sperm Concentration Sperm concentration is defined as the number of sperm per milliliter in the total ejaculate and is positively related both to time to pregnancy and pregnancy rates (80). 3 The normal lower limit is ≥16 million/mL, revised from ≥20 million/mL in 1999 and ≥15 million/mL in 2012 (74,80,85). Only intact sperm are counted in determining sperm concentration (74). Fifteen percent to 20% of infertile men are azoospermic (no sperm) and 10% have a density of <1 million/mL (86).

Sperm Motility and Vitality Sperm motility is the percentage of progressively motile sperm in the ejaculate and is related to pregnancy rates. (80). Further classification of speed of motility was added back to the 2021 guidelines, as the extent of progressive sperm motility has been shown to be directly related to pregnancy rates. There are four distinct categories in which to grade motility: rapidly progressive, slowly progressive, nonprogressive, and immotile (80). Further elaboration on specifics for each category is beyond the scope of this chapter. The 5th percentile limit is now 42% total motility (progressive motility + nonprogressive motility), a slight increase from the 2010 value (74,80). The 5th percentile for progressive motility was slightly decreased to 30% in the 2021 report (80). When many immotile sperm are present or when the total sperm motility is <40%, vitality studies should be performed (80). Viable immotile sperm may have flagellar defects while the presence of nonviable immotile sperm suggests epididymal pathology (80).

Sperm Morphology Morphology testing assesses anatomic malformations of the sperm. 3 The lower limit for normal morphology is ≥4% using strict criteria, which was not a change from the 2010 WHO report (74,80). Assessment of sperm morphology involves fixing and staining a portion of the specimen (80). The strict Tygerberg criteria were introduced by Kruger et al. in 1986 to assess sperm morphology (80,87,88). Using this system, the entire spermatozoon—including the head, midpiece, and tail—is assessed, and even mild abnormalities in head forms are classified as abnormal (80). Most sperm from normal men exhibit minor abnormalities when subjected to

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1875"></a>

صفحه 1875

Tygerberg standards (74). An abnormality of sperm morphology is known as teratozoospermia and these sperm have poor fertilizing potential and may have abnormal DNA (80). Low strict sperm morphology (<9%) was a better discriminator for infertility than either count or motility in a large cross-sectional multicenter study (89). A disadvantage to any morphology assessment is that reproducibility may be hampered by the subjective nature of the assessment (80).

Round Cells, Bacteria, and Leukocytes

3 Round cells include immature germ cells and leukocytes (80). The prevalence of round cells in a large IVF cohort was 5.4%, of which 73% were found to be immature germ cells (90). Immature germ cell elevation is associated with lower sperm concentration and normal morphology, compromised sperm DNA integrity, lower fertilization, and higher pregnancy loss rates with intracytoplasmic sperm injection (ICSI) (90), while leukocytes (predominantly neutrophils) are associated with inflammation (74). However, the prognostic significance of leukocytes in semen is controversial; even low levels can be associated with reactive oxygen species and sperm DNA damage, but their presence has inconsistent effects on IVF/ICSI outcomes (91,92). Bacteriospermia prevalence among infertile men is ∼50%, with detection rates likely affected by semen collection technique and specific test parameters, and prevalence is not necessarily associated with leukocytospermia; it may represent asymptomatic colonization without an inflammatory response (90,93). The most common pathogens associated with bacteriospermia are Chlamydia trachomatis (41.4%), Ureaplasma urealyticum (15.5%), and Mycoplasma hominis (10.3%), with M. hominis and U. urealyticum having higher prevalence in men with leukocytospermia compared to those without (93).

Antisperm Antibodies Antisperm antibodies are often associated with sperm agglutination. They may be present in seminal plasma, on sperm surfaces and in maternal serum, and risk factors include history of ductal obstruction, prior genital infection, testicular trauma, and prior vasectomy reversal (21).

3 Antisperm antibody testing is limited by the lack of specific antibody binding, which is important because only a subset of antibodies is thought to have cytotoxic or agglutinating effects (94). Although the presence of antisperm antibodies has typically been managed with ICSI, it is uncertain whether that treatment is beneficial (95), and antibody testing would not be indicated if ICSI were planned a priori (21).

Sperm DNA Integrity Sperm DNA fragmentation is defined as sperm DNA damage in the form of double- or single-strand breaks and can be a result of defective spermatogenesis or oxidative stress, among other causes (80). Recent meta-analyses indicated that sperm DNA fragmentation may have a role in impaired embryo development, impaired implantation, and impaired pregnancies in both unassisted and ART pregnancies (94,96,97). Sperm chromatin structure assays, terminal deoxynucleotidyl transferase–mediated deoxyuridine triphosphate nick end labeling (TUNEL) assays, sperm chromatin dispersion tests, and single gel electrophoresis assays have varying thresholds for DNA damage diagnosis and varying positive predictive values (PPVs) for pregnancy with assisted reproduction; TUNEL assay results have the best correlation (98). These tests reflect the aggregate sperm DNA integrity of a heterogeneous sperm population rather than an individual fertilizing sperm, which limits the utility of this testing in the context of fertility treatment (98). 3 Nonetheless, such testing may add etiologic information, particularly in otherwise unexplained cases, and may prompt recommendations for reducing sperm DNA

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1876"></a>

صفحه 1876

damage with interventions such as antioxidant supplementation (98).

Differential Diagnosis of Male Factor In more than half of patients, an etiology for male factor can be discerned. Table 36-4 lists the differential diagnoses for male factor infertility (99). Several groups have attempted to assess the distribution of male infertility diagnoses (100,101). Factors that definitively lead to male infertility include secondary hypogonadism, genetic causes, seminal tract obstruction, oncologic disease therapies, and severe sexual dysfunction, while plausible causative factors include congenital urogenital anomalies and acquired testicular damage (unilateral cryptorchidism, testis cancer, and orchitis) (20). Although not considered as primary etiologies for male factor infertility reported to improve pregnancy rates, the benefit is less clear for clomiphene citrate, an estrogen antagonist at the hypothalamus and pituitary that promotes gonadotropin release (101).

1 Although not considered as primary etiologies for male factor infertility, varicocele, leukocytospermia, obesity, and uncontrolled chronic disease are more prevalent in infertile compared to fertile men (20).

Reprinted from de Kretser DM. Male infertility. Lancet. 1997;349(9054):787–790. Copyright © 1997 Elsevier. With permission.

Male Age The incidence of pregnancies conceived with the sperm source being of “advanced paternal age (APA)” is increasing. Interestingly, the definition of what constitutes APA has not been formally defined (102). There are reproductive risks associated with the sperm source being of APA, commonly defined as paternal age of 40 years or older at the time of conception, including increased risk of infertility and miscarriage (102). 3 Pregnancy rates are decreased with APA of ≥40 to 45 and particularly ≥age 50 (103). These associated risks are thought to be the result of worsening sperm quality (lower semen volume, lower sperm motility), including increased DNA fragmentation, as paternal age increases. In pregnancies conceived with ART, outcomes have been shown to be worse when there is APA, specifically when there is concomitant oligospermia (102).

Pregnancies conceived when there is APA are at an increased risk too, compared to pregnancies conceived when the partner is younger. These pregnancies are associated with an increased risk of genetic disorders (102). Some of these disorders are caused by de novo autosomal dominant mutations, recently coined paternal age effect disorders (or PAE), and include disorders such as achondroplasia (104). APA has been shown to increase the risk of some congenital anomalies, such as cleft lip and diaphragmatic hernia. There a reported association between APA and childhood cancer as well as poor neurocognitive outcomes in the offspring. The pathophysiology of this association remains uncertain (102). Psychiatric conditions such as schizophrenia have been associated with increased paternal age, but this may reflect trends of men with psychiatric conditions to delay fathering children until later ages rather than de novo genetic mutations (105).

Treatment of Male Factor Not Due to Azoospermia Medical treatment of reversible infectious or endocrine causes of male subfertility, such as sexually transmitted diseases and thyroid disorders, tends to be efficacious (101). Although injections of exogenous FSH have been reported to improve pregnancy rates, the benefit is less clear for clomiphene citrate (101). 3 Exogenous testosterone is not recommended in the treatment of male subfertility because of negative feedback inhibition at the pituitary that results in reduced intratesticular testosterone and decreased spermatogenesis (106). The prevalence of men receiving prescribed testosterone replacement therapy is 7% to 9% of whom one-third have normal or supraphysiologic testosterone levels prior to treatment and half do not receive appropriate follow-up (106,107). Antioxidant food supplements have been evaluated (101). 3 Glutathione, carnitine, and vitamin E do not appear to affect semen parameters, but administration of zinc and folic acid were associated with improved sperm concentration and morphology (108). The diagnosis and treatment of azoospermia (no sperm on semen analysis) will be discussed separately from other forms of male factor infertility.

Varicocele Repair A varicocele is an abnormal dilation of the veins within the spermatic cord (20). Clinically palpable varicoceles are present in 13.5% of men who recently fathered a pregnancy and 31% of

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1878"></a>

صفحه 1878

men with otherwise idiopathic oligozoospermia (20). The pathophysiologic effects of a varicocele appear to be mediated by an associated rise in testicular temperature, reflux of toxic metabolites from the left adrenal or renal veins, and/or higher reactive oxygen (109,110). 3 While treatment is associated with improved semen parameters in some studies, it is not clear whether varicocele repair improves fertility (109,111,112). Treatment via surgery or percutaneous embolization is typically considered if the varicocele is palpable and the semen analysis is abnormal and may allow some couples to use IUI instead of in vitro fertilization (IVF) (113) and improve IVF outcomes in the setting of oligozoo- or azoospermia (114). Surgery should NOT be pursued if the patient has nonobstructive azoospermia. Complications of treatment include infection, varicocele persistence and/or recurrence, and hydrocele formation (109). ART should be delayed 6 months after varicocele repair.

Artificial Insemination Artificial insemination has mainly been used to treat unexplained infertility (usually combined with superovulation) and male factor infertility (including same-sex female couples). 3 All artificial insemination procedures involve the placement of whole semen or processed sperm into the female reproductive tract, which permits sperm–ovum interaction in the absence of intercourse. The placement of whole semen into the vagina as a mode of fertility treatment is now rarely performed except in cases of severe coital dysfunction. All the common forms of artificial insemination involve processed sperm obtained from the male partner or a donor. Many techniques for artificial insemination have been described, but only intracervical (ICI) and intrauterine insemination (IUI) have been routinely employed.

Insemination Processing During and after intercourse, seminal fluid is usually prevented from reaching the intrauterine cavity and intra-abdominal space by the cervical barrier. 3 The introduction of seminal fluid past this barrier may be associated with pelvic infection, severe uterine cramping, or anaphylactoid reactions, possibly mediated by seminal factors such as prostaglandins. Thus, protocols for processing whole semen include the washing of specimens to remove seminal factors and to isolate pure sperm (115). Additional processing may include centrifugation through density gradients, sperm migration protocols, and differential adherence procedures (115). Finally, phosphodiesterase inhibitors, such as pentoxiphylline, have been used during semen processing to enhance sperm motility, fertilization capacity, and acrosome reactivity for IVF procedures (110,116).

Intrauterine Insemination IUI is the best studied and most widely practiced of all the insemination techniques. It involves placement of approximately 0.3 to 0.5 mL of washed, processed, and concentrated sperm into the intrauterine cavity by transcervical catheterization (117). 3 Historically, patients were advised to remain immobile and supine for 15 minutes after the procedure, but newer data suggests this may not improve outcomes (115,118). It is difficult to identify an optimal protocol or definitively determine pregnancy rates with IUI because of patient and protocol heterogeneity, with multifactorial influences on pregnancy rates including couple age, fertility diagnosis, stimulation type, and insemination timing relative to ovulation (116). Data on the optimal total motile sperm count (TMSC) to optimize pregnancy rates is mixed, however a recent retrospective study looking at more than 92,000 cycles indicated that pregnancy rates are optimized with a TMSC of ≥9 million. At less than 9 million, pregnancy rates decline, but

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1879"></a>

صفحه 1879

pregnancy was still achieved with a total motile count of less than 0.25 million TMSC (119). Pregnancy rates with semen samples containing the optimal amount of sperm (≥9 million TMSC) are reported to be 16.7% per cycle (119). Delivery rates (17% vs. 8%) are higher when ≥2 follicles ≥15 mm are present as compared to 1 follicle, particularly when GnRH antagonists are employed (120). Although teratozoospermia using older criteria has been associated with IUI failure (121), low strict normal morphology of 2% to 4% has not been found to be predictive of IUI failure as compared to morphology >4% in two retrospective studies (122,123), but effects of more severe teratozoospermia on IUI outcomes remain nebulous. No benefit has been found with respect to double IUI versus single IUI during a single cycle (124). The technique of fallopian tube sperm perfusion (FSP) has been proposed to improve IUI pregnancy rates, whereby the washed sperm is instilled slowly over several minutes through an inflated balloon catheter to prevent reflux, but has not been found in a large, randomized trial (125) or in a 2013 Cochrane systematic review (116) to be associated with improved pregnancy rates. Unexplained infertility and IUI will be discussed separately later in this chapter.

Timing of IUI IUI typically is performed 24 hours following the LH surge (126). IUI pregnancy rates doubled when human chorionic gonadotropin (hCG) trigger was used instead of natural LH surge (120). 3 Although timing of IUI is typically performed 36 hours following hCG administration to coincide with follicle rupture, no significant differences in pregnancy or live birth rates are seen whether IUI is performed at 24 or 36 hours following the hCG trigger (127). A recent small study showed that pregnancy rates were no different if the IUI was performed at the time of the trigger shot versus 36 hours after the trigger shot (if LH was low at the time of the trigger shot) (128).

Donor IUI For men with azoospermia or a significant genetic defect, and for women who are seeking pregnancy without a male partner, therapeutic donor insemination offers an effective option (129). In earlier prospective randomized or crossover trials, IUI was shown to be superior to ICI for donor insemination (130). The success rates with ICI are lower than those with IUI, particularly when using frozen sperm (117,130–132). Women receiving donor sperm because of same-sex partnership or being single had natural cycle IUI ongoing pregnancy rates that were similar to ICI at 10% per cycle and 40% cumulative rates over six treatment cycles (133). 3 In patients younger than 30 years of age who have no other infertility factors, delivery rates approach 90% after 12 cycles of IUI treatment with frozen sperm (134), so patients who do not conceive within 6 to 12 months should be assessed for female factors and encouraged to terminate treatment or proceed with alternative forms of therapy. The concomitant use of clomiphene citrate or human menopausal gonadotropin (hMG) for controlled ovarian hyperstimulation (COH) with IUI did not result in higher fecundity rates in these patients (134).

3 Psychological counseling should be offered because of the complexity of third-party reproduction (129). Higher pregnancy rates have been demonstrated when donor IUI is performed 1 day after natural cycle serum LH rise (20%) compared to those randomized to 2 days post-rise (11%) (135).

Donor Sperm Screening Although the use of fresh donor semen has been associated with higher pregnancy rates than the use of frozen specimens (136,137), both the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC)

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1880"></a>

صفحه 1880

and the ASRM recommend the use of frozen samples (129). This recommendation stems from the continued presence of human immunodeficiency virus (HIV) and other sexually transmitted infections in the general population and the lag time between HIV infection and seroconversion (129). Semen donors are screened for HIV infection, hepatitis B, hepatitis C, syphilis, gonorrhea, chlamydia, and cytomegalovirus infections, all of which may be transmitted through semen (129). All nonidentified (anonymous) sperm donor cryopreserved samples are quarantined for 6 months, and the donor is retested for infectious diseases before clinical use of the specimen (129). Donors are likewise questioned about any family history of genetically transmitted disorders, including X-linked, dominant, and recessive disorders. 1 Anonymous donors who have a known family history of a dominant or X-linked disorder are excluded as donor candidates (129).

Intracytoplasmic Sperm Injection ICSI has been utilized in the United States for the vast majority of ART cycles for male factor infertility to achieve pregnancy outcomes that are comparable with those of couples with nonmale factor infertility using conventional IVF treatment (138). ICSI for all indications, beyond male factor, has increased from 36.4% in 1996 to 76.2% in 2012 in the United States (139). 3 The technique involves direct injection of a live sperm into the oocyte, thereby theoretically bypassing limitations imposed by sperm motility and defects in capacitation, the acrosome reaction, and/or sperm binding to the zona pellucida (138). This microsurgical procedure involves stripping the aspirated cumulus complex of all surrounding granulosa cells and inserting a single viable sperm into the cytoplasm (ooplasm) of the oocyte that optimally is metaphase II (138), though less mature oocytes may still be considered for injection (140). ICSI should be offered if there is male factor infertility, cryopreserved oocytes are being inseminated or if surgically recovered sperm is used (86), though there is wide latitude for utility depending on use of semen normal value thresholds (141). 3 Higher pregnancy rates have been noted with fresh when compared to frozen specimens and with ejaculated when compared to surgically retrieved sperm (86). Success rates are affected by the age of the female partner and oocyte quality (86). Nonmale factor indications for ICSI are contentious (139) and include teratozoospermia (142), unexplained infertility, low oocyte yield, and advanced maternal age (139). The following indications do not have robust data to support improved outcomes with ICSI over conventional fertilization: unexplained infertility, low oocyte yield, advanced maternal age, and routine use. It may be of benefit when using previously cryopreserved oocytes (139).

Risks of ICSI Oocyte degeneration following ICSI is a relatively common occurrence with rates ranging from 5% to 19% (143). Prior reports have not supported the idea that oocyte degeneration is a function of technician skill; it is suggested that inherent oocyte quality, ovarian stimulation itself, unexpected random technical problems, or mechanical removal of cumulus and corona cells may contribute to this phenomenon (143). While accumulated data indicate that there is an overall increased risk of congenital abnormalities with ART, ICSI does not appear to be associated with a higher risk compared to conventional IVF (144). 11 The prevalence of sex chromosome abnormalities and translocation appears to be slightly higher with ICSI versus IVF; however, it is unclear whether these relate to the ICSI procedure or inherent gamete defects, as men with abnormal semen parameters have higher rates of sperm

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1881"></a>

صفحه 1881

aneuploidy (145). There may be an increased risk of imprinting disorders with ICSI (146). Although prior studies have not found an association with impaired intellectual or motor development among ICSI children, one study reported a higher incidence of autism when ICSI was used compared with IVF (147). In the setting of specific genetic abnormalities such as Y chromosome microdeletions, abnormal karyotypes, cystic fibrosis mutations or congenital absence of the vas deferens, genetic counseling should be offered to address the possible risk of infertility or other abnormalities in the offspring (145).

Azoospermia: Classification and Treatment

3 Azoospermia describes the absence of spermatozoa in the ejaculate and is found in 1% of all men, and up to 10 to 15% of infertile men (86,148). Causes are categorized into pretesticular (nonobstructive), testicular (nonobstructive), and posttesticular (includes obstructive and nonobstructive) etiologies, but in some cases the condition is idiopathic (86). In all cases of azoospermia, genetic evaluation is indicated; however, the nature of the genetic evaluation differs. In nonobstructive azoospermia, the patient should be evaluated with a karyotype (the most common abnormality being Klinefelter syndrome) and Y microdeletion analyses. In obstructive azoospermia, the patient should be evaluated with genetic testing for cystic fibrosis (149).

Pretesticular Azoospermia Pretesticular azoospermia from organic causes is relatively rare and results from gonadotropin deficiency, which leads to loss of spermatogenesis (86,148). The physician should perform a full endocrine history that includes information on puberty and growth and check for low serum levels of LH, FSH, and testosterone (86,148). Prolactin levels and pituitary imaging are indicated in cases of hypogonadotropic hypogonadism (86,148). 3 Hormonal treatment includes pulsatile GnRH, hCG, and exogenous gonadotropin (86,148,150). The best predictors of good response are postpubertal onset of gonadotropin deficiency and testicular volume >8 mL (86). Iatrogenic azoospermia commonly results from medical testosterone use. Treatment includes discontinuation of exogenous testosterone and recovery therapy with medications such as clomiphene citrate, hCG, and/or FSH with frequent follow-up semen analyses and endocrine labs (106). Azoospermia recovers to a median concentration of 6.5 million/mL 4.5 months following cessation of testosterone use; full recovery of sperm quantity was more likely in those men who used testosterone for less than 1 year, while those use who used it for several years were unlikely to recover (106).

Testicular Azoospermia Gonadal failure is the hallmark of testicular azoospermia. Causes of this condition may be genetic, acquired (e.g., radiation therapy, chemotherapy, testicular torsion, varicocele, or mumps orchitis), or developmental (e.g., testicular maldescent) (86,148). Testicular atrophy is often present (86,148,150). Because of the low chance of obtaining sperm, testicular biopsy is generally not recommended with hypergonadotropic hypogonadism (elevated levels of LH and FSH with low serum levels of testosterone) and consideration should be given to using donor sperm (86,148,150). Diagnostic testicular biopsies may be indicated in the setting of normal hormonal testing (86,148). If sperm are present in diagnostic testing, consideration can be given to using surgically retrieved spermatozoa for ICSI (86,148). If no sperm are present, consideration can be given to correcting acquired conditions such as varicocele, which may restore sperm to the ejaculate to permit ICSI or spontaneous pregnancy (151,152). 3

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1882"></a>

صفحه 1882

Chromosomal abnormalities by peripheral karyotype testing are present in about 7% of infertile, 5% of oligospermic, and 10% to 15% of azoospermic men (153). Sex chromosome aneuploidies such as Klinefelter syndrome (47, XXY) encompass two-thirds of these infertility- associated chromosome abnormalities (153). Microdeletions in the Y chromosome have been identified in 10% to 20% of men with idiopathic azoospermia or severe oligospermia with concentration <5 million/mL (Table 36-5) (153). These microdeletions can be transmitted to the male offspring, who may suffer from infertility (153). Therefore, screening for genetic causes is indicated in nonacquired cases of testicular azoospermia so that genetic counseling can be provided before treatment (145). The two most implicated candidate gene families are the RNA- binding motif (RBM) and the “deleted in azoospermia” (DAZ) families, but microdeletions at various loci on the Y chromosome have been described (154). For example, microdeletions in Yq11.23 can occur in one or more of three regions: AZFa (proximal), AZFb (central), and AZFc (distal) (153).
