Posttesticular Azoospermia Posttesticular or obstructive etiologies are associated with normal gonadotropin and testosterone levels and are present in up to 40% of azoospermic men (86,148). Ejaculatory dysfunction is associated with oligospermia or aspermia but rarely azoospermia (148). 3 Obstructive causes include congenital absence or obstruction of the vas deferens or ejaculatory ducts, acquired obstruction of these ducts, or ductal dysfunction, including retrograde ejaculation (86,148). In the congenital bilateral absence of the vas deferens (CBAVD) or hypogonadism, men with low-volume ejaculate should have a postejaculatory urinalysis to check for retrograde ejaculation, which is associated with diabetics and surgery to the bladder or prostate (86,148). Sperm may be isolated from the neutralized urine of men with retrograde ejaculation and processed for insemination or for ART (74). Transrectal ultrasound (US) may be of use to diagnose ejaculatory duct obstruction or unilateral vasal agenesis (to demonstrate contralateral atresia) but is generally not needed for CBAVD (148). 3 Renal imaging is necessary when either unilateral or bilateral vasal absence is diagnosed since there is a 10% to 25% incidence of associated renal agenesis (148). Most men with CBAVD will also have seminal

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1883"></a>

صفحه 1883

vesicle agenesis, so they will almost all have low semen volume, pH, and low seminal fructose levels (148). Spermatogenesis can be expected to be normal in CBAVD, so diagnostic biopsy is generally not indicated (155). In some cases, testicular biopsy may be indicated to differentiate between testicular and posttesticular causes (148). At least two-thirds of men with CBAVD have mutations of the cystic fibrosis transmembrane conductance regulator (CFTR) gene (148). However, because many CFTR mutations are undetectable, CBAVD patients should be assumed to have a mutation and thus testing of the female partner’s carrier status should be performed (148).

Vasectomy Reversal and Treatment of Obstructive Azoospermia Vasectomy can be reversed effectively using microsurgical vasovasostomy or vasoepididymostomy. The latter technique can be used for epididymal obstructions (156). Patency and subsequent pregnancy rates after vasectomy reversal approach are 100% and 80%, respectively (157). Pregnancy typically occurs within 24 months of reversal (157). 3 Rates of patency and pregnancy vary inversely with the length of time from vasectomy, particularly for reversal procedures performed after ≥15 years (156,157). Although 60% of reversal patients develop antisperm antibodies, these do not appear to affect fecundability (157). Following surgery, periodic semen analyses can identify reobstruction, which can range from 3% to 21% depending on which segments were anastomosed (157). For those patients with azoospermia 6 months after reversal, the procedure is considered a failure and testicular sperm aspiration with ICSI could be considered (157). However, repeat vasovasostomy is associated with patency rates of 75% and pregnancy rates of 43% (157).

Surgical Sperm Recovery for Intracytoplasmic Sperm Injection Among the many surgical methods for sperm recovery, the most widely described are microsurgical epididymal sperm aspiration (MESA), percutaneous epididymal sperm aspiration (PESA), testicular sperm extraction (TESE), and percutaneous testicular sperm fine-needle aspiration (TESA, also called fine-needle aspiration, or FNA) (155). Both MESA and TESE are open surgical procedures performed with an operating microscope and general or regional anesthesia, whereas the percutaneous procedures need only local anesthesia (155). The optimal method for surgical sperm recovery has not been determined and certainly varies based on patient history (155). 3 Hematoma risk is low regardless of method (155). Testicular atrophy is a rare complication of TESE and TESA even when biopsies are obtained from multiple testicular sites (155).

With obstructive azoospermia, pregnancy rates using sperm retrieval and ICSI are 24% to 64% and outcomes using either frozen thawed or fresh sperm are comparable (155). Because MESA allows for diagnosis and possible reconstruction of ductal pathology and usually yields very large numbers of sperm, sperm cryopreservation and avoidance of repeat surgery may be possible (155). If repeat sperm retrievals are needed, the minimum interval between procedures is 3 to 6 months to allow for adequate healing (155). Surgical sperm retrieval in the setting of nonobstructive azoospermia is addressed in the “Testicular Azoospermia” section.

Female Age and Decreased Ovarian Reserve

Decreased Fecundability and Diminished Ovarian Reserve Most women will experience an age-related decline in fecundability that is physiologic rather

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1884"></a>

صفحه 1884

than pathologic. 4 This decline begins in the early 30s and accelerates during the late 30s and early 40s, reflecting declines in oocyte quantity and quality (158). Age-related declines in fecundability are supported by research findings among women using a wide range of methods to conceive, including natural conception, donor sperm IUI for male factor infertility, use of donor egg in IVF, and age-related declines in IVF success rates when using a woman’s autologous eggs. Among populations that do not practice contraception, conception probability peaks at age 20, declines somewhat at age 32, steeply declines after the age of 37, and is rare after age 45 (158). Female partners of azoospermic husbands who undergo donor sperm IUI treatment had cumulative pregnancy rates over 12 cycles of 74% (age <31 years), 62% (ages 31 to 35), and 54% (age >35) (159). Chronologic aging of the endometrium does not seem to play an appreciable role in reduced fertility, given the excellent live birth rates using donor oocytes regardless of recipient age (reported LBRs have ranged from 37% to 50%) (160). One way that chances of conception using autologous oocytes decreases with age is related to the decline in the quantity of primordial follicles, established early in fetal life, to near zero at menopause (161). Ovarian reserve describes the functional capability of the ovary and the quantity of oocytes in the primordial follicular pool. 4 “Diminished ovarian reserve” (DOR) generally describes a woman of reproductive age with regular menses but reduced response to ovarian stimulation compared to age-matched women. The assessments used to define DOR can include assessment of anti-mullerian hormone (AMH), antral follicle count (AFC), or follicular phase FSH and estradiol. However (162), DOR has been heterogeneously defined based on age, biochemical parameters, sonography, poor follicular response to ovarian stimulation, and number of oocytes retrieved during IVF (163). The prevalence of DOR diagnoses in a US IVF cohort increased to 26% in 2011, but the diagnosis has not consistently predicted IVF or pregnancy outcomes (164). In fact, while markers may portend poor response to ovarian stimulation, they do not predict live birth rates (165). Etiologies of prematurely DOR can include iatrogenic causes (i.e., cytotoxic agents, radiation, and surgical removal of reproductive tissues), pelvic endometriosis or adhesions, or immunologic and genetic factors. 4 Although premature ovarian failure/menopause prior to age 40 has been associated with an increased number of premutation-level trinucleotide repeat lengths in the FMR1 gene, a similar increase has not been consistently confirmed in premenopausal DOR women (166,167).

Spontaneous Pregnancy Loss

4 Reproductive aging is associated with abnormalities in the oocyte meiotic spindles that lead to chromosome alignment errors and increase rates of conceptus aneuploidies, particularly trisomies (158). This serves to increase the risk for spontaneous pregnancy loss and thereby decrease live birth rates in older women (158,168). A large study based on the Danish national registry estimated the rates of clinically recognized spontaneous pregnancy loss for various age groups to be 13.3% (12 to 19 years), 11.1% (20 to 24 years), 11.9% (25 to 29 years), 15.0% (30 to 34 years), 24.6% (35 to 39 years), 51.0% (40 to 44 years), and 93.4% (>45 years) (169,170). Using sensitive hCG assays in women during their reproductive years, 22% of all pregnancies were found to be lost before they could be clinically diagnosed (170).

Ovarian Reserve

4 Ovarian reserve refers to the size of the nongrowing, or resting, primordial follicle population in the ovaries. This ovarian reserve, in turn, presumably determines the

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1885"></a>

صفحه 1885

number of growing follicles and the “quality” or reproductive potential of their oocytes (168). Although ovarian reserve and fecundability decrease with aging, they may not be consistently reflected by a woman’s chronologic age because ovarian reserve does not decline at the same pace at a given age for all women. The prognostic value of AMH levels and AFC among IVF patients, independent of age, has established them as standard ovarian reserve tests (171–174).

Serum Anti-Mullerian Hormone AMH is produced by the granulosa cells of preantral and small antral follicles (175,176). The serum level of AMH in women with normal cycles declines with age (175) and becomes undetectable after menopause (177). 4 Unlike other serum markers, AMH can be measured at any time in the menstrual cycle (177) and levels are best interpreted using age-specific reference ranges (178). Evolving assay platforms from Diagnostic Systems Laboratories to Beckman Coulter AMH Generation II (178), modifications to the generation II assay protocol (178) and further use of automated platforms (179) limits interpretation of serial AMH results. In women engaging in procreative intercourse without a history of infertility, AMH level does not correlate with monthly fecundability regardless of age at least in the short term (6 to 12 months) (165). 4 AMH was found to be a good predictor of both excessive (>3.5 ng/mL) and poor (<1 ng/mL) IVF stimulation response and is strongly correlated to the AFC (176,177,180).

Antral Follicle Count Using transvaginal US in the early follicular phase, all 2- to 10-mm ovarian follicles are counted and the total for both ovaries is called the basal AFC (161). The AFC correlates very well with chronologic age in normal fertile women and appears to reflect what remains of the primordial follicular pool (161). Decreases in AFC with age are gradual rather than sudden (181). 5 A total AFC <4 is predictive of poor response and higher cancellation rates with IVF (182,183).

With the use of machine learning, IVF success prediction models using AMH and/or AFC can be used together with age and other clinical factors such as BMI, reproductive history, and clinical infertility diagnoses, to provide highly accurate IVF live birth probabilities. 5 For example, the use of AMH and/or AFC reclassified more than 60% of patients to have higher IVF live birth probabilities and more than 10% of patients found to have lower probabilities compared to the age-control model (174). Some providers use other ovarian reserve tests in addition to AMH and AFC.

Serum Day 3 FSH

4 As women age, FSH physiologically rises in the early follicular phase (cycle day 3), with levels of 5.74 IU/L at ages 35 to 39 and 14.34 IU/L at ages 45 to 59 (184). In a study of 181,536 IVF cycles in the United States, a basal FSH ≥12 constituted the 90th centile of the dataset and was selected as the threshold for elevated level (164). Higher levels are seen after unilateral oophorectomy (184). In women in their 40s, levels >20 IU/L are predictive of menopause (184). Because the incidence of abnormal values is lower in younger women, testing is typically performed for women aged 35 or older (184). In subfertile women with an FSH ≥8 IU/L, spontaneous pregnancy rates decrease by 7% per unit of FSH increase, with a 40% reduction at 15 IU/L and 58% at 20 IU/L (185). FSH levels vary widely by assay, laboratory, and population (186). Basal FSH ≥12 mIU/mL has a PPV of 30.9% when used alone and 48% when combined with female age ≥40 years for predicting cycle cancellation for poor response or fewer

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1886"></a>

صفحه 1886

than four oocytes obtained at retrieval, such that IVF therapy should not necessarily be withheld from patients diagnosed with DOR (164). Compared to women with normal levels, elevated basal serum FSH (>10 mIU/mL) did not affect monthly fecundability in women aged 30 to 44 without a history of infertility attempting to conceive spontaneously, regardless of age (165).

Basal Estradiol Level Basal day 3 FSH is often combined with estradiol (E2) testing. E2 levels on day 3 reflect follicular growth rather than the number of antral follicles (175). Elevations in FSH and decreases in inhibin B (see below) that accompany aging result in advanced follicular growth at the end of the preceding luteal phase. In response, early follicular E2 levels are typically higher in older women and in women with advanced reproductive aging (161).

Clomiphene Citrate Challenge Test Clomiphene citrate is thought to have antiestrogenic effects on the hypothalamic–pituitary axis, resulting in a decrease in E2-mediated suppression of FSH production by the pituitary (187). The clomiphene citrate challenge test (CCCT) involves the measurement of serum FSH and E2 on day 3 of the menstrual cycle, and again on day 10 after administration of clomiphene citrate (100 mg orally each day) from days 5 to 9 (187). 5 The CCCT has been reported to be more sensitive than basal FSH alone in identifying poor response to exogenous gonadotropins, but others report that the predictive values of the tests do not differ substantially (186,187).

Serum Inhibin B Serum inhibin B is secreted by ovarian granulosa cells starting at the preantral follicle stage and therefore reflects the size of the growing follicular cohort (176). Reduced inhibin B levels are seen with aging even in normal fertile women (175). Inhibin B alone has poor predictive value for ovarian response (176,186) but improves the predictive value when added to the CCCT (187). Unlike basal testing, levels of inhibin B measured on the 5th day of ovarian stimulation were predictive of live birth following IVF/ICSI (188).

Options for Diminished Ovarian Reserve

5 Treatments of DOR include autologous IVF, use of donor oocytes or embryos, and adoption. Multiple stimulation protocols have been proposed for autologous IVF in poor responders including judicious use of gonadotropins with LH activity, estrogen pretreatment, use of GnRH antagonists during and prior to ovarian stimulation, microdose flare GnRH agonists, and selective estrogen receptor modulators such as clomiphene and letrozole along with gonadotropin injections (189,190). All have varying degrees of success, but counseling for DOR patients in whom donor gametes are not of interest must include disclosure that per-cycle pregnancy rates are low, particularly in older women (191), such that multiple IVF cycles may be necessary and the prognosis may be poor or futile. However, isolated case reports of success do provide some hope in this regard (192,193), so it may be appropriate to offer autologous therapy to motivated, well-counseled patients.

Adjuncts During Autologous IVF

5 Adjunctive autologous IVF therapies include androgens such as DHEA and testosterone, growth hormone, Coenzyme Q-10 (CoQ10), vitamin D, and omega-3 fatty acids. Pretreatment of women with DOR for 2 to 5 months with dehydroepiandrosterone (DHEA; 25

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1887"></a>

صفحه 1887

mg three times daily) has been described to improve oocyte yield and pregnancy rates with IVF in case series (194–196). Putative mechanisms for this action include increasing serum concentrations of insulin-like growth factor 1 (IGF-1), which may potentiate gonadotropic effects on the follicle, and providing the ovary with additional prohormone for additional ovarian steroidogenesis (196). Pretreatment with DHEA led to higher ovarian volumes and AFCs in women with premature ovarian insufficiency (197), however some prospective cohort and randomized trials have not found statistically significant improvements for stimulation parameters or pregnancy rates in poor (198,199) or normal responders (200). A similar rationale has prompted the use of transdermal testosterone 10 to 12.5 mg for 14 to 28 days pretreatment or during stimulation with varied results in terms of number of oocytes retrieved and pregnancy rate (201,202). A Cochrane review of 17 randomized controlled trials found moderate quality evidence of live birth benefit for DHEA and testosterone in poor responders (203).

Growth hormone, by enhancing the granulosa cell response to gonadotropins through increases in local IGF-1 production, has been found to improve pregnancy and live birth rates for poor responders in some meta-analyses including the Cochrane database (204–206). Suggested dosing for growth hormone is 2.5 mg subcutaneously from day 6 of stimulation to day of trigger (204). The cost of growth hormone may be prohibitive for some patients; it is generally not covered by insurance for off-label use, and such use may be restricted by the 1990 Anabolic Steroids Control Act (207).

Nutritional supplementation may provide important adjunctive benefits. CoQ10 is found in higher concentrations in follicular fluid of oocytes that produce good-quality embryos by morphokinetic assessment and pregnancies (208). Oocytes and early embryos obtain energy solely from CoQ10-mediated mitochondrial oxidative phosphorylation which allows proper oocyte maturation and fertilization, but aging diminishes CoQ10 production mitochondrial oxidative phosphorylation (209). These changes can be reversed by CoQ10 supplementation in mice and humans (210). Women randomized to 600 mg of daily CoQ10 for 2 months prior and during their IVF cycle had a nonstatistically significant increase in good-quality embryos and embryo euploidy compared to the placebo group (209). Omega-3 fatty acids such as those found in fish oil extends reproductive lifespan in murine models, and this may be through effects on the pituitary. Normal-weight reproductively healthy women taking 4 g of eicosapentaenoic acid and docosahexaenoic acid daily for 4 weeks had reduced baseline and GnRH-stimulated serum FSH levels (211). Vitamin D deficiency has been inconsistently associated with AMH levels and AFC, so it is difficult to derive clinical recommendations for vitamin D supplementation solely for the indication of DOR (212). Supplementation for vitamin D deficient patients has produced inconsistent outcomes with respect to pregnancy rates in the context of IVF (213,214).

Ovulatory Factor

6 Ovulatory dysfunction accounts for 30% to 40% of all cases of female infertility (215) and may reflect as absent or infrequent ovulation. 1 Menstrual history may be suggestive of ovulatory dysfunction if oligomenorrhea, amenorrhea, or polymenorrhea are revealed (215). Menstrual abnormalities are present in 18% to 20% of the general population (216). Women with regular monthly menses may experience anovulation (216), although this occurs infrequently at 1% to 14% (217). Figure 36-4 shows the fluctuations of E2, progesterone, FSH, and LH in a typical, 28-day ovulatory cycle. The normal length of the menstrual cycle in reproductive-age women varies from 21 to 35 days, with a mean of 27 to 29 days (218).

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1888"></a>

صفحه 1888

Most of the variability in cycle length occurs during the follicular phase (219) but the luteal phase, often considered to be fixed at 14 days, can range from 7 to 19 days (220). 6 In women with absent or infrequent ovulation, serum FSH, prolactin, and thyroid-stimulating hormone (TSH) testing should be obtained (215).

Figure 36-4 Relative hormonal fluctuations in a normal, ovulatory, 28-day menstrual cycle.

Methods to Document Ovulation

The “Fertile Window”

1 The fertile window is the 6-day interval ending on the day of ovulation, and does not extend past ovulation (54,219). Sperm can survive for up to 6 days in well-estrogenized cervical mucus, but the egg may be fertilizable for less than a day (219). Daily intercourse during this window may increase the probability of conception (54,219). 1 The average woman is fertile between days 10 and 17 of the menstrual cycle, but many women can conceive outside of this range (220). Therefore, if timed intercourse (TI) is too cumbersome, intercourse two to three times weekly throughout the menstrual cycle will likely result in at least some of those occasions falling within the fertile window (221). Duration of abstinence prior to the fertile window has not been established, although one author suggests 5 days (219).

Basal Body Temperature This inexpensive yet relatively unreliable method involves daily recording of oral temperature (222). The secretion of progesterone following ovulation causes a temperature increase of about 0.5 °F to 1 °F over baseline (222). Ovulation is assumed after 3 consecutive days of raised temperatures (222). Charting of daily basal body temperatures (BBTs) produces a characteristic biphasic pattern in women with ovulatory cycles (222). 1 Limitations to BBT include its inability to prospectively predict ovulation and its frequent false-negative results (223). For these reasons as well as the associated inconvenience, BBT charting is not generally recommended (215).

Cervical Mucus

تصاویر استخراج‌شدهٔ همین صفحه

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1889"></a>

صفحه 1889

During the fertile window, cervical mucus is typically clear, while at other times of the menstrual cycle it is dry and sticky (54,222). 1 The volume of cervical mucus peaks 2 to 3 days prior to ovulation, thus identifying higher day-specific probabilities of conception (54).

Urinary Luteinizing Hormone Monitoring At a mean time of 2 hours following the peak of the serum LH surge, urinary LH can be detected (224). Commercially available kits for documenting the LH surge are generally accurate, quick, convenient, and relatively inexpensive enzyme-linked immunosorbent assays (ELISA) that use 35 to 50 mIU/mL as their threshold for detection (224,225). When the LH surge is detected, ovulation may occur within the next 48 hours (54,224,225). The positive predictive and negative predictive values for these kits have been described to be 92% (224) and 95% (226), respectively. Because the duration of the surge may be <12 hours, twice-daily testing may increase detection rates (225). However, the 2 days of highest probability of conception are the day of and the day prior to the LH surge, so this may lead to abstaining from intercourse during a potentially fertile time (219). False-positive rates occur in 7% of cycles (54,223,227), and may be more common in conditions with tonic elevation in basal LH levels such as PCOS. 2 The tests cannot be used in patients with irregular cycles (219).

Midluteal Serum Progesterone When used to document ovulation, serum progesterone measurement should coincide with peak progesterone secretion in the midluteal phase, timed 7 days prior to expected onset of the next menstrual period or 7 days following the LH surge (215). The lower limit of progesterone levels in the luteal phase varies among laboratories, but a level above 3 ng/mL (10 nmol/L) typically confirms ovulation (215). Interpretation of isolated luteal phase measurements of serum progesterone is complicated by the frequent pulses that characterize the secretion of this hormone (215).

Ultrasound Monitoring Ovulation is characterized by a decrease in the size of a monitored ovarian follicle and by the appearance of fluid around the ovary or in the cul-de-sac using transvaginal US (215). 1 Follicles reach a preovulatory diameter of 17 to 19 mm in spontaneous cycles or 19 to 25 mm for ovulation induction cycles using clomiphene or letrozole (228,229). A combination of urinary LH testing and US can be used, with LH kit use starting when the US-measured follicle size reaches 14 mm (223). Ten percent of the cycles of normally fertile women may have a luteinized unruptured follicle, whereby progesterone is released and the luteal phase progresses normally without visible signs of follicle rupture on ultrasound monitoring (230). This incidence is increased to 25% in women with unexplained infertility (230). Ultrasound for the detection of ovulation has a sensitivity of 84% and a specificity of 89% for the confirmation of ovulation (231).

Polycystic Ovarian Syndrome

6 The most common cause of oligo-ovulation and anovulation—in the general population and among women presenting with infertility—is PCOS (232). The diagnosis of PCOS is determined by exclusion of other medical conditions including pregnancy, hypothalamic– pituitary disorders, or other causes of hyperandrogenism such as androgen-secreting tumors or nonclassical congenital adrenal hyperplasia and the presence of two of the following conditions

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1890"></a>

صفحه 1890

(233):

Oligo-ovulation or anovulation (manifested as oligomenorrhea or amenorrhea) Hyperandrogenemia (elevated levels of circulating androgens) or hyperandrogenism (clinical manifestations of androgen excess) Polycystic ovarian features detected by US

Documentation of elevated serum LH: FSH ratios or hyperinsulinemia are not required for either diagnosis or treatment of PCOS (232,233). Patients with PCOS should be counseled and screened regarding potential metabolic disease and obstetric complications prior to fertility treatment (232).

Ovulation Induction in Women with Polycystic Ovarian Syndrome Despite the use of similar medications, the indications and goals of ovulation induction should be distinguished from those of superovulation. The goal of ovulation induction refers to the therapeutic restoration of the release of one egg per cycle in a woman with ovulatory dysfunction (228). In contrast, the explicit goal of superovulation for women with unexplained infertility is to cause more than one egg to be ovulated, thereby increasing the probability of conception (228). Overall, 60% to 85% of women with PCOS will ovulate when treated with clomid or letrozole. 6 Of the women who ovulate, 15% to 20% will be pregnant per cycle and 50% will be pregnant by 6 months of treatment (234–236).

Weight Loss Obesity in PCOS patients is associated with poor infertility treatment outcomes (232,237–239), although the impact on pregnancy loss rates is less clear (232,237,240). 6 Given that even a 5% weight loss may improve pregnancy rates, weight loss should be encouraged in all overweight and obese infertility patients (232). In general, lifestyle modification is the first-line therapy, followed by pharmacologic treatment and weight-loss surgery (232). Lifestyle recommendations include a decrease in daily caloric consumption by 500 kcal and regular physical exercise, although the optimal regimen for the latter is unknown (232,241). Weight-loss interventions should be undertaken prior to attempting conception in patients with excess body weight (232).

Ovulation Induction Agent Pharmacology (Clomiphene Citrate [Clomid, Serophene], Letrozole [Femara], Anastrozole [Arimidex]) Clomiphene citrate is a weak synthetic estrogen that mimics the activity of an estrogen antagonist when given at typical pharmacologic doses for the induction of ovulation (228). It is cleared by the liver and excreted into the stool, with 85% clearance in 6 days (228). 6 A functional hypothalamic–pituitary–ovarian axis is usually required for appropriate clomiphene citrate action (228). More specifically, clomiphene citrate is thought to bind and block estrogen receptors in the hypothalamus for prolonged periods, thereby decreasing the normal ovarian–hypothalamic estrogen feedback loop (228). This blockade increases GnRH pulsatility, leading to increased pituitary secretion of gonadotropins which promote ovarian follicular development (228). Letrozole is a potent, reversible, nonsteroidal aromatase inhibitor that has been used off-label for the treatment of patients with anovulatory infertility since 2001 (242). Letrozole is administered orally and cleared by the liver with a shorter half-life (48 hours) than clomiphene citrate (2 weeks). By reducing ovarian E2 secretion, letrozole disrupts E2’s

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1891"></a>

صفحه 1891

negative feedback effect on pituitary gonadotropin release. The consequent rise in FSH promotes ovarian follicular development.

Ovulation Induction Outcomes Ovulation induction is first-line treatment for anovulatory infertility (243). Over the course of 6 months, clomiphene is associated with 49% ovulation, 23.9% pregnancy, and 22.5% live birth rates in women with anovulatory infertility (237). 5 Clomiphene effectiveness is decreased by obesity, increased age, and hyperandrogenic states (237,244,245). Side effects of clomiphene citrate include vasomotor flushes, mood swings, breast tenderness, pelvic discomfort, and nausea (228). In a minority of individuals, the antiestrogenic effects of clomiphene citrate at the level of the endometrium or the cervix may have adverse effects on fertility (228). In the presence of visual abnormalities, clomiphene citrate should be discontinued promptly (228). Multiple gestation rates with clomiphene citrate are approximately 8%, most of which are twins (228). Though historically clomiphene citrate has been the drug of choice for the treatment of women with PCOS-associated anovulation, current evidence suggests that letrozole results in higher ovulation and live birth rates compared with clomiphene, particularly for obese (BMI >30) women. In a multicenter trial of 750 women with PCOS- associated infertility randomizing to letrozole (2.5 mg) or clomiphene citrate (50 mg) from cycle day 3 to 7 for up to five cycles, the cumulative live birth rate was significantly higher in the letrozole arm (27.5% vs. 19.1%; RR 1.44, 95% CI 1.10–1.87) (246). Twin pregnancy rates were similar in the letrozole (3.4%) and clomiphene (7.4%) groups (246). A subsequent meta-analysis of six trials comparing clomiphene and letrozole for ovulation induction in anovulatory women with PCOS observed higher live birth rates with letrozole (OR 1.79; 95% CI 1.38–2.31) (247). Side effects with letrozole include dizziness and fatigue. 10 Although concerns have been raised regarding possible associations between fetal congenital anomalies and the use of aromatase inhibitors, no such increase was observed when letrozole was compared to clomiphene (248).

Ovulation Induction Dosing Letrozole is supplied in 2.5-mg tablets, and the typical starting dose is 2.5 mg per day. Clomiphene citrate drug is supplied in 50-mg tablets; the usual starting dosage is 50 mg/day, but patients who are very sensitive may respond to 12.5 to 25 mg/day (228). Either agent is typically begun within the first 5 days after the onset of a spontaneous or progesterone-induced menses and is continued for 5 days (i.e., treatment on days 3 to 7 or 5 to 9 of the menstrual cycle) (228). Prior to initiating letrozole, patients should be counseled that ovulation induction represents an off-label use of the drug. Given the pregnancy category X designation of both clomiphene and letrozole, care should be taken to ensure that the patient is not pregnant prior to administration of these agents. If ovulation does not occur at the initial dosage of 2.5-mg letrozole or 50-mg clomiphene citrate, the dosage is increased in each subsequent cycle by 2.5 mg or 50 mg per day, respectively (228). Seventy-four percent of women will ovulate with 100 mg/day (228), the maximum dose approved by the US Food and Drug Administration (233). However, some patients need higher doses, which have been safely given up to 250 mg/day (228). A step-up method increases the dose within a single cycle without intervening menses if no follicular response is documented by US 4 to 5 days after the last dose (249). 5 Ovulation induction treatment should be limited to 6 ovulatory cycles or 12 total cycles (232,250,251).

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1892"></a>

صفحه 1892

Monitoring Ovulation Induction Therapy If preovulation monitoring is not performed, patients should be instructed to have intercourse every 2 to 3 days following the last day of therapy and check serum progesterone weekly × 5 weeks before inducing a withdrawal bleed or increasing the dose of the ovulation induction agent (237). Although no clear advantage has been demonstrated for any ovulation monitoring technique, regular contact should be maintained with patients to review response to therapy (228). The urinary LH surge may be detected 5 to 12 days after treatment is completed, and can be confirmed by midluteal serum progesterone testing 7 days later (228). With US monitoring, treatment should be withheld if large cysts are seen on baseline testing (228). Following ovulation induction use, follicles typically reach a preovulatory diameter of 19 to 25 mm by US, but may be as large as 30 mm (228,229). 5 A combination of LH testing and US can be used, with LH kits starting when the largest US-measured follicle reaches 14 mm in diameter (223).

Human Chorionic Gonadotropin If a dominant follicle develops, but there is no spontaneous LH surge, hCG can be used to induce final follicular maturation (228), with ovulation occurring approximately 40 hours following administration (252). Although administration of hCG at mid-cycle does not appear to improve conception chances in most infertility patients using clomiphene citrate (126,253,254), it may be useful for patients with known ovulatory dysfunction (235) or for IUI. 5 The medication may be derived from urine (5,000 to 10,000 international units intramuscularly) or manufactured with recombinant technology (250 μg subcutaneously, equivalent to 5,000 to 6,000 IU urinary) (252).

Insulin Sensitizers Insulin resistance contributes to the pathogenesis of PCOS. Metformin is an oral biguanide that is approved for the treatment of noninsulin-dependent diabetes and has been used in PCOS to increase the frequency of spontaneous ovulation (255). Metformin acts by several mechanisms, including inhibition of gluconeogenesis in the liver and increasing the uptake of glucose in the periphery (255). Although the literature is conflicting, larger studies have suggested that the live birth rate with metformin alone (7.2%) is lower than that achieved with clomiphene, and, overall, the combination does not confer additional benefit over clomiphene alone (237,256). Subgroups of patients including those who are obese may benefit from combination therapy (244,257). Pre-pregnancy use of metformin does not appear to reduce miscarriage rate, and there is insufficient evidence to recommend metformin use during pregnancy to reduce the risk of miscarriage (258). Risks of metformin include gastrointestinal upset and rare lactic acidosis, so it should be avoided in settings of hepatic/renal dysfunction and prior to surgery or use of contrast radiologic dye (255). Reported effective doses include 500 mg three times daily (255), 850 mg twice daily (255), and 1,000 mg twice daily (237). The medication is best tolerated when started at the lowest dose and increased gradually. Extended- release formulations are associated with less gastrointestinal upset (237). Because regular ovulation may not occur with metformin alone, patients may need medications to induce withdrawal bleeding to reduce the risk of continued anovulation and endometrial hyperplasia (232).

Dexamethasone

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1893"></a>

صفحه 1893

Adjunctive oral dexamethasone may improve ovulation rates in patients resistant to clomiphene alone (243). These improvements have been reported even in the absence of adrenal hyperandrogenemia (259,260), and the mechanism of action remains unclear. Dose regimens have included 0.5 mg × 5 days starting on day of clomiphene (261), 0.5 mg × 6 weeks prior to starting clomiphene (262), and 2 mg × 10 days starting on day of clomiphene (259,260).

Oral Contraceptive Pretreatment

5 Administration of oral contraceptives (OCs) × 2 months prior to beginning a cycle of clomiphene may improve ovulation and pregnancy rates in clomiphene-resistant patients, perhaps by mitigating a pre-existing hyperandrogenic environment (243,263).

Gonadotropin Therapy Anovulatory PCOS patients who fail to ovulate or conceive with oral agents may be considered for ovulation induction with exogenous gonadotropin injections (264). For women with clomiphene-resistant PCOS, treatment with gonadotropins results in ovulation rates of 43% to 83% and pregnancy rates of 21% to 29% (265,266). Evening medication administration allows for morning monitoring and mid-day decision-making. Monitoring involves serum E2 levels and transvaginal US measurements of follicle development. Typical protocols monitor at baseline, 4 to 5 days after treatment initiation, and every 1 to 3 days until follicular maturation. Expected follicle growth is 1 to 2 mm daily after achieving 10 mm diameter (264). Given the goal of promoting growth of a single mature follicle, low initial gonadotropin doses of 37.5 to 75 international units (IU)/day are generally recommended, with increases in doses by 50% of the previous dose after 7 days if no follicle >10 mm is observed (232,264). Typical treatment duration is 7 to 12 days, but some patients require longer medication regimens for adequate stimulation (264). The maximum required gonadotropin dose seldom exceeds 225 IU per day (264). Ovulation triggering with hCG is recommended for gonadotropin cycles and is used when 1 to 2 follicles are 16 to 18 mm diameter and the E2 level per dominant follicle is 150 to 300 pg/mL (264). Ovulation is expected 24 to 48 hours after the hCG trigger. GnRH agonists such as leuprolide (500 µg subcutaneously) can be used to trigger ovulation to minimize risks associated with ovarian hyperstimulation syndrome (OHSS), but require progesterone supplementation following administration (264). Intercourse should be recommended within 24 to 48 hours of ovulation triggering or IUI 24 to 36 hours after triggering (264). Testing for pregnancy is performed within 15 to 16 days after ovulation triggering.

Gonadotropin Preparations Several gonadotropin preparations are available. hMG is derived from human urine and includes approximately equivalent FSH and LH (derived from hCG) activity of 75 IU each (252). Formulations of hMG and FSH can be administered either subcutaneously or intramuscularly (252). FSH-only preparations may be derived either from urine or via recombinant methods, and are packaged either as lyophilized powder or premixed liquid cartridges/pens (252). Except for highly purified urinary FSH, which has an FSH activity of 82.5 IU per ampule, all other products contain 75 IU of gonadotropin when supplied in ampules (252). All preparations of FSH are highly purified, with minimal to no batch-to-batch variation and a high level of safety regardless of the derived source (252). 6 For PCOS patients, FSH alone appears to be sufficient in the gonadotropin preparation, although hMG is not harmful (Table 36-6) (264). Contraindications to gonadotropin therapy are listed in Table 36-7.

صفحه 1894

Gonadotropin Outcomes Cumulative live birth rates are similar with gonadotropins compared to clomiphene for ovulation induction when the goal is monofollicular ovulation and a maximum of six ovulatory cycles is similarly recommended (232). 6 When compared to other anovulatory patients, PCOS patients using gonadotropins are at higher risk for multiple gestations (36%), OHSS (4.6%), and cycle cancellation (10%) as a result of their high numbers of baseline antral follicles (232,236,264). Cancellation should be strongly considered in patients who reach E2 levels of 1,000 to 2,500 pg/mL, have ≥3 follicles ≥16 mm, or ≥2 follicles ≥16 mm plus ≥2 follicles ≥14 mm (232). Sequential use of gonadotropins and either clomiphene or aromatase inhibitors has been associated with lower gonadotropin requirements and lower cancellation rates and treatment duration without compromising pregnancy rates. The addition of aromatase inhibitors is associated with a lower number of dominant follicles and lower maximum E2 levels (267–269).

Surgical Treatment For clomiphene or letrozole-resistant patients, laparoscopic ovarian diathermy has been performed to decrease ovarian androgen-producing tissue and to promote ovulation without the risk of multiple pregnancy associated with gonadotropin administration (232,270). In this technique, 3 to 6 puncture sites per ovary are typically performed via laparoscopy using electrocautery/diathermy (271–274) or laser (275–277), although transvaginal US-guided (278) procedures have been reported. Within 12 months after ovarian drilling, cumulative ovulation, clinical pregnancy, and live birth rates are 52%, 26% to 48%, and 13% to 32%, respectively (270). These outcomes are similar to those using gonadotropins, but

تصاویر استخراج‌شدهٔ همین صفحه

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1895"></a>

صفحه 1895

ovarian diathermy carries a lower multiple gestation rate (270). Outcomes are not significantly different when diathermy versus laser are used for drilling (270), but they are compromised when patient age >35 years or basal FSH >10 mIU/mL (272). 6 The risks of ovarian diathermy include surgical complications, adhesions, recurrence of anovulation, and a risk of iatrogenically diminishing ovarian reserve (270,279).

Ovulation Induction for Other Anovulatory Disorders

Hyperprolactinemia Hyperprolactinemia can be associated with oligomenorrhea or amenorrhea and should be evaluated with a pituitary MRI to exclude macroadenoma or other intracranial pathology (280).

5 Dopamine agonists are first-line agents in symptomatic patients with hyperprolactinemia to restore ovulation (280). Bromocriptine normalizes prolactin levels and induces ovulation in 80% to 90% of patients (281). It is taken two to three times daily, and most patients will respond to a total dose <7.5 mg daily (281). Side effects can be significant and include nausea, vomiting, postural hypotension, and headache (281). For this reason, bromocriptine is begun with a low dose of 1.25 mg (half of a 2.5-mg tablet) daily with food in evenings and prolactin level can be repeated 2 weeks later to adjust the dose as appropriate (282). The 1.25-mg dose may be adequate for those with prolactin level <50 ng/mL, while those <100 ng/mL may need 2.5 mg daily (282). In patients unable to tolerate oral administration, bromocriptine has been used vaginally 5 mg daily (283). Cabergoline has similarly high efficacy and the advantage of a 0.25-mg twice-weekly dosing schedule and fewer side effects (281). 5 In a review of over 700 cases of cabergoline use at the time of conception, the risk of miscarriage and malformations was no higher than the baseline rate in the general population (281).

Hypogonadotropic Hypogonadism

5 Anovulation in the presence of low serum LH, FSH, and E2 levels defines hypogonadotropic hypogonadism and reflects dysfunction within the hypothalamic– pituitary axis (280). Causes of hypogonadotropic hypogonadism, including craniopharyngiomas, pituitary adenomas, arteriovenous malformations, or other central space- occupying lesions, should be excluded using magnetic resonance imaging (280). Stress, extreme weight loss, anorexia, excessive exercise, and low BMI are all associated with functional hypothalamic suppression, so good nutrition and optimal body weight should be encouraged to restore ovulation (280,284). Leptin is a hormone produced by peripheral adipocytes that reflects energy stores and is deficient in women with diet or exercise-induced amenorrhea (285,286). Exogenous leptin has been reported to restore ovulation in these women (285,286). Other conditions of hypothalamic dysfunction, such as congenital hypothalamic failure (Kallmann syndrome), can be treated using pulsatile GnRH therapy or gonadotropins. In these patients, FSH and LH should be administered (280,287). Pulsatile GnRH agonist therapy (25 ng/kg every 60 to 90 minutes) simulates normal physiology and offers some advantages over gonadotropin injections, including fewer multiple gestations and less OHSS while maintaining excellent pregnancy rates (287).

Hypothyroidism The prevalence of hypothyroidism among mid-reproductive aged women is 2% to 4% and is mostly caused by autoimmune factors (288). Menstrual abnormalities, including those from

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1896"></a>

صفحه 1896

anovulation, are present in 23% to 68% of overtly hypothyroid women and can be corrected with levothyroxine replacement (288). The presence of antithyroid antibodies (even if euthyroid) are associated with increased rates spontaneous pregnancy loss and lower live birth rates (289). However, among euthyroid Chinese women with elevated thyroid peroxidase antibodies, levothyroxine supplementation 25 to 50 mcg daily did not affect miscarriage or live birth rates with IVF in a 2017 randomized trial (290). 5 In any case, because even very mild or subclinical hypothyroidism can have adverse effects on fetal brain development and subsequent intelligence quotient, it is prudent to screen and treat women with thyroid hormone abnormalities before commencing infertility treatment (288).

Tubal Factor Tubal factor accounts for 25% to 35% of female infertility (16). More than half of cases of tubal infertility are a result of salpingitis. 7 The incidence of tubal infertility has been reported to be 8%, 19.5%, and 40% after one, two, and three episodes of PID, respectively (291). Live birth rates are negatively affected by the severity of a single episode of PID (292). C. trachomatis and Neisseria gonorrhoeae are common pathogens associated with PID and infertility (293). M. hominis and U. urealyticum have been implicated in PID but their contribution to infertility is less clear (293). Many patients with documented tubal damage have no history of PID and are presumed to have had subclinical chlamydial infections (294,295). Noninfectious causes for tubal factor include pelvic adhesions from prior surgery, tubal endometriosis, tubal polyps, tubal spasm, and intratubal mucous and debris (16).

Hysterosalpingography Hysterosalpingography (HSG) remains the standard initial test to evaluate tubal patency (296). HSG is performed after menses but prior to ovulation between cycle days 5 and 12 to avoid potential pregnancy and take advantage of the thinner proliferative phase endometrium (297). The patient is typically premedicated 30 to 60 minutes prior to the procedure with ibuprofen or related medication (297). Lidocaine injected intracervically may provide further pain relief (298). With the patient in the dorsal lithotomy position, either a metal cannula or a balloon catheter is inserted through the cervix and past the internal cervical os. 2 Contrast dye is injected under fluoroscopy to visualize the uterine cavity, fallopian tube architecture, and assess tubal patency (297,299). Certain disease processes, such as salpingitis isthmica nodosa, have a characteristic appearance on HSG, with honeycombing as a result of contrast-filled diverticular projections (300). Compared to laparoscopy, HSG has a sensitivity and specificity of 65% and 83% for the diagnosis of tubal patency (301). By flushing inspissated mucus and debris, the HSG procedure may have therapeutic value, particularly with use of oil- based contrast. A large multicenter RCT that randomized 1,119 women to oil- or water-based contrast found a significantly higher live birth rate after use of oil-based contrast at HSG (38% vs. 28%; RR 1.38, 95% CI 1.17–1.64) (302).

Hysterosalpingography Risks Although use of oil-based contrast is thought to carry potential risk of intravasation-associated oil embolism, this complication has not been observed in large prospective studies (303). Patients with a history of mild iodinated contrast reaction can be pretreated with glucocorticoids (299). HSG is contraindicated in patients with moderate or severe contrast reaction. The risk of PID after HSG is 0.3% to 3.1% overall but is >10% in the setting of hydrosalpinges (304,305).

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1897"></a>

صفحه 1897

Therefore, HSG should be avoided in the setting of current or suspected pelvic infection (297,305). The role of routine antibiotic prophylaxis for HSG is controversial (306), but in high- risk patients doxycycline could be considered. Recommended dosing is 100 mg twice daily, beginning the day before HSG and continuing for 3 to 5 days. If prophylaxis is not used and hydrosalpinges are noted on examination, postprocedure doxycycline treatment is recommended. Other rare complications of HSG include vascular intravasation, cervical laceration, uterine perforation, hemorrhage, vasovagal reactions, severe pain, and allergic response to the contrast dye (297).

Sonohysterography Many infertility practices routinely use sonohysterography to assess tubal patency because it can be performed in a typical fertility clinic and involves no ionizing radiation exposure for patient or staff. 2 This advantage over HSG is at least partially offset by an inability to accurately define tubal architecture when performed using standard 2D approaches. Hysterosalpingo- contrast sonography (HyCoSy) is a version of sonohysterography that uses an echogenic contrast media with air bubbles to aid in the determination of tubal patency with sensitivity and specificity ranging from 76% to 96% and 67% to 100%, respectively (307).

Chlamydia Serology Chlamydia is a well-known cause of tubal inflammation and dysfunction, particularly with repeated infection. The compromised specificity of chlamydia antibody testing (CAT) as a result of cross-reactivity with other Chlamydia species limited its clinical utility as a predictive test for tubal factor infertility. Newer, Chlamydia trachomatis-species specific assays demonstrate promise in the assessment of tubal function, even in the presence of tubal patency. In a study of 1,250 women with documented tubal patency, anti-C. trachomatis IgG3 seropositivity was associated with a lower likelihood of pregnancy and a threefold increased risk of ectopic pregnancy (308).

Laparoscopy

2 Laparoscopy is considered the gold standard for diagnosing tubal and peritoneal disease (309). It allows visualization of all pelvic organs and permits detection and potential concurrent treatment of intramural and subserosal uterine fibroids, peritubal and periovarian adhesions, and endometriosis (309). Abnormal findings on HSG can be validated by direct visualization at laparoscopy using chromopertubation, which involves the transcervical installation of a dye such as methylene blue to directly visualize tubal patency and fimbrial architecture (309). 2 Even laparoscopy has been reported to have a false-positive rate of 11% for proximal tubal occlusion when resected tubal segments are examined pathologically (310).

Treatment of Tubal Factor Infertility

7 As IVF success rates continue to improve, the role for surgery in the treatment of tubal infertility has been questioned (311). National ART registry data indicates that the live birth rate per cycle in patients with tubal infertility are similar to the overall rate. However, surgery remains effective for certain indications and may be the optimal approach in some patients. Variables impacting the decision between reparative surgery or IVF for tubal factor infertility include patient age, ovarian reserve, number of children desired, extent of tubal disease, other infertility factors, surgeon experience, IVF success rates of the program, cost, and patient preference (312).

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1898"></a>

صفحه 1898

Proximal Tubal Occlusion Proximal tubal cannulation performed either via HSG or hysteroscopy can restore tubal patency in up to 85% of obstructions, although the re-occlusion rate approaches 30% (16). The best candidates for proximal tubal cannulation have suspected muscle spasm, stromal edema, amorphous debris, mucosal agglutination, or viscous secretions, while nonresponders include those with luminal fibrosis, failed tubal re-anastomosis, fibroids, congenital atresia, or tuberculosis (16). Occlusion caused by salpingitis isthmica nodosa, endometriosis, synechiae, salpingitis, and cornual polyps will only occasionally respond to cannulation (16). Tubal cannulation involves the placement of an outer catheter into the uterotubal ostium for the selective injection of contrast media or colored dye (16,300). If tubal blockage is demonstrated, an inner catheter with a guide wire is advanced into the tube with gentle pressure. Tubal perforation, typically minor, occurs in 1.9% to 11% of cases generally with no adverse clinical consequences (16,300,310). Catheterization under fluoroscopy during HSG is referred to as selective salpingography (313). Visualization of patency with the hysteroscopic approach can be accomplished using laparoscopy (16). Tubal patency and pregnancy rates are similar with both the hysteroscopic and fluoroscopic approaches with pooled pregnancy rates around 30% (314). If occlusion persists or recurs, IVF is usually recommended.

Distal Tubal Occlusion (Excluding Sterilization or Hydrosalpinx)

7 Distal tubal disease and occlusion are causal in 85% of all tubal infertility and can be secondary to a variety of inflammatory conditions including infection, endometriosis, or prior abdominal or pelvic surgery (300,315). Patients younger than 35 years of age with mild distal tubal disease, normal tubal mucosa, and absent or minimal pelvic adhesions are the best candidates for corrective microsurgery (315). IVF should be considered for older patients or those with DOR, combined proximal and distal tubal disease, severe pelvic adhesions, tubal damage that is not amenable to reconstruction, or additional infertility factors (315,316). Fimbrioplasty involves lysis of fimbrial adhesions or dilation of fimbrial phimosis, whereas salpingostomy (also known as neosalpingostomy) involves the creation of a new tubal opening in an occluded fallopian tube (315). In well-selected patients, pregnancy rates are reported to be 32% to 42.2%, 54.6% to 60%, 30% to 34.6%, and 55.9% for adhesiolysis, fimbrioplasty, neosalpingostomy, and nonsterilization-related anastomosis, respectively (300,315). As a group, these procedures are associated with a 7.9% rate of subsequent ectopic pregnancy (315).

Hydrosalpinx Distal occlusion may lead to fluid buildup in the fallopian tube causing a hydrosalpinx (300). Hydrosalpinx fluid impairs fertility via decreased endometrial receptivity or a direct embryotoxic effect (300). Two separate meta-analyses concluded that IVF pregnancy rates were approximately 50% lower in the presence of hydrosalpinges (317,318). Salpingectomy for hydrosalpinx prior to IVF significantly improves both pregnancy and live birth rates when compared to IVF performed with the fallopian tubes in situ (319,320), although laparoscopic tubal occlusion appears to be a reasonable alternative (321). Ultrasound-guided needle aspiration of a hydrosalpinx is associated with variable benefit and high recurrence rates. Sclerotherapy involves ultrasound-guided injection of the hydrosalpinx with ethanol to sclerose and reduce the secretory function of the tube. A meta-analysis of ten studies revealed similar clinical pregnancy rates between sclerotherapy and salpingectomy (322).

Sterilization Reversal

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1899"></a>

صفحه 1899

Twenty percent of women express regret following sterilization, and 1% to 5% of those will request reversal (316,323). The technique for sterilization reversal involves microsurgical dissection of the occluded ends of the fallopian tube followed by a two-layered reapproximation of the proximal and distal tubal segments (316). Surgical approaches include minilaparotomy, laparoscopy (316), and robotic-assisted laparoscopy (323). A systematic review revealed a 65% to 68% pooled pregnancy rate for these surgical approaches, with success most associated with patient age (324). Ectopic pregnancy rates following the tubal reanastomosis procedure are generally <10% but may approach 18% (315,325). The main predictors of success are age <35 years (316,325), isthmic–isthmic or ampulo-ampullar anastomosis (325), final anastomosed tubal length >4 cm (316), and less destructive sterilization methods such as rings or clips (316,325). Unlike vasectomy reversal, the length of time between fallopian tubal sterilization and reversal does not seem to affect outcome (316). 8 IVF should be considered in lieu of sterilization reversal for older patients or those with DOR, severe pelvic adhesions, additional infertility factors or prior unsuccessful reanastomosis (315,316).

Uterine Factors

7 Pathologies within the uterine cavity are the sole cause of infertility in as many as 15% of couples seeking treatment (326) and are diagnosed in >50% of infertile patients (326). Therefore, the evaluation of the couple with infertility should consistently include an assessment of the endometrial cavity. Uterine cavity abnormalities include endometrial polyps, endometrial hyperplasia, submucous myomas, intrauterine synechiae, and congenital uterine anomalies (327).

Diagnostic Imaging for Uterine Pathology

Hysteroscopy

7 Hysteroscopy is considered the gold standard for uterine cavity evaluation because it permits direct visualization (326–328). The procedure involves insertion of an endoscope through the cervical canal into the uterine cavity and instillation of distention media to facilitate visualization (326–328). Diagnostic hysteroscopy may be performed in the office using a small diameter hysteroscope and saline distention, often without need for anesthesia (327). To optimize visualization of the endometrial cavity and avoid performing the procedure during early pregnancy, hysteroscopy is typically scheduled during the early- to mid-follicular phase of the cycle (326). Disadvantages to the procedure include poor visualization when uterine bleeding is present and the inability to evaluate structures outside the uterine cavity, including those in the myometrium and adnexa (326). Office hysteroscopy is reported to have 72% sensitivity for cavity abnormalities when compared to operative hysteroscopy using general anesthesia (326).

Hysterosalpingogram Insofar as it allows assessment of tubal and uterine pathology, HSG (“Tubal Factors”) is a reasonable initial imaging technique to use in the basic infertility evaluation (327). Hysterosalpingogram shows the general configuration of the uterine cavity and indicates endometrial lesions as filling defects or irregularities of the uterine wall (327). 2 Excessive contrast may lead to false-negative findings (327), which may account for the 50% sensitivity of HSG compared to hysteroscopy in diagnosing endometrial polyps (308). Inability to discriminate between air bubbles, mucous, and debris and true intracavitary pathology may account for HSG’s high false-positive rate when compared with hysteroscopy (327,328). Other drawbacks include patient discomfort, use of iodinated contrast, and radiation

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1900"></a>

صفحه 1900

exposure (327).

Transvaginal Ultrasound Compared to hysteroscopy, standard 2D transvaginal US has a 75% PPV and 96.5% negative predictive value (NPV) for intracavitary polyps but a 0% PPV for intrauterine adhesions (328). Like HSG, it has a sensitivity of 44% for uterine malformations (328).

Saline Sonohysterography Saline infusion sonography (SIS), synonymous with sonohysterography, involves the transcervical instillation of saline, often via a balloon catheter, during transvaginal US to distend the uterine cavity and delineate the endometrium (329). As with office hysteroscopy and HSG, SIS is performed during the cycle’s follicular phase and anesthesia is typically not required (329). Endometrial polyps appear as hyperechogenic-pedunculated lesions, submucous fibroids have mixed echogenicity, and adhesions contain densely echogenic and cystic areas (329). Compared to hysteroscopy, SIS has 100% sensitivity, specificity, positive and negative predictive values for uterine polyps (328). As a result of the smaller volume of distention media used, SIS is generally better tolerated than HSG or hysteroscopy (326,329). Another advantage of SIS is the ability to evaluate the adnexa, and the myometrium for fibroids or adenomyosis. When combined with 3D technology, SIS is particularly good for assessing the overall uterine contour and delineating congenital anomalies such as septate uteri (329). Standard SIS has a somewhat lower sensitivity of 77.8% in detecting uterine congenital anomalies when compared to hysteroscopy; however, this sensitivity is higher than 2D transvaginal US or HSG (328). 2 Hysterosalpingogram and SIS perform similarly for intrauterine adhesions, each having approximately 50% PPV and >90% NPV (328).

Magnetic Resonance Imaging (MRI) Although transvaginal US, HSG, SIS, and hysteroscopy may suggest congenital uterine anomalies, pelvic MRI is considered the gold standard for imaging and is particularly useful for diagnosing rudimentary uterine horns (330). Pelvic MRI has the best sensitivity and specificity for intramural and submucous myomas compared to pathologic examination (331) and is especially useful for large or multiple fibroids (332). 7 MRI has been suggested as a tool to differentiate fibroids from adenomyosis, but routine use over US is not recommended (332,333).

Congenital Anomalies of the Uterus Congenital uterine anomalies occur in 3% to 4% of women. This increases to 5% to 10% in women with early pregnancy loss and up to 25% in those with second and third trimester pregnancy losses (330). During female embryonic development, the paired paramesonephric or mullerian ducts elongate toward each other and fuse in the midline. This is followed by resorption of the intervening septum to form the upper vagina, cervix, uterus, and fallopian tubes by week 20 of gestation (334). Failure of any of these steps leads to absent uterine development or development of unicornuate, bicornuate, arcuate, didelphys, or septate uteri (334). Because of the proximity of the paramesonephric ducts to the urinary system, renal anomalies often coexist with mullerian anomalies. Appropriate urologic imaging should be performed whenever a mullerian anomaly (outside of septate uteri) is diagnosed (330,334). 2 Uterine anomalies are more closely associated with pregnancy wastage and poor obstetric outcomes than infertility, as the prevalence of congenital uterine defects is generally similar among

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1901"></a>

صفحه 1901

fertile and infertile women (330). The exception to this is mullerian agenesis. Patients with mullerian agenesis can have genetically related children only through use of IVF and a gestational carrier (330). However, there have been recent efforts to treat absolute uterine factor infertility with uterine transplantation. Uterine transplantation is a complex surgery that entails either a live or deceased donor and a multidisciplinary team for recipient and donor (335). The arcuate uterus is the mildest congenital uterine anomaly and typically live birth rates are comparable to those in women with normal uteri. Rudimentary uterine horns require removal on diagnosis (330). The number of reproductive-age patients presenting with in utero exposure to DES is now very rare, as the substance was banned in 1971 (330). Women whose mothers were exposed to DES have higher rates of uterine malformations (e.g., T-shaped uterus) and associated obstetric complications (330).

Acquired Abnormalities of the Uterus

Leiomyomas Leiomyomas, also called myomas or fibroids, are benign monoclonal uterine myometrial tumors that affect 25% to 45% of all reproductive-age women; this number is even higher in African Americans (336). The mechanisms by which fibroids cause infertility are unknown, but may involve altered uterine contractility, impaired gamete transport, or endometrial dysfunction (337). Among women with infertility and uterine leiomyomas, pregnancy rates are primarily affected by leiomyoma location (331,337). 7 Subserosal fibroids do not appear to affect fertility or obstetric outcomes, while cavity-distorting intramural, noncavity-distorting >5 cm, and submucosal myomas are associated with lower implantation and live birth rates (331,337,338).

Myomectomy In women desiring fertility who require treatment for fibroids, myomectomy is the preferred approach and uterine artery embolization is relatively contraindicated (337). Removal of cavity- distorting intramural and submucous myomas is generally recommended prior to proceeding with infertility treatment (331,337). 7 The utility of surgical removal of noncavity-distorting intramural fibroids is unknown (331,337,338). Myomectomy can be performed hysteroscopically (332), via laparotomy (336,339), laparoscopically (alone (336,340), or with robotic assistance) (339,340), or vaginally (341). Hysteroscopic removal is generally preferred for small submucous fibroids (332), while use of the other methods generally depends on patient preference, operator skill, or the presence of other pelvic pathologies (336,339,340). The utility of pretreatment with GnRH agonists prior to surgery is debatable. GnRH agonists may shrink larger fibroids (5 to 6 cm) enough to allow hysteroscopic resection (332) and may decrease the risk of intraoperative blood loss and postoperative anemia (336). Fluid overload and uterine perforation are the most common complications of hysteroscopic myomectomy (332), while bleeding and adjacent organ injury are more often associated with alternative approaches (336). Fibroids located low on the uterus and posteriorly are less amenable to laparoscopic resection (336). Transmyometrial approaches raise concern for uterine rupture during subsequent pregnancy, although this risk appears to be very low (336).

Endometrial Polyps The incidence of asymptomatic endometrial polyps among women with infertility has been reported to range from 6% to 8% (342,343) but may be as high as 32% (344). Risk factors for

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1902"></a>

صفحه 1902

polyp development include obesity, unopposed estrogen exposure, and polycystic ovary syndrome (345). The mechanisms by which endometrial polyps may impair fertility are incompletely described but may relate to disordered endometrial receptivity (345). One report localized 32% of endometrial polyps in infertile women to the posterior uterine wall, indicated that 40.3% of patients had multiple polyps, and stated a 6.9% hyperplasia rate (346). Polypectomy is generally performed via curettage, blind avulsion, or hysteroscopic removal (344). 7 Although the efficacy of polypectomy prior to infertility treatment has not been clearly established (344), a prospective randomized trial showed a 2.1-fold higher rate of pregnancy among women who underwent the procedure prior to IUI (347). Higher pregnancy rates have been noted for polyps removed from the uterotubal junction when compared to those removed from other locations (346). Smaller nonrandomized studies provide conflicting data on the negative fertility effects of polyps <1.5 to 2 cm (346,348,349).

Intrauterine Synechiae or Asherman Syndrome Severe trauma to the basalis layer of the endometrium with subsequent tissue bridge formation leads to intrauterine synechiae or Asherman syndrome (350). Symptoms of severe disease include amenorrhea, menstrual irregularities, spontaneous abortion, and recurrent pregnancy loss (350). 1 The causes of intrauterine adhesions are often iatrogenic, with patients typically reporting intraoperative or postoperative complications of uterine evacuations for incomplete pregnancy loss, pregnancy termination, or postpartum hemorrhage (350). 1 Myomectomy, hysterotomy, diagnostic curettage, cesarean section, tuberculosis, caustic abortifacients, and uterine packing are less common causes in Western countries (350). In developing countries, Asherman syndrome caused by genital tuberculosis is quite common (351). Hysteroscopic resection of synechiae is the preferred treatment to restore fertility in women with Asherman syndrome. Hysteroscopic lysis of adhesions can be accomplished with energy (L-hook electrodes, loop electrodes, or lasers) or nonenergy instruments like cold scissors (352).

Patients with genital tuberculosis have a very poor prognosis (350,351,353). Postoperative prevention of adhesion reformation may involve estrogen therapy alone × 1 month or in combination with intraoperative placement of an intrauterine device (such as a small Malecot catheter or pediatric Foley catheter) for 1 to 2 weeks (350,353). There is no standard regimen for estrogen therapy, but oral conjugated estrogens 2.5 mg daily overlapping with progestin (350) or E2 valerate 2-mg injections daily (353) have been suggested.

Luteal Phase Defect and Progesterone Supplementation

Mechanisms The luteal phase is normally characterized by progesterone secretion by the corpus luteum and appropriate endometrial secretory transformation that allow for embryonic implantation in the endometrium and support of early pregnancy for the first 7 to 8 weeks of gestation (354,355). Luteal phase deficiency (LPD) is a condition in which endogenous progesterone is not sufficient to maintain a functional secretory endometrium during the implantation window (356,357) and is thought to account for 4% of infertility (356). Proposed mechanisms for LPD include inadequate production of progesterone following ovulation (358), improper GnRH pulsatility causing insufficient gonadotropin production during the LH surge (356,357), and inadequate endometrial responsivity to progesterone (356). Assisted reproductive technologies (ARTs) or gonadotropin ovulation induction medications may induce iatrogenic LPD via

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1903"></a>

صفحه 1903

disruption of granulosa cells from follicular aspiration and suppression of endogenous LH secretion through a combination of supraphysiologic E2 levels and GnRH agonist/antagonist therapy (355,358).

Diagnosis Diagnostic criteria for LPD have been variably defined but have included a low midluteal phase serum progesterone level, a BBT rise lasting less than 11 days, and a shortened luteal phase of less than 14 days (359). Unfortunately, the characteristic pulsatile secretion of progesterone during the luteal phase of the menstrual cycle combines with wide temporal variations (even within a 60- to 90-minute time span) to make interpretation of midluteal progesterone levels difficult (354). Similar rates of shortened luteal phase are found in fertile and infertile women and there is significant variability of luteal phase length from cycle to cycle in an individual woman (357). The notion of a universal optimal duration of progesterone to achieve embryo implantation has been questioned (360). 7 There is significant interobserver variability in pathologic interpretation of endometrial biopsies from infertile women (361) and out-of-phase biopsy results poorly discriminate between fertile and infertile women (362).

Treatment

8 Progesterone therapy is considered standard practice during ART cycles but is more controversial during non-ART fertility treatments (363,364). When used, progesterone supplementation can be administered via oral, vaginal, or intramuscular routes, with highest serum levels associated with daily 50- to 100-mg intramuscular progesterone (363,364). Most products are delivered in oil, and caution should be used to ascertain the presence of sesame or peanut allergies prior to initiation. Vaginal micronized progesterone, 200 to 600 mg daily in divided doses, is associated with the highest endometrial concentrations of progesterone. The side effects of this delivery route can include vaginal discharge and irritation. Other vaginal preparations include a once-daily gel and a 100-mg insert that is given two to three times daily. As a result of erratic absorption and decreased bioavailability, oral micronized progesterone is less effective for progesterone supplementation (363). There remains no consensus regarding the superiority of vaginal versus intramuscular administration. When hCG is used for trigger, hCG levels peak at the time of egg retrieval, followed by a rise in serum progesterone levels that peak 5 days postretrieval and then decline (363). There are no differences in pregnancy or live birth rates when progesterone is initiated on the same day, immediately following retrieval versus 1 day after retrieval (365). A systematic 2015 review indicated lower pregnancy rates when progesterone support was administered prior to hCG-triggered egg retrieval or delayed until 6 days postretrieval and suggested an initiation window for intramuscular progesterone of day 0 post-egg retrieval to day 3 postretrieval (363). This initiation window is consistent with descriptions of luteal phase support during GnRH-agonist–triggered cycles (366). Because of the more rapid endometrial advancement compared to the intramuscular route, delayed vaginal progesterone administration until 48 hours postretrieval has been advocated, but better-quality randomized trials are needed before drawing definitive conclusions (363). 8 Though the optimum duration of supplementation has not been determined, progesterone is typically continued until 8 to 10 weeks of gestation (363,364). Intravaginal luteal phase supplemental progesterone may increase pregnancy rates in women with PCOS treated with letrozole (367). 7 A 2017 systematic review and meta-analysis indicated a

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1904"></a>

صفحه 1904

benefit for unexplained infertility using primarily vaginal progesterone initiated 0 to 2 days post-IUI in cycles using clomiphene alone or with gonadotropins with 1 additional live birth for every 11 patients treated (364).

Pelvic Factor

Endometriosis Endometriosis affects 6% to 10% reproductive-age women (368) but is present in 25% to 50% women with unexplained infertility (309). 7 It is characterized by endometrial glands and stroma outside the uterine cavity and is found mainly on the peritoneum, ovaries, and rectovaginal septum (368). Fecundability rates in affected patients are estimated at 2% to 10% per month (369). Possible mechanisms for infertility among women with endometriosis include anatomic distortion from adhesions or fibrosis and the impact of chronic inflammation on gametes, embryos, tubal fimbria, and eutopic endometrium (368,369). Laparoscopic visualization and histologic confirmation of biopsied lesion(s) remains the optimal method for the diagnosis of endometriosis. 7 The disease is staged laparoscopically according to the Revised ASRM’s classification, with stages III to IV (moderate–severe) characterized by ovarian endometriomas, dense tubal or ovarian adhesions, and/or cul-de-sac obliteration (368). Vaginal US has excellent specificity for endometriomas and other deep disease (370). It is unclear whether the mere presence of endometriosis negatively affects IVF outcomes. Endometriosis patients have similar rates of embryonic aneuploidy (371) and live birth with ARTs (372,373) as compared to unaffected patients. 7 However, more severe disease (stages III and IV) and previous endometriosis surgery are associated with fewer oocytes retrieved and reduced pregnancy/live birth rates (by 30% to 40%) compared to patients without endometriosis (372,373).

Endometriosis Infertility

Management Hormonal suppression of endometriosis typically has minimal benefit for endometriosis-related infertility (368,374). For minimal to mild disease, laparoscopic ablation appears to significantly improve pregnancy rates when compared to diagnostic laparoscopy alone as evidenced by a large, randomized trial reporting 31% (treated) versus 17% (untreated) pregnancy rates (375,376). A subsequent meta-analysis supported the effectiveness of laparoscopic treatment in stages I to II endometriosis-associated infertility (368,369,377,378). 7 Number needed to treat analysis suggests that eight laparoscopies involving treatment of mild or minimal endometriosis would need to be performed for each pregnancy gained. A Cochrane review found moderate evidence in favor of excisional laparoscopic treatment of minimal-mild endometriosis for spontaneous pregnancy in the 9 to 12 months postoperatively (374). Although removal of endometriomas may be indicated prior to IVF when their presence would interfere with oocyte retrieval (376), endometrioma resection during IVF/ICSI treatment is associated with decreased ovarian function in up to 13% of cases (379,380), reduced quantity of dominant follicles, and fewer retrieved oocytes compared to unoperated patients in a meta-analysis of 2,649 IVF cycles (381). Furthermore, 40% of endometriomas recur postoperatively (376). 8 Therefore, IVF is considered a reasonable first-line therapy for endometriosis-associated infertility because of the short time to pregnancy and avoidance of surgery (311). Sclerotherapy, whereby the endometrioma is drained via transvaginal puncture and a sclerotic

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1905"></a>

صفحه 1905

agent such as ethanol is instilled into the cyst cavity, was associated with higher numbers of retrieved oocytes and similar pregnancy rates as compared to laparoscopic management, in a meta-analysis of 18 studies, and may be an alternative to surgery (372).
