Adhesions Adhesions may result from sharp, mechanical, or thermal injury, infection, radiation, ischemia, desiccation, abrasion, or foreign body reaction (382). Adhesiolysis improves pregnancy rates by 12% at 1 year and by 29% at 2 years in infertile women with adnexal adhesions (382). Use of adhesion barriers reduces adhesion formation following laparoscopy and laparotomy, but there is no consistent evidence for improvement in pregnancy rates (382,383).

Unexplained Infertility Fifteen percent to 30% of couples are diagnosed with unexplained infertility, in which the basic infertility evaluation reveals normal semen parameters, evidence of ovulation, at least one patent fallopian tube, and no other obvious, treatable cause of infertility (384). Patients with unexplained infertility may feel reassured that even after 12 months of attempting without success, 20% will conceive in the following 12 months and over 50% in the following 36 months (6). In couples with good prognostic factors of female age <30, <24 months of infertility, and a previous pregnancy in the same partnership (6), unexplained infertility may merely reflect the lower extreme of normal fertility (384). Technology is likely limited in terms of diagnosing all causes for infertility (384). 2 The utility of evaluations other than basic testing in an infertile couple has yet to be proven (385).

Proposed Mechanisms for Unexplained Infertility

Immunologic Factors The impact of the immune system on reproductive function is an area of intense study and debate. Among the potential immune causes of reproductive failure are imbalances in the T- lymphocyte population. By increasing the secretion of anti-inflammatory TH2 cytokines and inhibiting the secretion of proinflammatory TH1 cytokines, progesterone shifts the immune microenvironment balance to TH2 dominance to support embryo implantation and early pregnancy (386). The absence of a reliable screening test for TH1:TH2 balance has handicapped studies designed to influence the balance toward improved outcomes. Although serum antiphospholipid antibodies and antithyroid antibodies are more prevalent among patients with unexplained infertility than among fertile women (387), the presence of antiphospholipid antibodies has not been found to adversely affect IVF outcomes, so screening is discouraged (388). Furthermore, a randomized, double-blinded, placebo-controlled trial found no difference in in vitro fertilization-embryo transfer (IVF-ET) live birth rates with administration of heparin 5,000 units SC and aspirin 100 mg daily in fertility patients with detectable antiphospholipid antibodies (389). The relationship of thyroid antibodies and reproductive outcomes are discussed in the Ovulatory Factor section.

Unexplained infertility has been associated with antisperm antibodies (390), described further in the Male Factor section. Uterine natural killer cells (UNKs) account for more than 70% of leukocytes in the secretory endometrium and appear to play a primary role in regulating early placentation (391). UNKs express specialized cell surface receptors known as killer cell immunoglobulin receptors (KIRs), encoded by highly polymorphic genes classically divided into

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1906"></a>

صفحه 1906

inhibitory (KIR A) and activating (KIR B) haplotypes (392). The ligand for KIRs is the highly polymorphic HLA-C molecule expressed by extravillous trophoblast cells. In a study of 668 euploid single embryo transfers with annotation of maternal KIR and embryonic HLA-C haplotypes, maternal KIR haplotype was found to influence the risk of pregnancy loss and the HLA-C ligands modified the risk (393). High-risk combinations such as KIR A/homozygous C2 resulted in a 51% increase in the risk of pregnancy loss with euploid embryo transfer (393). 2 These studies support a potential role for immune factors contributing to unsuccessful implantation and pregnancy, but such testing is not yet recommended outside of an IRB- approved experimental study.

Infection C. trachomatis and related clinical and subclinical infections have been discussed in the “Tubal Factor” section. No consistent associations have been reported between chlamydial species, M. hominis and unexplained infertility in men or women (295,394,395). U. urealyticum and Mycoplasma genitalium may be of more concern (396). Prophylactic doxycycline (100 mg twice daily for 4 weeks) given to infertile couples improved pregnancy rates only among couples in whom the male partner cleared ureaplasma infections (397). Antimicrobial prophylaxis is often given for ART, but the extent to which clearance of these organisms improves pregnancy rates is not clear (396,398). Chronic endometritis is an inflammatory condition characterized by the presence of plasma cells in the endometrium. It is found in 56.8% of patients with unexplained infertility, 57.6% of patients with repeat implantation failure, and 42.3% of women with endometriosis (399–401). A significantly higher rate of spontaneous pregnancies and live births were observed in patients with chronic endometritis who were successfully treated with antibiotics as compared to patients with persistent endometritis after antibiotics (400). 8 Although the uterine cavity has classically been designated to be sterile, several different bacterial communities have been detected in the endometrial environment that appear to influence implantation following IVF embryo transfer (402).

Undiagnosed Pelvic Pathology Newer evidence suggests that undiagnosed endometriosis may be a prominent contributor to the pathogenesis of unexplained infertility. An alternative to laparoscopy to suggest the presence of endometriosis is endometrial biopsy testing for a surrogate biomarker of the condition, BCL6 (B-cell CLL/lymphoma 6). BCL6 is overexpressed in patients with endometriosis and BCL6 expression has a 94% accuracy for diagnosing endometriosis. Recent evidence suggests that BCL6 expression is significantly more likely to be elevated in patients with UI than in fertile controls. Patients with UI plus +BCL6 had worse IVF outcomes than women with unexplained infertility and normal endometrial BCL6 expression (403,404). Classically, following a negative infertility workup, laparoscopy has been proposed to evaluate for peritubal adhesions that may distort tubo-ovarian relationships and endometriosis (405,406). However, there is a lack of consensus as to the frequency of these abnormalities and many practitioners will forego laparoscopy in lieu of empiric fertility treatment in such patients. In young patients, laparoscopy may be cost-effective compared to fertility therapy (407).

Occult Male or Oocyte Factors

2 Occult male factor, such as impaired sperm DNA integrity (see Male Factor) despite normal semen analysis and oocyte factors (e.g., such as premature zona hardening, mitochondrial dysfunction, and/or aberrant spindle formation) have been suggested as

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1907"></a>

صفحه 1907

putative mechanisms for unexplained infertility (408). Further research on their involvement and recommendations for testing and treatment are needed.

Treatment of Unexplained Infertility Treatment options for unexplained infertility include expectant management, ovarian stimulation with oral medications + IUI, or immediate IVF. A large longitudinal, multicenter cohort study showed that good prognosis patients (≥30% chance of spontaneous ongoing pregnancy in the next 6 to 12 months) can reasonably proceed with expectant management and have a high rate of achieving pregnancy (reported as ∼65% in an overall time to pregnancy of 5.7 months). However, some patients strongly desire to pursue treatment immediately. Several RCTs have supported the use of ovarian stimulation with oral agents (clomiphene or letrozole) + IUI for the first-line treatment of unexplained infertility in women who are <38 years of age. The American Society of Reproductive Medicine recommends trial of three to four cycles of OS + IUI with oral agents prior to proceeding to IVF (409–412). It is important to note that gonadotropins + IUI should not routinely be used in patients with unexplained infertility, as the FASTT trial, a large randomized controlled trial, concluded that patients undergoing three cycles of CC-IUI followed by six cycles of IVF had a significantly faster time to pregnancy and cost savings of >2,000 dollars than patients who underwent CC + IUI, followed by gonadotropins + IUI, and then IVF (413). An RCT in 2015 showed that letrozole resulted in a lower frequency of live birth as compared to clomiphene citrate in patients with unexplained infertility, but no difference in the incidence of multiple gestations, therefore, most practitioners initiate OS with clomid as a first-line treatment option, if able (414).

In older women, >38 years of age, immediate IVF could be considered as this has been shown to be associated with higher pregnancy rates and shorter time to pregnancy than other approaches (412). The decision to pursue immediate IVF versus a trial of OS + IUI should be carefully made between the patient and their reproductive endocrinologist, as many factors need to be considered including cost, invasiveness, reproductive prognosis, age, and desired family size.

Superovulation (Controlled Ovarian Hyperstimulation) with IUI Unlike ovulation induction, in which the goal is to stimulate the release of a single oocyte in anovulatory women, the explicit goal of superovulation (for non-ART or ART purposes) is to cause more than one egg to be ovulated, thereby increasing the probability of conception in women with unexplained infertility (228). 8 In most superovulation protocols, a baseline scan is performed on day 2 of the menstrual cycle to assess AFC and the presence or absence of ovarian cysts. Baseline estrogen and progesterone levels are typically obtained on this day (415). Superovulation with clomiphene is otherwise conducted as described above for ovulation induction. Triggering of ovulation typically occurs when at least 2 follicles have reached an average diameter of ≥17 to 18 mm and the endometrial thickness is ≥8 mm (176,411,416–419) Following a trigger shot (usually hCG), IUI is performed, as described above in the male fertility section.

Cost-Effectiveness

8 In Massachusetts, where insurance companies are required to cover infertility treatment, the cost-effectiveness of IVF in the treatment of unexplained infertility was determined (413). Couples were randomized to accelerated treatment with three cycles of clomiphene citrate/IUI, followed by up to six cycles of IVF if no pregnancy occurred (n = 256)

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1908"></a>

صفحه 1908

versus conventional treatment with clomiphene/IUI × three cycles, then injectable gonadotropins/IUI × three cycles, then IVF × six cycles (n = 247) (413). The median time to pregnancy was 8 months in the accelerated versus 11 months in the conventional treatment groups. The authors used an average cost per cycle with clomiphene/IUI of $500, with gonadotropin/IUI of 2,500,andassumedIVFcostswere<17,749/cycle. They reported that the accelerated treatment protocol resulted in a savings of $2,624 per couple and lowered the per delivery charges by $9,856 compared to conventional treatment regimens (413). The study concluded that couples with unexplained infertility who have not achieved pregnancy after three cycles of clomiphene/IUI should proceed directly to IVF (413).

Assisted Reproductive Technologies (ARTs) Process

Process of ART ARTs include IVF, ICSI, gamete intrafallopian transfer (GIFT), zygote intrafallopian transfer (ZIFT), cryopreserved embryo transfers, and the use of donor oocytes (420). Because of improved success rates associated with IVF embryo transfer, the performance of GIFT and ZIFT has declined in the United States (420), so this review will focus primarily on IVF and ICSI. Both processes involve the following:

Prevention of a premature LH surge Follicle growth Pretreatment Adjunctive medications Oocyte maturation/ovulation triggering Oocyte retrieval Luteal support Fertilization by IVF or ICSI In vitro embryo culture Transfer of fresh embryos Cryopreservation of surplus embryos Transfer of frozen embryos First trimester pregnancy monitoring

Baseline Ovarian Cysts

8 Prior to beginning therapy, a baseline US may be performed on cycle day 2 or 3 during menses (or following GnRH agonist suppression) to confirm an optimally thin (<4 mm) endometrium and quiescent ovaries. With clomiphene citrate cycles, the presence of ovarian cysts on a baseline scan was associated with decreased rates of ovulation but not of pregnancy, although cyst size was not predictive of response (421). Ovarian cysts on baseline evaluation have been associated with decreased pregnancy rates in ovulation induction cycles employing gonadotropins (422). In IVF patients, functional (estrogen- producing) cysts are seen in 9.3% of women following GnRH agonist suppression (423). Although nonfunctional ovarian cysts up to 5.3 cm did not affect IVF outcomes (424), functional ovarian cysts (mean diameter 2 cm and baseline E2 180 pg/mL) have been associated with increased gonadotropin requirements (dosing and duration), higher cancellation rates, fewer retrieved oocytes, poorer embryo quality, and lower pregnancy rates per cycle (9.6% vs. 29.7%

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1909"></a>

صفحه 1909

in cyst-free cycles) (423). Ovarian cysts >20 mm that are seen on the baseline scan tend to resolve spontaneously within 1 to 2 months and OC administration does not appear to hasten their resolution (425). However, OC or progesterone/progestin pretreatment prior to GnRH agonist cycles is associated with decreased risk of cyst formation (426,427). Functional cyst aspiration prior to gonadotropin stimulation, which is commonly performed to expedite initiation of treatment, did not improve IVF outcomes in one large study (423). US-guided transvaginal cyst aspiration prior to IVF stimulation led to similar pregnancy outcomes as use of antagonist for cyst resolution, although prestimulation antagonist was associated with slightly longer duration and dose of stimulation (428).

Prevention of Premature Luteinizing Hormone Surge

LH Surge and Premature Luteinization

9 Without GnRH agonist or antagonist suppression (Fig. 36-5), LH surges occur in IVF cycles as a result of high E2 levels in the early follicular phase. This, in turn, results in a lower oocyte yield and reduced pregnancy rates (429). Spontaneous ovulation prior to oocyte retrieval is reported to occur in 16% of nonsuppressed IVF cycles (430). The premature LH rise typically occurs after 5 to 7 days of stimulation (429). 8 In contrast, premature luteinization is a somewhat misleading term that refers to a rise in serum progesterone (cutoffs vary from 0.8 to 2 ng/mL) observed on the day of hCG administration and occurs in the setting of low LH levels secondary to gonadotropin- suppressive medications (431). The incidence of premature luteinization has been estimated at 6% to 7% when the progesterone cut-off level is set at >1.5 ng/mL (432), but it may be as high as 35% (431). Mechanisms underlying premature luteinization may include incomplete pituitary desensitization, increased granulosa cell receptor sensitivity to LH secondary to aggressive COH or innately poor responders, and/or the presence of multiple follicles, each producing a normal amount of progesterone consistent with the late follicular phase (431,432). Although the literature on this subject remains inconsistent, the negative effects of premature luteinization (433) have been attributed to endometrial advancement rather than oocyte or embryo dysfunction (431,432). Methods proposed to address premature luteinization have included earlier triggering with hCG, less aggressive stimulation protocols, cryopreservation of embryos (freeze all) (432) and administration of the antiprogestin mifepristone at the time of hCG (434). Medroxyprogesterone acetate 10 mg orally daily begun either on day 1 or day 5 of gonadotropin stimulation was effective in preventing premature ovulation in a study of oocyte donors. However, when initiated in the presence of a large follicle, low-dose (5 to 10 mg) intramuscular progesterone triggers ovulation to occur (435,436).

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1910"></a>

صفحه 1910

Figure 36-5 In vitro fertilization protocols using gonadotropin-releasing hormone (GnRH) agonist or antagonist with gonadotropins in controlled ovarian hyperstimulation. hCG, human chorionic gonadotropin.

GnRH Agonists Native GnRH is rapidly degraded in the circulation (437). Commercial preparations of GnRH agonists are decapeptides like GnRH but for modification at two amino acid residues, which increase the half-life and receptor binding affinities (437). Over the course of 10 to 14 days, agonists initially bind and upregulate pituitary GnRH receptor activity, leading to a “flare response” of increased gonadotropin secretion. 8 This is followed by receptor desensitization (depleted gonadotropin pools along with rapid uncoupling of the GnRH receptor from its regulatory protein and loss of signal transduction) which suppresses circulating levels of pituitary gonadotropins and, with high doses and prolonged use, eventually decreases GnRH receptor numbers (415,429,437–439). Therefore, prolonged use

تصاویر استخراج‌شدهٔ همین صفحه

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1911"></a>

صفحه 1911

of GnRH agonists induces a menopause-like state characterized by low E2 levels and accompanied by common side effects such as hot flashes and moodiness (415). The flare effect may cause ovarian cyst formation as described in the “Unexplained Infertility” section of this Chapter (415). GnRH agonists are commercially available for either depot or daily use and can be administered intranasally (buserelin and nafarelin) or by intramuscular or subcutaneous injection (leuprolide, triptorelin, or buserelin). Intranasal preparations have lower absorption rates when compared to injectable agonists and are associated with milder suppression effects (440). Typical starting daily doses of leuprolide are 1 mg, 0.5 mg, or 25 mcg (microdose) (438).

GnRH Agonist Protocols Some of the more commonly used ART medications and protocols are summarized in Figure 36-5 and Table 36-6. 8 In the “long protocol,” a GnRH agonist is started in either the luteal phase (day 21) of the previous cycle or after approximately 21 days of OC suppression (441). Either approach diminishes the GnRH agonist’s flare effect and suppresses endogenous FSH and dominant follicle selection to promote synchronous follicular growth (429). After 10 to 14 days of GnRH agonist administration, a pelvic US and E2 level are used to confirm suppression and gonadotropin stimulation begins. The GnRH agonist is continued (the dose may be halved or unchanged) throughout the cycle until the hCG trigger (157,429). Mean serum progesterone levels on the day of hCG administration using the long protocol are reportedly 0.84 ng/mL (432). 8 The long protocol provides for better oocyte yields and pregnancy rates in normal responders when compared with shorter protocols that use later administration or early cessation of agonists (429). Shorter protocols using lower GnRH agonist doses have been advocated for poor responders in whom excessive suppression may be undesirable, perhaps because of direct negative effects of the agonist on the ovary (157). Long protocols, particularly those using single-dose depot rather than daily GnRH agonist formulations (442), are associated with higher gonadotropin dose and duration, which themselves have been associated with lower pregnancy rates (443,444). Alternatively, GnRH flare protocols have been developed that may improve oocyte yield in poor responders. Flare regimens involve pretreatment with 14 to 21 days of combination OCs. Four days following the cessation of the OC pills, a microdose (25-mcg leuprolide) or full daily dose (i.e., 0.5 mg to 1 mg or 10 to 20 units) of agonist is added in the early follicular phase to take advantage of the agonist flare effect. Gonadotropin stimulation is initiated 1 to 2 days later while continuing the agonist, or the agonist is discontinued after an interval and antagonists are used for subsequent suppression (438,445,446).

GnRH Antagonists GnRH antagonists (cetrorelix and ganirelix) were developed by modifying the GnRH decapeptide at six positions. They compete with endogenous GnRH for binding to pituitary GnRH receptors (437). 8 Because they have no agonistic activity, GnRH antagonists lead to almost immediate suppression of FSH and LH and do not require the additional time for pituitary downregulation that characterizes the GnRH agonists (437). With prolonged use, GnRH antagonists downregulate GnRH receptors (437). The only delivery method available for GnRH antagonist is subcutaneous injection, although orally active agents are in development (437). GnRH antagonists can be given as 0.25-mg daily doses or as a single 3-mg dose with no differences in outcomes (439). The time between daily injections should not exceed 30 hours

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1912"></a>

صفحه 1912

(397). With single-dose regimens, avoidance of multiple injections is attractive but additional small doses starting 4 days after the initial dose are required in 10% of cycles (439). Use of GnRH antagonists in ART protocols typically begun on days 4 to 7 of stimulation. 8 This timing balances the risk for premature LH surges with the need for initiation of endogenous FSH-mediated follicular recruitment and endogenous E2 production prior to administration (415,429,439).

GnRH Antagonists: Fixed Versus Flexible Protocols Several GnRH antagonist protocols have been developed for use in ART cycles. Fixed protocols involve starting the antagonist on days 4, 5, 6, or 7 of stimulation, regardless of follicular response (415,419,429,439). Flexible protocols were developed to reduce gonadotropin stimulation dose and duration (429). In flexible protocols, varying thresholds have been described for antagonist initiation. 8 These include addition of the antagonist when the leading follicle has reached 12 to 16 mm in average diameter or when the E2 level has risen above 600 pg/mL (416,418,429,438,439). When flexible protocols are used, pregnancy rates are similar when antagonist is initiated on days 4 or 5 of stimulation but drop significantly when antagonist is initiated after day 6. This suggests that rapid follicular growth may be more important in preventing LH surges and improving pregnancy rates than the day the antagonist is initiated (416). Fixed initiation of a GnRH antagonist on day 6 of stimulation has been associated with higher pregnancy rates when compared to flexible protocols in one meta-analysis involving four randomized trials; however, the true superiority of one approach over the other remains to be defined (416,438,439). Diminished E2 levels can be expected following GnRH antagonist administration, but this does not appear to affect follicular growth (419). 8 Dose increases in exogenous FSH (415,438) or LH (438,439) do not appear to be necessary.

GnRH Agonists Compared with Antagonists Because there is no pituitary desensitization period required when GnRH antagonists are used in ART, cycles can begin more quickly than those that employ GnRH agonists (415). Antagonists are associated with lower duration of stimulation, lower stimulation dose, and reduced rates of OHSS (447). Antagonists are often used without pretreatment with OCPs. Initiation of antagonist cycles relies on the start of spontaneous menses, often making scheduling easier for GnRH agonist cycles (448). Study and protocol heterogeneity make it difficult to detect consistent differences in IVF pregnancy outcomes after GnRH agonist and antagonist cycles (447,449). 9 A Cochrane review of 27 randomized controlled trials indicated that, while the number of good-quality transferred embryos produced are similar, clinical pregnancy rates are higher by 4.7% in agonist compared to antagonist cycles. They concluded that for every 21 couples, 1 additional pregnancy would be gained by using a GnRH agonist protocol (447). The number of oocytes retrieved, and live birth rates also favored agonist usage (447).

Follicular Growth and Stimulation Protocols

Follicular Recruitment Primordial follicles are 0.03 mm in size and reach the point of ovulation only after 150 days (five menstrual cycles), during which they grow into preantral (primary and secondary follicles), early antral, small antral, and large antral follicles (450,451). The resting follicle pool includes primordial and preantral follicles (451). Resting follicles continuously enter either growth or

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1913"></a>

صفحه 1913

apoptotic phases from birth until menopause, a process which is gonadotropin independent (451). After 90 days, activated resting follicles contain a fluid-filled antrum, marking their responsiveness to gonadotropins and are called early antral follicles of 0.2- to 0.5-mm size (451,452). Over the next 70 days, these early antral follicles grow into 2-mm small antral follicles, with this slow pace of growth mediated by AMH (451). Small antral follicles, size 2 to 5 mm, which are recruitable and gonadotropin dependent, begin to slowly enlarge in the late luteal phase of the cycle preceding ovulation. Concurrently, LH-induced theca cell androgen production increases (451,453). In the early follicular phase of a natural cycle, the follicle that will ultimately become dominant has a diameter of 5 to 8 mm (454). Growth of the follicle to 10 mm indicates selection, with the next largest follicle typically 8 mm (453,454). When the antral follicle grows beyond 10 mm, follicular AMH decreases and FSH induces the appearance of LH receptors on the granulosa cells (440). 1 At this point, somewhat contrary to the 2-cell-2- gonadotropin model, LH can exert FSH-like actions on the granulosa cell, including stimulation of aromatase with associated decreases in intrafollicular androgen and increases in intrafollicular E2 levels (440,451). With complete absence of endogenous LH, such as that seen with hypothalamic hypogonadism, exogenous FSH produces fewer preovulatory follicles, inadequate E2, lower ovulation rates, and a thinner endometrium when compared to ovaries stimulated with agonist downregulation and exogenous hMG (FSH and LH) (440). 6 Therefore, in the setting of normal hypothalamic function, it seems likely that even with suppressive therapy, enough endogenous LH is present to synergize with exogenous FSH to provide adequate stimulation for recruitment (440). Aromatase inhibitors such as letrozole have been proposed to improve follicular recruitment and subsequent egg yield via reducing estrogen’s negative feedback on endogenous gonadotropins but failed to show any benefit in a secondary analysis of a randomized controlled trial that used letrozole in cotreatment with gonadotropins during IVF (455). Androgen pretreatment such as testosterone was also posited to improve follicular recruitment and subsequent egg retrieval yield but showed no benefit for poor responders when given for 2 months prior to IVF ovarian stimulation in a multi-national double-blinded randomized placebo-controlled trial (456).

Follicular Waves and Timing of Stimulation The above referenced 2- to 5-mm small antral follicles develop synchronously, cyclically, in cohorts, or “waves,” during each menstrual cycle (450). The emergence of a wave is detectable ultrasonographically when the lead follicle reaches 4 to 6 mm, which occurs in association with at least two follicles reaching ≥6 mm (452). The wave is considered major (ovulatory) when over the next ∼2 days the lead follicle is ≥10 mm and exceeds other follicles by ≥2 mm, while in minor (anovulatory) waves the largest follicle is <10 mm and is similar in size to other follicles (452,454,457). In major waves of unstimulated cycles, the nonovulated follicles become atretic, while in minor waves all follicles in the cohort become atretic (457). Most unstimulated human menstrual cycles have two waves of follicular development (452,454). In two-wave cycles, the first wave is minor and has been variously described to occur the day before ovulation (452,454) or the day after ovulation (457), with progesterone elevations facilitating follicle atresia (457). 1 The second wave (in two-wave cycles) is major and occurs 14 to 15 days after ovulation (or the day after menses onset) (452,454,457). Of the 32% of menstrual cycles with three waves, most involved two minor waves occurring the day of ovulation and 12 to 13 days after ovulation (or 1 day prior to menses onset) and one major wave

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1914"></a>

صفحه 1914

occurring 17 days after ovulation (or 6 days after menses onset) (452,454,458). However, 19% of the three-wave cycles had three major waves occurring at each of the same time points (454). For simplification, day 1 of menses (if onset occurred in the evening, then day 1 is considered to be the next day) will likely correspond to the emergence of the ovulatory wave in women with two waves, while day 1 of menses will correspond to emergence of an anovulatory wave in women with three waves (450). Dual follicular and luteal stimulation and retrievals within the same menstrual cycle of poor responders demonstrates practical applications of follicle wave development, although the approach is not superior to conventional stimulation. The concept also lends itself to more flexibility in stimulation initiation timing for planned freeze-all cycles, which is especially useful for time-sensitive fertility preservation circumstances (459,460).

Synchronization of Follicle Growth A small, randomized trial in 2012 found that women who began stimulation on cycle day 1 of spontaneous menses had two additional follicles ≥15 mm at time of trigger compared to the group starting stimulation on cycle day 4; nonsignificant trends favoring the day 1 start were observed for cycle cancellation rates and number of total and mature oocytes retrieved (450). 8 During the hormone-free interval on combination OC pills, the dominant follicles emerged at the 4 to 5 mm diameter ∼3 to 5 days after pill cessation (461), providing support for the practice of starting gonadotropin stimulation at this time. These findings are intriguing and suggest a path toward greater synchrony during stimulation. Based upon findings in cows, one group hypothesized that, in women, aspirating the dominant follicle at ∼menstrual cycle days 6 to 7 should induce emergence of a new wave 2 days later, at which point gonadotropin stimulation could begin to precisely synchronize the follicle cohort (457). Pharmacologic induction of wave emergence has been proposed to include high-dose E2 (i.e., a single 8-mg estradiol valerate injection) followed immediately by 5 days of progesterone 100 mg daily given at any time of the cycle to induce luteolysis, suppress FSH, and regress the dominant follicle (457). Upon cessation of the progesterone, a new wave should begin with a serum FSH surge, at which point gonadotropin stimulation can start; the authors suggest that OCs would not affect wave dynamics, but a citation is not given (457). While a 1999 case report in humans suggested promise for follicle growth synchronization with dominant follicle aspiration, a larger study did not find significant differences in retrieved oocyte yield, though higher oocyte maturity was noted in the preretrieval aspirated group (458,462–464).

Monitoring and Timing of Trigger Monitoring during ART is helpful to detect the synchronous growth of dominant follicles, though practitioners vary in specific monitoring elements. 8 Survey respondents of consistently high-performing US IVF clinics all used both E2 and US follicle size for stimulation monitoring, and 40% to 50% additionally measured progesterone and LH during the stimulation, with monitoring performed four to six times during the stimulation (465). However, a Cochrane study in 2014 found that US monitoring alone without serum testing may be a reasonable approach (466).

In one report, women who were predicted to be normal responders using 225 to 300 IU of FSH required 11 days of stimulation, had 11 to 13 follicles ≥15 mm on the day of hCG trigger, had a peak E2 level of approximately 2,100 pg/mL, and had 10 to 11 oocytes retrieved, of which 82% to 83% were mature (176). 8 The optimal level of E2 at the time of hCG administration is between 70 and 140 pg/mL per oocyte or follicle (417). Oocytes have been recovered from

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1915"></a>

صفحه 1915

follicles with US mean diameters between 13 and 24 mm corresponding to fluid volumes of >1 mL and ≤7 mL, with optimal recovery rates for 3 mL (467). In a worldwide survey of IVF practices, of which 18% were in the United States, the majority administered trigger when 2 to 3 lead follicles (81% of respondents) reached an US mean diameter of 16 to 20 mm (92% of respondents) (468). High-performing US IVF clinics administer trigger after a mean of 9 to 10 days of stimulation, when at least two lead follicles reach mean diameter ≥18 mm, with that study reporting a range of 17 mm to >20 mm (465). However, it may be advantageous to trigger poor responders at <18 mm to reduce premature ovulation (469). Further, when letrozole is coadministered with gonadotropins, there were fewer mature eggs from follicles <17 mm than with gonadotropins alone (455). In freeze-all cycles, patient triggering in the setting of late- follicular phase progesterone elevations >2 ng/mL was not associated with impaired embryo development, aneuploidy, or lessened pregnancy rates as compared to lower progesterone levels (470). See the “Superovulation” section of “Unexplained Infertility” for additional information on gonadotropin stimulation (455).

Follicle-Stimulating Hormone Versus Human Menopausal Gonadotropin Concern has been raised that exogenous LH might be needed to address the sudden decrease in endogenous LH that is associated with GnRH antagonist use and the possible excess endogenous LH suppression in long GnRH agonist protocols (440). In a 2010 survey of high-performing US clinics, none chose an FSH-only protocol for young normal responders (465). An analysis of

21,000 IVF/ICSI cycles that used antagonist suppression performed in 15 Latin American countries found hMG and LH protocols were associated with fewer oocytes retrieved, while pregnancy and live birth rates as compared to FSH-only protocols were not statistically significantly different (471). 8 This suggests that less expensive FSH-only protocols may be reasonable in some women (471). The authors did acknowledge, however, that the hMG and LH treated women were older and had higher rates of ovarian insufficiency than the FSH-only patients (471), so individualizing protocols given extremes of age and ovarian response is appropriate. In support of individualized protocols is a study showing hMG use in women with elevated AMH >5.2 ng/mL is associated in agonist and antagonist protocols with fewer instances of retrieving >15 oocytes and higher fresh transfer live birth rates as compared to FSH only, although OHSS rates were similar (472). Higher androgen and lower progesterone levels on the day of hCG trigger are observed when hMG is used for stimulation rather than FSH alone, indicating a more favorable endocrine profile with hMG (472,473). The ratio of hMG to FSH must be evaluated cumulatively over the entire stimulation rather than just at the beginning, as there appears to be a “sweet spot” of a ratio of 0.3 to 0.6 which has been shown to be predictive of the optimal live birth rate (474).

Stimulation Dose and Optimal Number of Retrieved Oocytes Concerns have been raised about premature luteinization and lower pregnancy rates with higher- dose FSH stimulation protocols (432); each additional 100 IU of gonadotropins has been associated with a 2% lower rate of clinical pregnancy and live birth (443), and 300 units daily FSH did not increase number of retrieved oocytes or live birth rate compared to 225 units in a randomized controlled trial of “normal responders” (475). A possible explanation stems from comparative evaluations of unstimulated cycles in which the dominant follicle contains higher follicular E2, androgen, AMH, and LH concentrations compared to those measured in stimulated dominant follicles and may indicate follicle quality is inversely proportional to quantity

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1916"></a>

صفحه 1916

(476,477). Reduced-dose stimulation to obtain <8 or ≤4 oocytes using clomiphene citrate 50 to 100 mg for 5 days (or more extended durations through the day of trigger) along with gonadotropin doses of 75 to 150 units ranging from a few days to longer duration have been described (477–479). A meta-analysis of >300 minimal stimulation cycles found that a median of five retrieved oocytes was optimal for pregnancy and live birth rates (444). These protocols are less expensive and multiple cycles may be noninferior in terms of live birth rates to higher-dose protocols (480). A Cochrane review found that 150 units daily of FSH, that is, a one-size fits all approach, yielded responses that were appropriate for expectations based on ovarian reserve markers; an individualized approach did not yield differences (481). However, one of the same authors from the Cochrane review did make a distinction that patients greater than 30 years old with an AFC <4 may enjoy a higher cumulative live birth rate using a higher FSH dose (482,483).

Higher-stimulation doses are understood to be a function of patient characteristics (genetically determined ovarian sensitivity to gonadotropins or age-related aneuploidy) rather than exerting a direct adverse effect on IVF outcome and a more nuanced portrait of different ovarian reserve categories is needed to draw conclusions between dose and outcome (484). 8 Other publications provide compelling support for protocols that, regardless of responder status, aim for increased number of retrieved oocytes in a single retrieval cycle and has led some to disavow previous rationales for minimal stimulation (477,485). Sixty percent of high-performing US IVF centers in 2010 reported that for a young normal responder with healthy BMI they would use a starting gonadotropin dose of 300 units per day (465). Exceeding a total daily gonadotropin dose of 450 IU produces mixed results, ranging from no benefit to higher egg yield or improved live birth rate with regimens such as split-dosing 300 units twice-daily FSH or a mix of 450 units of FSH plus 150 units of hMG (415,486,487). A previous trial indicating no benefit with higher gonadotropin doses was notable for nonsignificant trends toward higher age, higher BMI, lower AMH, and lower antral count in the high FSH dose compared to the lower-dose group (475). A randomized trial of minimal stimulation versus conventional higher-dose stimulation in 564 first cycles with women age <39 found that while minimal stimulation was associated with 49% cumulative (over 6 months) live birth rates, conventional stimulation live birth rates were significantly higher at 63% (479). In fresh oocyte donor cycles, recipient live birth rates were maximized when >10 oocytes were retrieved (488); each 3.4 mature (M2) oocytes was associated with 1 euploid embryo (489). Among oocyte donors, embryo aneuploidy rates increased with decreased ovarian sensitivity (i.e., gonadotropin doses per oocyte increased) but increased overall gonadotropin doses were not associated with aneuploidy (489). In autologous cycles, 15 to 20 oocytes were optimal for fresh transfer live birth (490,491). 8 Higher numbers of oocytes 15 to 25 were associated with more usable blastocysts and >1 live birth from a single autologous IVF cycle and each additional oocyte was associated with an 8% increase in the chance for >1 live births (490). The likelihood of a single autologous retrieval leading to live birth over subsequent fresh and frozen/thaw transfers was highest with >15 retrieved oocytes in a randomized trial of >1,000 women allocated to either antagonist or long agonist suppression (492). A second retrospective paper reporting on >1,000 women who underwent a single retrieval cycle similarly found that

15 oocytes retrieved was associated with the highest cumulative live birth rates with single embryo transfer compared with fewer numbers of oocytes (493). In >400,000 autologous IVF cycles, increased number of oocytes >10 was associated with a 3% lower miscarriage rate

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1917"></a>

صفحه 1917

compared to cycles with <4 oocytes, even after adjusting for age (494). In poor responders of mean age 38 with AFC 5 to 6 and AMH 0.7 ng/mL, those randomized to high gonadotropin doses had nearly 10% lower cycle cancellation rates, 8% lower rates of no oocytes retrieved, two additional mature oocytes retrieved (4.8 vs. 2.7), more embryos available for transfer (2.7 vs. 1.8), and a nonsignificant trend to higher pregnancy rate per initiated cycle (478). Oocyte donors in whom ≥17 oocytes were obtained had 2 to 3 additional euploid embryos per retrieval cycle compared to cycles with <17 oocytes, and aneuploidy rate did not increase with gonadotropin dose (489). Although minimal stimulation protocols, by design, are simpler with less up- front medication and freezing services that may contribute to cost, these should be weighed against the advantages of protocols that aim for safely maximizing per-cycle live birth efficiency (485). However, all these protocols are hampered by the same challenge, the immutable inefficiency of live birth rate per oocyte (11.3% for age <35 years), which is exacerbated by increasing age (1.2% for age >42 years) (495).

Pretreatment

9 Combined OCs are commonly taken for 14 to 28 days prior to GnRH analogs to ease cycle scheduling (448), synchronize follicular development (496), further prevent LH surges (496), reduce the incidence of ovarian cysts (496), and reduce cancellation rates caused by hyperstimulation (497). Patients can begin OCs anytime between days 1 and 5 of menses (496). For antagonist cycles, COH begins 2 to 5 days after OCs (irrespective of menses) (448). During long GnRH agonist protocols, the agonist overlaps the final 5 days of OC use, followed by initiation of COH on the second or third day of withdrawal bleeding (426,497). Microdose flare protocols involve pretreatment with 14 to 21 days of OC use, followed 4 days later by a microdose of agonist. COH typically starts 1 to 2 days later (438). The progestins norethindrone acetate 10 mg orally daily, medroxyprogesterone acetate 10 mg orally daily, or a single intramuscular dose of progesterone (not specified) can be used in place of an OC in ART cycles. Recommended duration and the timing of treatment initiation vary widely. A 5- to 20-day duration and initiation between days 1 and 19 of menses have been reported (496). Four milligrams of daily micronized 17β E2 or E2 valerate has been used in lieu of OCs in ART cycles, with initiation between cycle days 15 and 21 and a duration of 10 to 15 days (496). OC pretreatment for antagonist cycles has been shown to increase the duration and number of medication doses during stimulation. There is “moderate quality” evidence from a recent Cochrane review for reduced ongoing pregnancy and live birth rates with a possible improvement in pregnancy loss rates with OC pretreatment in antagonist cycles (427). However, OC pretreatment during GnRH agonist cycles is associated with higher pregnancy rates than those in cycles without pretreatment (426). Progestin and E2 pretreatment do not conclusively affect live birth rates in either agonist or antagonist cycles (427). They may be useful to synchronize stimulated follicle growth (see “Follicular Growth and Stimulation Protocols”).

Adjunctive Medications Prenatal vitamins should be given to all infertility patients beginning at least 1 month prior to initiation of infertility treatment. Low-dose aspirin 80 to 100 mg daily commencing at downregulation and given for variable durations (up to 13 weeks’ gestation or if recurrent loss to 38 weeks) is commonly used during IVF regimens to improve ovarian stimulation response and endometrial implantation. In some recurrent loss patients (those with antiphospholipid syndrome), low-dose aspirin is given with heparin (498). A 2016 Cochrane review of “low to

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1918"></a>

صفحه 1918

moderate quality” RCT evidence comprising >2,600 patients found no evidence of benefit for routine use of aspirin in IVF in terms of pregnancy, miscarriage, or live birth (499). Aspirin use confers a fourfold increased risk (50% vs. 13.6% in nonusers) of first trimester subchorionic hemorrhage in non-IVF and IVF pregnancies, which was not observed with heparin use (498). These findings prompted the authors to discontinue the use of aspirin in routine infertility and IVF patients, reserving it for antiphospholipid syndrome and some recurrent loss patients, although there is no support for its routine use in RPL patients without the antiphospholipid syndrome. These authors will discontinue the aspirin if subchorionic bleeding is detected (498). Glucocorticoids given to women during the peri-implantation period may improve pregnancy rates in women undergoing IVF (rather than ICSI). A Cochrane study found this result of marginal statistical significance and did not observe improvement in terms of pregnancy or live birth rates for glucocorticoids given to an overall sample of 1,800 patients. More study is needed before recommendations to specific subgroups (i.e., autoantibodies, unexplained infertility, or recurrent implantation failure [RIF]) (500). In 2015, one group discontinued their routine use of oral doses of methylprednisolone (16 mg daily) and doxycycline (100 mg twice daily) for 4 days commencing either on the day of oocyte retrieval or 4 days prior to frozen ET, because they did not find that these medications to be statistically beneficial in terms of pregnancy, miscarriage, or live birth rates, regardless of whether ICSI and assisted hatching were performed (501). Metformin may limit OHSS in PCOS patients (502). An updated Cochrane review found that metformin improved clinical pregnancy rates with IVF but showed no conclusive benefit for live birth rates (503).

Physiology of Oocyte Maturation Prior to maturation, oocytes are arrested in the prophase stage of meiosis I, also known as the germinal vesicle stage (504,505). Meiosis I oocytes must reach at least the early antral follicle stage to respond to FSH and be competent to resume meiosis (506). In vivo, LH receptors on the follicle are induced by FSH during later stages of follicular development (506). Therefore, only fully grown oocytes respond to the LH surge in vivo to begin the cytoplasmic and nuclear maturation that are required for developmental progression toward the metaphase stage of meiosis II. At this point, the developmentally competent oocyte will extrude the first polar body, the oocyte–cumulus complex will detach from the ovarian wall (507), ovulation will occur, and fertilization is possible (504–506).

Trigger During ART Cycles Because spontaneous LH surges occur inconsistently during non-ART gonadotropin cycles and are suppressed in ART cycles, hCG has been used to trigger ovulation. In combination with its long half-life, the homology between hCG and LH (identical α subunits) permits cross-reactivity with the LH receptor and induction of final oocyte maturation and ovulation (508). 8 hCG is derived from urine (5,000 to 10,000 IU intramuscularly) or through recombinant technology (250 μg subcutaneously, equivalent to 5,000 to 6,000 IU of intramuscular urinary product) (252). The half-life of hCG is 2.32 days, compared to 1 to 5 hours for LH (508). Ovulation is typically triggered when at least 2 follicles are ≥17 to 18 mm in average diameter (but <24 mm) and the endometrial thickness is ≥8 mm (176,411,416–419,509). Similar clinical outcomes have been noted when 5,000 IU or 10,000 IU of urine-derived hCG (510) and urinary or recombinant preparations (509,511), are used for triggering. If there is concern for OHSS, GnRH agonists can substitute for hCG to trigger

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1919"></a>

صفحه 1919

ovulation in antagonist protocols. The combination of hCG and GnRH agonist (dual trigger) led to superior egg yield, embryo development, pregnancy, and live birth rates in a randomized double-blinded trial with hCG-only trigger as the control. A meta-analysis did not find a difference in live birth rates (512).

Oocyte Retrieval Oocyte retrieval is performed via transvaginal US–guided needle puncture into each follicle and aspiration of follicular fluid. Either general anesthesia or intravenous conscious sedation may be used (513). Prophylactic antibiotics such as ceftriaxone are recommended at the time of retrieval (514). The vaginal prep can be performed either with sterile saline alone or with povidone-iodine and vigorous saline flushing (515,516). The highest oocyte yield is obtained when oocyte retrieval is performed 36 to 37 hours after the hCG injection (509). Earlier retrieval (35 hours) is associated with a much lower oocyte yield, and later retrieval risks ovulation. Spontaneous follicular rupture appears to occur at a mean of 38.3 hours following hCG administration (509). However, a meta-analysis found a higher live birth rate at a trigger to retrieval interval closer to 38 hours (517).

Luteal Support The rationale and regimens for luteal phase support with progesterone are discussed in the “Uterine Factor” section. Luteal phase E2 supplementation is not necessary for fresh embryo transfers unless GnRH agonist trigger is utilized (366,518,519). In the case of GnRH agonist trigger, E2 0.1-mg patches starting 1-day postretrieval, coadministered with intramuscular progesterone, has been proposed to offset corpus luteum dysfunction (366). 8 Luteal support with low-dose hCG (1,000 to 1,500 units) given at egg retrieval and again up to 5 days postretrieval has been used successfully (366).

Fertilization by IVF or ICSI Following semen collection and sperm processing (described in the “Male Factor” section), sperm are incubated in media for 3 to 4 hours to promote sperm capacitation and the acrosome reaction. Before fertilization, retrieved oocytes are cultured in media. Conventional IVF involves insemination concentrations of 100,000 to 800,000 motile sperm/mL per oocyte with each oocyte in a small droplet of media under oil (15,520,521). 8 For every three cycles done for severe male factor, the use of ICSI prevents one case of fertilization failure when compared to conventional IVF (15). A retrospective study found higher rates of egg maturity and normal fertilization when egg denudation and ICSI were delayed to 41 hours or longer following trigger administration as compared to performing these tasks after 36 to 38 hours (522). The indications for, procedure, and risks of ICSI are discussed under “Male Factor.”

In Vitro Embryo Culture

Embryo Development Initial embryo development is typically assessed 15 to 20 hours after insemination or ICSI, when fertilization is characterized by the presence of two pronuclei and the extrusion of the second polar body (15,521,523). 8 Embryos are examined again for cleavage after 24 to 30 hours of culture (15). The first embryo cleavage occurs approximately 21 hours after fertilization, and subsequent divisions occur every 12 to 15 hours up to the 8-cell stage on the 3rd day of embryo development (524). Compaction to form the 16-cell morula occurs on the 4th day of embryo development, and differentiation of the inner cell mass and trophectoderm to form a

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1920"></a>

صفحه 1920

blastocyst (containing a fluid-filled area called a blastocoel) is completed by the 5th to 6th days (525,526).

Culture Environment Prior to compaction, while the embryo is under genetic control of the oocyte, it uses a pyruvate- based metabolism. At this point, the embryo requires at least a few amino acids and prefers a relatively oxygenated environment (though much lower than atmospheric oxygen) similar to that found in the fallopian tube (524,527). After compaction, glucose and amino acid needs increase, the embryonic genome is activated, and metabolism requires a very low oxygen environment similar to that found in the uterus (524,527). Supplementation of culture media with hyaluron and albumin is beneficial in the postcompaction period (524). Culture media systems are classified as either single or sequential. Single culture media was developed to provide all the necessary nutrients required (day 1 through day 5/6) in a single media formulation, reducing stress on the embryo by keeping a stable environment and allowing any embryo-generated autocrine/paracrine factors to remain (528). Single culture media uses stable dipeptide glutamine to avoid toxic ammonium buildup from the breakdown of amino acids (528). Sequential culture media systems were designed to mimic the changing metabolic and nutritional requirements of the developing embryo by culturing embryos in media designed for the cleavage-stage embryo (days 1 to 3) and, on day 3, switching to culture media designed to support blastocyst development. Although both culture systems have advantages, there is insufficient evidence to recommend one over the other, particularly as to whether any benefit of increased blastocyst development translates into improved pregnancy or live birth rates (528). Regarding obstetric outcomes, single-step culture increased the odds of large for gestational age babies but did not differ from sequential culture in terms of abnormal placentation, preeclampsia, gestational diabetes, or prematurity. Culturing embryos together as a group is advantageous in terms of blastocyst formation when compared to individual culture (529,530).

Extended Culture to Blastocyst Although precompaction human embryos can survive when placed in the uterus, the uterine cavity is a nonphysiologic location for them and there is greater uterine pulsatility during this period that may cause the embryos to be expelled (531). Therefore, the blastocyst stage represents a more physiologic time for embryo transfer (531). Because nearly 60% of morphologically normal cleavage embryos, but only 30% of blastocysts, are chromosomally abnormal, extended culture allows for better selection of embryos with improved quality (531,532). Notably, if a patient is a carrier of a balanced translocation, nearly 80% of cleavage embryos and 60% of blastocysts may be chromosomally abnormal (533). The euploidy rate of blastocysts is higher when they have completed development by postretrieval day 5 compared to day 6 or day 7. Euploid day 7 blastocysts have the potential for healthy implantation, though with lower prognosis than day 5/6 blastocysts (534,535).

Blastocyst Versus Cleavage Transfer Outcomes Comparisons involving equal numbers of transferred embryos demonstrate that fresh blastocyst transfer is associated with up to 10% higher pregnancy rate and up to 13% higher live birth rate than cleavage-stage transfer, which can be helpful information if considering elective single embryo transfer (eSET). Drawbacks to extended culture include

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1921"></a>

صفحه 1921

the small possibility that no embryos will survive to fresh blastocyst transfer and a 23% reduction in women having additional embryos for cryopreservation. However, ovarian reserve with AMH >1 ng/mL strongly positively influences the presence of supernumerary blastocysts available for cryopreservation. Monozygotic twinning rates may be higher with blastocyst culture, although this has not been a consistent finding. In oocyte recipients, a randomized control trial found that blastocyst transfer provided higher clinical pregnancy and live birth rates, a shorter time to pregnancy and reduced costs compared to cleavage stage transfer. Although cycle cancelation for failed embryo development is higher in poor responders, their blastocysts nevertheless outperform cleavage embryo transfer pregnancy and implantation rates, thus providing a rationale to offer blastocyst culture to all responders (536,537).

Criteria for Extended Culture There are no established guidelines or criteria that determine when to utilize extended culture (538). Varying suggestions include: maternal age ≤42 with ≥five 2-pronuclear (2PN) stage embryos on postretrieval day 1; maternal age of ≤40 and ≥three good-quality day 3 embryos having 4 to 10 cells with <15% fragmentation; maternal age of ≤41 to 42 and ≥four good-quality day 3 embryos having 4 to 10 cells with <15% fragmentation; and age <37 with ≥4 morphologically good embryos on day 3 or ≥4 embryos with 6 cells and <10% fragmentation (525,526,538). Extended culture of poor-quality cleavage embryos improves prognostic information for transfer; if they develop into good-quality blastocysts, there is favorable pregnancy and live birth prognosis; persistent poor quality at the blastocyst stage portends a higher miscarriage rate (539).

Embryo Transfer

Embryo Morphology

8 Embryo morphology guides the choice of embryo for transfer. Pronuclear embryos are assessed by their distribution and number of nucleoli, the position of the second polar body relative to the first, and cleavage rates (abnormal rates are too fast, too slow, or arrested) (526). Preferred cleavage-stage embryos have a normal developmental pattern characterized by early cleavage on day 1, 4 cells on day 2, and 8 cells on day 3. Embryo fragmentation should be ≤10%, the blastomere size should be regular and there should be no multinucleation (523). The Gardner and Schoolcraft system for scoring blastocysts uses a scale from 1 (worst) to 6 (best), with grades 1 to 3 indicating growth of the blastocele until it completely fills the embryo. Grade 4 blastocysts are expanded with a larger blastocele volume and a thinning zona pellucida. The trophectoderm in a grade 5 blastocyst is starting to hatch through the zona, and the grade 6 blastocyst has completely escaped or hatched from it (521). The inner cell mass is graded A–C based on tightness and cellularity (A is best), and the trophectoderm is assessed from A to C based upon cohesiveness and cellularity (A is best) (521). Conventional embryo morphology grading remains the most common methodology in choosing embryos to transfer but it has limitations. To assess the development and morphology of embryos, handling of the embryos and removing them from the controlled environment of the incubator is required. This exposure leads to differences in oxygen levels, temperature and pH, all of which may have adverse effects on the development of the embryos. Understandably, conventional morphology assessment is limited to specific points in time to avoid repeated exposure to the atmospheric environment (540). Time-lapse imaging has been developed by installing a camera in the incubator to allow

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1922"></a>

صفحه 1922

for continuous undisturbed evaluation of the embryos. In addition to the ability to assess morphology at multiple time points, morphokinetic monitoring, which assesses the rate at which embryos reach developmental events, can be accomplished with time-lapse imaging. Slower developing embryos for various cleavage and blastulating morphologic milestones show a higher association with aneuploidy; while not a replacement for direct ploidy testing, this may be helpful for noninvasive prioritization of embryos for transfer. Prior results of randomized studies comparing clinical outcomes of time-lapse monitoring versus conventional morphology have been mixed (540–543). Some investigators report higher numbers of day 5 blastocysts and blasts suitable for cryopreservation, and increased numbers of euploid blasts using time-lapse incubation as compared to standard incubation (544). Although there appears to be a potential benefit to time-lapse imaging, more high-quality, well-designed, randomized controlled trials need to be performed before any definitive conclusions can be drawn (540–542,545). A Swedish population registry study found no association of perinatal or maternal IVF outcomes between time-lapse incubation and conventional embryo incubation (546).

Number of Embryos to Transfer Multiple gestation pregnancies increase complications for mothers and fetuses, so guidelines have been developed to minimize this adverse outcome (547). 9 Single embryo transfer should be the standard if an embryo is euploid, regardless of the age of the patient or stage of the embryo (547). Single embryo transfer is recommended for patients less than age 38 undergoing a fresh cycle if they have had a prior live birth or there is an expectation that at least one high-quality embryo will be available for cryopreservation (547). Patients less than age 38 undergoing frozen embryo transfer (FET) should have a single embryo transferred if vitrified blastocysts are available, if it is the patient’s first FET, or if the patient had a previous live birth (547). In all other cases, transfer of embryos should be limited to two embryos in women under the age of 35. In older women, the maximum number of transferred cleavage-stage embryos should be: 3 in women aged 35 to 37, 4 in women aged 38 to 40, and 5 in women >40 years of age (547). Because of their high implantation potential, no more than three blastocysts should be transferred to any woman regardless of her age (547). 9 Limits on the number of embryos transferred when the embryos were created from donor oocytes should be based on the age of the donor, rather than the recipient (547). These recommendations were affirmed by the ASRM and the Society for Assisted Reproductive Technology in 2022. Double embryo transfers of embryos with differing quality grading did not negatively impact live birth rate, though the multiple gestation rate was higher than with single embryo transfer (548,549).

Transfer Procedure

8 The goal of transcervical embryo transfer is to atraumatically deliver the embryos to an optimal intrauterine location for implantation. Prior to performing the embryo transfer, the cervix and vagina are cleansed using media or saline. The removal of remaining cervical mucus appears to be beneficial in improving live birth and clinical pregnancy rates (550). Embryo transfer can be done using several transcervical techniques. Direct transfer is performed by loading the transfer catheter with embryo(s) and transferring immediately without a trial transfer. Trial transfer permits assessment of the cervical canal prior to performing the actual transfer. Trial transfer can be combined with an afterload technique if the trial transfer is difficult. In the afterload technique, the outer sheath of the trial transfer catheter is left in place while removing the inner catheter and a new inner transfer catheter loaded with the embryo(s) is threaded through

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1923"></a>

صفحه 1923

the trial catheter sheath into the uterus. Afterload technique can be planned and utilized intentionally without a trial transfer. Transabdominal ultrasound should be utilized to assess the endometrial cavity and other pelvic structures prior to and during the transfer to assure deposition of the embryo(s) in the upper or middle area of the uterine cavity, but no closer than 1 cm from the fundus (550). Vaginal ultrasound guidance has been found to have embryo transfer outcomes that equal those using abdominal ultrasound imaging (551). If ultrasound guidance is unavailable, pretransfer uterine length measurement has good outcomes as well (552). 8 Implantation is more likely when using a soft catheter and when fundal contact is avoided (550). When the embryos are deposited, the entire catheter should be removed immediately, as there does not appear to be any benefit in delaying catheter withdrawal (550). After the transfer, the catheter is checked for retained embryos. If present, retained embryos should be immediately retransferred, as there is no detriment in pregnancy rates (550). When the embryo transfer is completed, the patient may leave immediately without a period of bed rest (550). Although intrauterine infections decrease pregnancy rates, the efficacy of antibiotic administration at the time of transfer is not supported (550).

Cryopreservation of Embryos Embryo cryopreservation at the pronuclear, cleavage, and blastocyst stages has permitted multiple transfer cycles from a single oocyte retrieval. Because transfer of cryopreserved embryos is less expensive than a second fresh cycle, overall fertility treatment costs can be optimized. Techniques for embryo cryopreservation include slow freezing and rapid freezing or vitrification. Slow freezing protocols use lower concentrations of cryoprotectants but are more time-consuming when compared to vitrification, which uses high concentration cryoprotectants for rapid cooling and is less expensive (553). Embryo thawing is accomplished by brief exposure to air and warm water followed by rehydration (554). Although pregnancy rates for FET cycles using the two cryopreservation methods are similar, vitrification is associated with higher post- thaw embryo survival (93% vs. 76% with slow freezing) (553). 8 Favorable post-thaw embryo survival rates after vitrification have contributed to the increasing success of FET, with live birth rates that are now no different than fresh embryo transfer (555). The success of FET has led to a growing trend toward freezing entire embryo cohorts for transfer later. This strategy has the added advantage of greatly reducing the risk of OHSS with reassuring perinatal and obstetric outcomes (556). Although some studies report a favorable live birth rate per transfer when using a freeze-all strategy compared with fresh transfer, cumulative birth rates have not been shown to be different (557). Particularly in PCOS patients, a freeze-all strategy with subsequent warmed embryo transfer improved live birth rates compared to fresh transfer, though an increase in preeclampsia and singleton infant birth weight were noted. Well-designed randomized controlled trials are needed before recommending routine use of a freeze-all strategy (557).

Endometrial Preparation for Frozen Embryo Transfer When FET is combined with a recipient’s natural cycle, no exogenous treatment is given and transfer is timed to spontaneous ovulation, performed 7 days following detection of the serum LH surge (360); alternatively, transfer can be performed 160 hours ± 4 hours from hCG trigger or LH surge (558). Natural cycle FETs are associated with a reduced incidence of preeclampsia compared to medicated FETs, perhaps mediated through protective factors secreted by the corpus luteum (559). One study found that modified natural cycles that included hCG triggering

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1924"></a>

صفحه 1924

produced higher live birth rates compared to unmedicated natural cycles (558). In medicated FET cycles, E2 supplementation begins in the early follicular phase and is continued for up to 13 to 15 days prior to initiating progesterone (554). Multiple E2 preparations have been described for use in FET cycles, but none have been proven superior (560). Transvaginal US is used to assess endometrial thickness during estrogen therapy and estrogen administration continues until an optimal thickness of ≥6.5 to 8 mm is reached (360,554). Progesterone supplementation begins 48 to 72 hours prior to transfer when cleavage-stage embryos are used and 6 to 7 days prior to transfer when blastocysts will be thawed (554,560), unless different progesterone timing is indicated (360). Several progesterone preparations have been described for use in FET cycles, but none have been proven superior (“Uterine Factor”) (560). Medicated FET cycles offer control and flexibility of transfer timing while maintaining low cancellation rates; however, adequate pituitary suppression may not always be achieved (561). 8 A GnRH agonist may be used with medicated FET cycles to prevent premature LH surges that may adversely affect endometrial maturation (561). GnRH antagonists may be used for the same purpose and are noninferior for FET outcomes as compared to GnRH agonists (562). Coordination between the embryo and endometrium is critical to successful implantation (360). For some women, this remains elusive and results in RIF, defined as failure of at least three IVF cycles in which one or two morphologically high-grade embryos are transferred (360). Prior studies of the transcriptome of the endometrium have shown the ability to accurately catalog the endometrium in all phases of the menstrual cycle and have challenged the assumption that the window in which the endometrium is receptive to embryo implantation is universal in all women (360). 8 The Endometrial Receptivity Array (ERA©, Igenomix) has been developed to address the optimal window of implantation in patients with RIF using a molecular diagnostic tool (360). While the initial reproductive outcomes after applying the ERA© in patients with RIF were promising, (563), several studies now suggest a lack of benefit when using the ERA for most patients and, as such, this test may have limited utility outside of specific or outlier cases (564,565).

First Trimester Pregnancy Monitoring HCG, including the α subunit, can be detected in spent culture media from the 2PN stage onward. The production of hCG by the blastocyst can be detected in the serum as early as 7 days post-transfer, and serum quantitative hCG levels may be obtained 11 to 14 days following embryo transfer (554,566). 8 A serum threshold of 200 mIU/mL of hCG measured 12 days after transfer is 92% and 80% predictive of ongoing pregnancies for day 3 and day 5 embryos, respectively, with levels normally rising approximately 40% per day (566). If normally rising hCG levels are detected, transvaginal US is planned at 6 to 7 weeks of gestation to determine the location of the pregnancy, the number of gestational sacs, and pregnancy viability (566). 10 In those at high risk for ectopic pregnancy, early diagnosis using serial hCG and US examinations are important to reduce the risk of a ruptured ectopic pregnancy and to improve the success of medical management (567). These patients may benefit from transvaginal US at 5.5 to 6 weeks of gestation as a gestational sac should become visible in a normal pregnancy during this time (567). When concern arises as to pregnancy viability, serial US findings of growth arrest with fetal pole length <20 mm at time of fetal demise correlate with aneuploidy of conceptus.

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1925"></a>

صفحه 1925

ART SUCCESS RATES IVF can be a highly effective treatment for many patients, but fewer than 150,000 ovarian stimulation cycles with oocyte retrievals were performed in 2020 in the United States (568). Low IVF utilization rates are thought to be a result of the high cost of IVF and low retention rates among patients after an unsuccessful first IVF cycle. A patient’s decision to pursue medical treatment for infertility is often impacted by personal, financial, and medical factors. The provider’s role is to establish the clinical diagnoses, discuss the risks and benefits of various treatment options, and recommend treatment. If the treatment options include COH and IVF (such as in unexplained infertility), providers would typically counsel the patients about the success rates of IUI (with clomid or gonadotropins) and IVF in the context of the patient’s clinical data and diagnoses. Patients who are paying out-of-pocket for IVF treatment can benefit from a thorough discussion about the cost of each treatment option in the context of the per-cycle cost and the number of treatments that may be needed for each option. 8 Because the latter is directly related to the patient’s per-cycle treatment success, understanding the relationship between the personalized IVF success probability and potential cost of IVF can help patients plan and maximize their chances of having a live birth (baby). Public reporting of each IVF center’s success rates has been available for more than two decades. There have been significant efforts to incorporate predictive modeling and technology to provide patient-centric probabilities of IVF success based on each patient’s own health data.

National IVF Outcomes Registry Various national, multinational, and regional IVF outcomes registries have been established (Table 36-8). They share certain objectives, such as achieving conformity in the reporting of clinical outcomes to allow for quality-of-care assessments, providing anonymized data to public and private stakeholders for research, and facilitating public health and safety monitoring compliance (569). Examples include the Society for Assisted Reproductive Technology Clinical Outcomes Reporting System (SART-CORS) in the United States; registries from 40 countries aggregated by The European IVF Monitoring Consortium (EIM) for the European Society of Human Reproduction and Embryology (ESHRE) in the EU (568,570); the Human Fertilisation and Embryology Authority (HFEA) in the United Kingdom (571); the Canadian Assisted Reproductive Technologies Register (CARTR) Plus Registry in Canada (572); and the International Committee Monitoring Assisted Reproductive Technologies (ICMART), a nonstate actor in official relations with the WHO, with data reporting from fertility centers from 79 countries (573,574).

Table 36-8 Live Birth Rates from IVF Cycles Using Patient’s Own Eggs and Uterus

Founded in 1985 in the United States, SART collects, verifies, and reports clinical data and

تصاویر استخراج‌شدهٔ همین صفحه

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1926"></a>

صفحه 1926

outcomes pertaining to IVF cycles performed by more than 450 fertility centers to the Center for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC) annually, in compliance with the Fertility Clinic Success Rate and Certification Act of 1992 (Public Law 102–493) (569,571,576). SART has been reporting, since 2014, the parameter of cumulative live birth rate, which reflects the chance of achieving a live birth after a fresh or FET within a year of cycle initiated for egg retrieval (578).

11 This allows tracking of individual outcomes over time, accounting for fresh and frozen transfers stemming from the retrieval (578).

The SART CORS database has been the data source for 189 peer-reviewed publications from 2004 to 2021 and 206 conference abstracts from 2015 to 2021 (569,577). On the public health front, SART CORS data provided key statistics on IVF-related live birth and multiple birth rates related to the use of eSET, which together with improved efficacy of embryo vitrification and increased insurance coverage in some states, helped clinicians to achieve the desired clinical outcome of healthy singleton live births from IVF (549,579–581). SART reporting shaped ASRM Committee Opinions and guidance on the limits to the number of embryos to transfer. SART reporting showed that, in 2017, of women under 38 years of age with IVF live birth, only 12.4% had a twin gestation, which was decreased from 23% in 2014 (547,582). Among patients under 35 years of age with IVF live births, the high-order birth rates have also decreased from 6.4% (2003) to 0.2% (2020) (575). The SART CORS database has continued to evolve as fertility care expands to serve women pursuing oocyte cryopreservation for age-, cancer- or other disease-related fertility preservation, same-sex couples, and couples using IVF with their own or donor oocytes for clinical infertility. Like other health care areas, at the time of writing, significant efforts and new strategies are still needed for SART CORS to better inform our understanding of social determinants and race-related disparities in IVF live birth outcomes. Specifically, African American women have the lowest IVF live birth rates (577). Research on racial disparities is also challenged by limited data entry rates of race and ethnicity at many centers with unclear race/ethnicity reporting method (self- or provider-reported) (569,577).

Despite the important role of SART/CDC in maintaining treatment quality and public health reporting, its impact on provider–patient counseling and medical decision-making is subject to a wide range of perceptions among peer providers (583). SART asks that its reports not be used to compare IVF centers, presumably because success rates can be impacted by patient demographics and clinical attributes that vary among IVF centers.

Prediction Models Personalize IVF Success The ability to provide highly accurate and personalized probabilities of IVF success benefits the individual patient and gives providers insight about prognostic groups and profiles in their patient population. IVF success prediction models are not new, but the use of machine learning to develop and validate prediction models has dramatically improved model performance as defined by prediction accuracy, the ability to rank patients based on differences in success probabilities using area-under-the-curve (AUC) in a receiver-operating curve analysis, and the percentage of patients who would receive a significantly different probability compared to conventional age-based prognostic classification (584).

Of the clinical data available prior to treatment, the patient’s age is a key predictor of COH or IVF success and accounts for more than 50% of the relative importance of quantifiable predictors of IVF success (174,584,585). However, many other clinical predictors—ovarian reserve, BMI, reproductive history, clinical diagnoses, sperm

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1927"></a>

صفحه 1927
