parameters, total amount of gonadotropins used, oocyte parameters, and embryo scores— impact and predict treatment success.

Fertility providers are interested in using data and technology to improve the patient experience by offering personalized IVF success prognostics. To accomplish this, providers must first define their goals. The intended goal of the prediction model determines the clinical predictors used by the model. For example, a prediction model that aims to inform a patient’s decision to do her first IVF cycle should not use clinical predictors that can only be known after she starts IVF, such as the amount of gonadotropin used or oocyte and embryo parameters (174,585). A prediction model that aims to inform a patient’s decision to do eSET should use clinical predictors—such as the number of blastocysts—that are available up to the day of embryo transfer (586).

When choosing a prediction model, it is important to consider whether the dataset used to develop and test the model reflects the patient population to which the model will be applied. Specifically, several researchers continue to raise concerns that a model developed and tested in one center may not have the same performance or accuracy when applied to another center, or that rigorous steps may be required to test and recalibrate the model (587–590). This problem can be partially addressed by using a prediction model developed from data pooled from multiple centers or a national registry, though the applicability of the model to each individual center still needs to be tested (585,591–593). Alternatively, center-specific prediction models can be developed and tested based on each center’s own data (174,584,586,594). Since machine learning models are designed to learn, assessing the robustness of a model is paramount before applying it to clinical practice. For this reason, when evaluating predictive models in reproductive medicine, some have advised that proposed models and manuscripts first be reviewed by a mathematician or machine learning data scientist before publication (595).

Cessation of Therapy Patients must be accurately informed of estimated success rates and reasonable expectations for all therapeutic interventions (596). In the context of the probability of having a live birth from autologous oocytes in IVF, the ASRM Ethics Committee defines “very poor prognosis” as having 1% to 5% probability of live birth, whereas having less than 1% live birth probability is considered “futile” (597). It is generally accepted that women aged 44 to 45 have 1% to 2% live birth rate and those aged over 45 have less than 1% live birth rate (597–599), though a slightly higher IVF live birth rate has been reported for 45-year-old women (598). However, even when using donor eggs in IVF, racial disparities in live birth rates were reported with little understanding of the cause. Specifically, among racially concordant donors and recipients, Black and Hispanic recipients had significantly lower live birth rates compared to White recipients, while Asian recipients showed a nonsignificant trend toward lower live birth rates (600).

For patients who are younger than 40 and have failed multiple IVF treatments using their own eggs, it is possible that clinicians cannot identify a cause for the repeated failures (even if causes of infertility were known). In that case, it may be exceedingly difficult for clinicians to provide the prognosis and counsel with confidence toward cessation of IVF using autologous oocytes. RIF as a diagnosis or predictor of subsequent IVF treatment success is still heavily debated and the lack of mechanistic understanding of many cases of IVF failure means clinicians cannot be expected to provide targeted alternatives or prognosis (601–603).

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1928"></a>

صفحه 1928

Clinicians are expected to counsel patients with futile or extremely poor prognosis of IVF treatment about the limitations, risks, and alternative treatments. Although clinicians have a right to decline treatment, the treatment decision should be patient-centered and it may be reasonable to provide treatment for psychological closure, if the patient is thoroughly informed of the prognosis (597,599). In the United States and some EU countries where legally permitted, the use of donor eggs in IVF is a commonly used alternative treatment to the use of autologous eggs in IVF. The SART National Summary Report 2020 showed live birth rates in the 40% to 47% range based on the use of fresh donor eggs, frozen donor eggs, thawed embryos, and donated embryos (SART National Summary Report 2020) (575,604). Other options include natural cycle IVF, which has shown live birth rates of 8.5% and 5.9% for age groups 43 to 44 and 45 to 50, respectively, in a Chinese population (605). It is beyond the scope of this Chapter to discuss many other alternative treatments that have been reported with limited sample size, are still in the “innovation” phase or are not yet widely adopted.

Third-Party Reproduction When gametes and/or the ability to gestate a pregnancy are compromised through circumstances or disease, other reproductive options can be considered. These include use of donor sperm (“Male Factor”), donor oocytes, donor embryos, a gestational carrier, or a combination of these approaches. In contrast to donor gametes (sperm or oocytes), the decision to donate embryos is typically made after the embryos have been generated and there is a known surplus. A gestational carrier receives and gestates embryos created from the intended mother’s oocytes. Patients who choose to utilize a gestational carrier may have irreparable uterine factor infertility or suffer from medical conditions that contraindicate pregnancy. In true surrogacy, the birth mother is also the genetic mother but not the mother who will raise the child. Legal and psychosocial counseling are suggested for all parties embarking on any form of third-party reproduction.

Donor Oocyte

10 Patients with premature ovarian insufficiency, poor oocyte quality, poor ovarian response to stimulation, or failed fertilization or implantation after multiple ART cycles may be candidates to receive donated oocytes. A female same-sex couple may choose to have one partner undergo IVF and place the resulting oocytes fertilized with donor sperm into the other partner (606). With carefully selected donors using nonfrozen oocytes, live birth rates per cycle of donor oocyte IVF are ∼40% or higher regardless of the recipient’s age (160,575). Recipients must be aware that advanced recipient age is associated with higher risk for preeclampsia, diabetes, and cesarean section (607). Oocyte donors must endure all the interventions and risks of the ART process, except for embryo transfer and luteal support. Because of the intensity of therapy and the potential infectious disease and genetic risks for donor, recipient, and the resulting offspring, oocyte donors must be screened for infectious and heritable disorders similar to those performed for sperm donors (“Male Factor”) and undergo meticulous informed consent and a comprehensive psychosocial evaluation (129). Oocyte donors may be anonymous or known to the recipient (129). Oocyte recipients undergo endometrial preparation as described in the “ART Process” section, which may be synchronized with the donor for a fresh transfer (Fig. 36-6) or may be dissociated from the donor retrieval if using cryopreserved oocytes or embryos. Other topics, such as methods for donor recruitment and

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1929"></a>

صفحه 1929

financial compensation for the donor are challenging issues (608).

Figure 36-6 Regimens of ovarian stimulation and hormone replacement used to synchronize the development of ovarian follicles in the oocyte donor and the endometrial cycle in the recipient. hCG, human chorionic gonadotropin; OCP, oral contraceptive pill; GnRH-a, gonadotropin-releasing hormone agonist; TVA, ultrasound-guided transvaginal aspiration of oocytes. (Adapted from Chang PL, Sauer MY. Assisted reproductive techniques. In: Stenchever MA, ed. Atlas of Clinical Gynecology: Reproductive Endocrinology. Vol. 3. Current Sciences Group; 1998.)

Oocyte Cryopreservation Oocyte cryopreservation stopped being considered experimental by the ASRM in 2012, based on reports of excellent oocyte survival after vitrification and warming, and clinical pregnancy rates and safety being comparable to those of IVF. The utilization of cryopreserved oocytes has the potential to offer greater flexibility in pregnancy timing, more choices in the selection of a gamete donor, and reduced cost (609). The live birth rate using vitrified donor oocytes for IVF may be slightly lower than with fresh donor oocytes (609).

Planned oocyte cryopreservation (POC), aka “egg freezing,” allows women to defer childbearing while preserving fertility and minimizing further age-related fertility decline. There is very limited reporting of the ultimate live birth rates from IVF-embryo transfers using eggs from POC because many years generally transpire between the POC procedure and the transfer of resultant embryos. Based on the largest single-center report to-date, women can expect >50%

تصاویر استخراج‌شدهٔ همین صفحه

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1930"></a>

صفحه 1930

probability of having at least one live birth after having cryopreserved 20 or more mature oocytes at less than 38 years of age (610). Although ovarian tissue cryopreservation is a very different procedure that is still considered to be in the “innovation” stage, the IVF and spontaneous live birth rates from using cryopreserved ovarian tissue are 21% and 33%, respectively (611).

The use of cryopreserved oocytes from POC varies among fertility centers. For example, one study reported that 60% of patients did not return to use cryopreserved oocytes after POC while the use is less than 10% in patients who freeze oocytes for medical indications (such as prior to receiving gonadotoxic treatment). Clinicians should be aware of the careful counseling that should occur prior to performing any oocyte cryopreservation, but especially for POC since women who choose POC do not and may never have clinical infertility. POC counseling is challenging, as clinicians want to avoid being unnecessarily pessimistic and setting unrealistic expectations (609). Ultimate live birth rates likely also depend heavily on the age of the woman at the time of ovarian stimulation and egg retrieval.

Since female physicians, especially residents and fellows in training, are often faced with the need to defer family building, female physicians may better understand the potential benefits and limitations of POC (612). Black women are disproportionately affected with unexplained lower IVF live birth rates and Black women and other women of color pursuing medical careers face a more negative impact on family building (613). Therefore, it is important to raise awareness of the need to include POC counseling in the career and family counseling of all medical trainees, and specifically those of color.

Complications of Assisted Reproductive Technology

Risks of ICSI Risks of ICSI are detailed in “Male Factor.”

Cycle Cancellation Criteria for cycle cancellation vary; high-performing US clinics reported a threshold of ≤3 follicles (up to <5 follicles) (465). 10 Cycle cancellation in normal responders occurred in up to 6% of the cycles because of inadequate response and 1.5% of cycles for excessive response (176), and risk increases with older age and lower ovarian reserve (443). In 0.2% to 7% of retrievals, no oocytes will be obtained (507). Two proposed explanations include human error during the administration of hCG and early oocyte atresia despite normal follicular response (507).

Oocyte Retrieval The risks of oocyte retrieval include bleeding requiring transfusion or surgery, injury to adjacent structures requiring laparotomy, formation of a pelvic abscess leading to loss of reproductive function despite antibiotic prophylaxis, and risks related to anesthesia (614).

Multiple Gestation

9 Although the most serious complications of multiple gestations occur with high-order (≥3) multiples, twins have increased risks for low birth weight, preterm birth, and neurologic deficits when compared to singletons (615,616). Despite this, 20% of infertile patients view multiple pregnancy as a desired outcome (617). Because most patients lack insurance coverage for IVF, patients may push their physicians to take greater risks when

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1931"></a>

صفحه 1931

deciding on the number of embryos to transfer (617). The multiple pregnancy risk is higher in patients under the age of 35 who are undergoing IVF since their embryos are typically of better quality and implantation rates are higher, but multiple pregnancy can occur at any reproductive age (575). With increasing adherence to guidelines that suggest limitations on the number of embryos to transfer in each ART cycle and improved implantation rates that allow single embryo transfer (using patient’s own eggs), the percentage of ART-conceived live birth deliveries that yielded singletons increased from 76.1% in 2014 (in patients less than 35 years of age) to 94.5% in 2022 (575). Twin infant live birth rates (in patients less than 35 years of age and using their own oocytes) have decreased from 23.5% in 2014 to 4.2% in 2022; and the percentage of cycles that resulted in triplets or more decreased from 0.7% to 0.0% (575). 9 Most multiple pregnancies arising from ART are dizygotic, but monozygotic twinning occurs in 3.2% of IVF cycles (compared to a background rate of 0.4%) (617). Concerns have been raised that monozygotic twinning might increase after blastocyst culture, but this finding has not been consistent (525,618).

Selective Reduction Ten percent of multifetal pregnancies spontaneously lose at least one gestational sac during the first trimester. This loss rate increases to 21% in women >35 years of age (619). The spontaneous reduction rate to twins or singletons is much higher for triplets (14%) (620) than for quadruplets (3.5%) (619). 9 Selective pregnancy termination or multifetal reduction may be an option for some patients in whom spontaneous reduction does not occur by 11 to 13 weeks (621). Changes in abortion laws have impacted the availability of this treatment option. This involves first karyotyping each fetus through transabdominal chorionic villous sampling to preferentially reduce those that are abnormal, then injecting potassium chloride into the heart of the targeted fetus (621). Although selective reduction carries a 3% to 7% risk of losing the entire pregnancy when performed prior to 19 weeks, this is still lower than the 15% chance of spontaneously losing an entire triplet pregnancy (615,621). Selective fetal reduction is typically considered for triplet or higher pregnancies, but even reduction from twins to singletons may have benefits (615).

Ectopic and Heterotopic Pregnancy ART pregnancies effectively bypass the fallopian tubes to achieve pregnancy, but ectopic pregnancy (implantation outside the uterus) is still possible. Historically, 10 rates of ectopic pregnancy were higher in ART when compared to spontaneous conception, although more recent data have shown rates of ectopic pregnancy in ART to be similar to the general population rate of 2% (622). The absence of an intrauterine pregnancy on transvaginal US evaluation in conjunction with a maternal serum hCG level above a threshold of 1,500 to 2,500 mIU/mL suggests an abnormal gestation (567). This threshold or discriminatory zone is individualized to each institution depending on the characteristics of the hCG assay used and clinical expertise. A more conservative discriminatory zone decreases the risk of incorrectly diagnosing an ectopic pregnancy and terminating a viable pregnancy (567). 9 This is especially important in the setting of multiple gestation, which will produce higher hCG levels at an earlier stage (567). Heterotopic pregnancy involves concurrent intrauterine and ectopic pregnancy, usually within the tube (623) but ovarian implantations have been reported (624). 10 The incidence of heterotopic pregnancy, which is extremely rare in spontaneous conceptions, is notably higher after IVF treatment with an incidence as high as 1% (623).

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1932"></a>

صفحه 1932

Risk factors for heterotopic pregnancy include multiple gestation, smoking, previous tubal surgery, and prior PID, in addition to ART (623). As with standard ectopic pregnancies, pain and bleeding are the most common presenting findings with heterotopic pregnancies (623). Heterotopic pregnancies are most often diagnosed in the first 5 to 8 weeks of gestation using laparoscopy or laparotomy (623). Only 26% of heterotopic cases can be diagnosed with transvaginal US, possibly because of difficulties in sonographic interpretation in the presence of concomitant ovarian hyperstimulation (623). After treatment of a heterotopic gestation with laparoscopy, laparotomy, or US-guided injection of potassium chloride into the extrauterine pregnancy, the overall delivery rate for the intrauterine pregnancy is nearly 70% (623).

Ovarian Hyperstimulation Syndrome OHSS is a medical complication that is completely iatrogenic and unique to stimulatory infertility treatment (625). Its symptoms are the result of ovarian enlargement and fragility, extravascular fluid accumulation, and intravascular volume depletion (626). Proposed mechanisms for the characteristic fluid shifts that accompany OHSS include increased protein-rich fluid secretion from the stimulated ovaries, increased renin and prorenin within follicular fluid, and increased capillary permeability mediated by angiotensin (626). 10 Vascular endothelial growth factor (VEGF), whose expression in granulosa cells and serum is augmented by hCG, and a variety of inflammatory cytokines have been implicated in the pathogenesis of this disease (626). Two distinct patterns of OHSS onset have been described. Early OHSS occurs 3 to 7 days following the hCG trigger and is associated with the administration of exogenous hCG (627). 9 Late-onset disease occurs 12 to 17 days after the hCG trigger; it is the result of endogenous hCG secretion from the pregnancy and tends to be more severe with multiple gestation (627). Pregnancy outcomes are inconsistently affected by the presence of OHSS, with higher rates of biochemical losses but similar rates of clinical losses when compared to patients without OHSS (627).

Severity Classification

10 An RCT (628) described mild, moderate, and severe OHSS classifications considering criteria from Golan (625) and Navot (629) as follows: mild OHSS (grade 1 to 2) is associated with abdominal distention and discomfort, with grade 2 including nausea, vomiting or diarrhea accompanied by ovary diameter of at least 5 mm. Moderate or grade 3 includes grade 2 plus sonographic subclinical ascites near the liver or in the pelvis if the pocket measures >9 mm. Severe OHSS (grade 4 to 5) is associated with grade 3 plus clinical ascites, hydrothorax, or dyspnea, with grade 5 including hemoconcentration, renal insufficiency or oliguria, elevated transaminases, venous thromboembolism, or respiratory distress syndrome.

Risk Factors for OHSS Mild OHSS occurs commonly in both stimulated non-ART and ART cycles, but severe disease is rare in non-ART cycles (630). Severe disease is more common with ART, occurring in 5% of antagonist and nearly 9% of agonist protocols (628). Polycystic ovary syndrome, polycystic ovarian morphology with attendant elevated AMH levels (>3.36 ng/mL), and previous episodes of OHSS are major risk factors for the disease (556,626,628,630,631). E2 concentrations of

3,500 pg/mL and >6,000 pg/mL at the time of hCG trigger were associated with severe OHSS in 1.5% and 38% of patients, respectively (630). More than 20 preovulatory follicles were

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1933"></a>

صفحه 1933

associated with a 15% incidence of severe OHSS (630). When 20 to 29 oocytes were collected at retrieval, 1.4% of patients developed severe OHSS; this rose to 22.7% with ≥30 oocytes (630).

Management of OHSS If outpatient management is appropriate, the patient should be instructed to limit her activity, to weigh herself daily, and to monitor her fluid intake (at least 1 L/day of mostly electrolyte- balanced fluid) and urine output (626). Daily follow-up by telephone or visit is important and the patient should be reassessed if she notes worsening of the symptoms or if her weight gain increases to more than 2 lb per day (626). Indications for hospitalization include inability to tolerate oral hydration, hemodynamic instability, respiratory compromise, tense ascites, hemoconcentration, leukocytosis, hyponatremia, hyperkalemia, abnormal renal or liver function, and decreased oxygen saturation (626). Fluid intake and urine output need to be carefully measured, and admission to an intensive care setting can be considered if the patient has hyperkalemia, renal failure, respiratory failure, or thromboembolic disease (626). Although IV fluids may worsen ascites, they are essential to correct hypovolemia, hypotension, electrolyte abnormalities and oliguria (626). Albumin (25%) can be dosed 50 to 100 mg IV every 4 to 12 hours if further intravascular volume expansion is needed (626). Diuretics can be considered to improve weight gain and oliguria only after hypovolemia has been corrected (626). Thromboembolic prophylaxis should be given (626). Single or repeated transvaginal or transabdominal US–guided paracentesis may relieve pain, hydrothorax, or persistent oliguria (626). 10 Rapid large-volume fluid removal can be considered, as compensatory fluid shifts are unlikely to occur in this typically young healthy population, so long as the patient is carefully monitored (626).

OHSS Prevention No method will prevent OHSS completely, but recognition of high-risk patients is the first step in decreasing that risk (632). PCOS, elevated AMH (>3.4 ng/mL), high peak E2 (>3,500 pg/mL), multifollicular development (≥25 follicles), or a high number of oocytes retrieved (≥24 oocytes) are associated with an elevated risk of OHSS (632).

COH and GnRH Analogs Careful gonadotropin stimulation for monofollicular development is discussed under “Ovulatory Factor.” For ART, stimulation protocols for high-risk patients include lower initial COH doses of 150 IU to 225 IU and GnRH antagonists for LH surge prevention, which reduce the total dosage and duration of gonadotropin stimulation (628). 10 GnRH antagonists when introduced following oocyte retrieval appear to accelerate regression of severe early OHSS without any adverse effects on pregnancy and live birth rates (633).

Ovulation Triggering Decreasing the dose of hCG to reduce the incidence of OHSS is controversial (632). GnRH agonists can be used instead of hCG during antagonist cycles to induce an endogenous LH surge. The very short half-life of endogenous LH may reduce the incidence and/or severity of OHSS (632). As GnRH agonist triggers are associated with lower pregnancy rates, it may be a preferable option for patients who are not planning a fresh embryo transfer (632). 10 For those wanting to continue with a fresh cycle, low-dose hCG coadministered with a GnRH agonist trigger may prevent the lower pregnancy rates observed when a GnRH agonist is used alone (632).

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1934"></a>

صفحه 1934

Coasting Coasting may be considered when E2 levels are >4,500 pg/mL and/or there are 15 to 30 mature follicles present (631). During coasting, gonadotropin stimulation is withheld and E2 levels are checked daily (631). An initial rise of E2 is typically observed within the first 48 hours of the coast, but the levels should subsequently plateau or decrease (634). The patient may be triggered when serum E2 levels fall to <3,500 pg/mL (631). 10 If GnRH agonists are used for initial LH surge suppression, switching to an antagonist during the coast has been associated with improved outcomes (631). There is some controversy surrounding the effectiveness of this strategy, as a systematic review of four RCTs concluded that coasting does not decrease the risk of OHSS but may be associated with retrieval of fewer oocytes. Additionally, the ideal length of coasting has not yet been established (632,635).

Cycle Cancellation

10 Cycle cancellation may be recommended if there are >30 mature follicles, the coast duration is >4 days, or if E2 levels rise to >6,500 pg/mL during coasting (631,634).

Adjunctive Medications The adjunctive use of metformin is associated with decreased OHSS rates in PCOS patients (503). Aspirin and calcium use appear to decrease the risk of OHSS as separate adjuncts (632). Evidence suggests that the use of letrozole following hCG triggering is effective in preventing moderate-to-severe early-onset OHSS (636). Cabergoline, a dopamine agonist that inhibits VEGF production, decreased OHSS rates when given at 0.5 mg daily for 8 days from the day of hCG administration (632,637). Long-term use of cabergoline should be avoided as it may be associated with maternal valvular heart disease (631,634,638). 10 The benefit of albumin administration in decreasing rates of OHSS is unclear (632).

Embryo Cryopreservation

10 Cryopreservation of all embryos without transfer appears to reduce late-onset OHSS although early-onset OHSS may still occur (634). With improved freezing techniques, live birth rates following frozen/thaw transfers are similar to fresh transfer (557). More information on embryo cryopreservation can be found in the “ART Process” section.

In vitro Oocyte Maturation When first conceived, in vitro oocyte maturation (IVM) completely obviated the need to stimulate the ovaries with gonadotropins. During those IVM cycles, immature follicles were aspirated following hCG administration, and the retrieved oocytes are grown in vitro until they mature. Over the past 20 years, several protocols have been described in the literature such as those that utilize follicle priming with FSH with and without hCG trigger (639). IVM is no longer considered experimental but should only be offered at specialized centers with appropriate training.

Mature oocytes are fertilized by insemination or ICSI (640). Potential clinical uses of IVM include patients at high risk of OHSS or patients who have limited time for stimulation and retrieval prior to gonadotoxic therapy. Compared to conventional IVF, blastocyst conversion is lower when IVM is used. Further research is needed to compare clinical outcomes of IVM versus conventional IVF as well as follow-up studies evaluating the health of the offspring born via IVM (639).

Risk of Cancer After Fertility Therapy

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1935"></a>

صفحه 1935

Infertility by itself is a predisposing factor for ovarian cancer and breast cancer (641). Although treatments that promote incessant ovulation and elevated estrogen levels offer biologic plausibility for further increased cancer risk, data regarding the impact of infertility therapy on malignancy are inconsistent and limited by methodologic issues (641). The practice committee for the American Society of Reproductive Medicine completed an analysis of accumulated data of prior studies addressing the potential risk of cancer with fertility drug use (641). No association between fertility drug use and an increased risk of invasive ovarian cancer, breast cancer, or endometrial cancer could be established when evaluating the available data (641). Although several studies have shown a small increase in absolute risk of borderline ovarian tumors, there is a lack of consistent evidence that any specific fertility drug increases the risk of borderline ovarian tumors (641). When considering other cancers, there does not appear to be a risk of invasive thyroid cancer, colon cancer, or cervical cancer (641). 10 There is insufficient evidence to determine if there is an increased risk of melanoma or lymphoma (641).

Stress Stress, as manifested by anxiety or depression, is thought to be increased among women experiencing infertility (642). Stress is the most common reason for patients, even those with insurance coverage, to terminate fertility treatment (643). 10 Although the preponderance of the evidence suggests that stress does not adversely affect IVF outcome, additional studies are needed before any conclusions can be made (644–646). If psychological disorders are present, treatment should be offered regardless of fertility status.

Neonatal and Child Development With the ever-growing number of births resulting from ART, it is important to consider potential perinatal risks and long-term childhood outcomes. Multifetal gestation and its subsequent effect on maternal and fetal morbidity and mortality represent the most significant risk of ART (647). Risks of multiple gestation include preterm birth, low birth weight, small for gestational age, congenital anomalies, and perinatal mortality. Singleton pregnancies as an outcome of ART as compared to naturally occurring pregnancies have also been associated with higher risks, including a higher risk of perinatal mortality, preterm delivery, low birth weight, and small-for- gestational age status (648). Pregnancies conceived via ART are at a higher risk of birth defects, though it has been questioned whether it is the ART that increases the risk or the underlying cause of infertility leading to use of ART. Specifically, use of ICSI in an IVF cycle has been shown to increase the risk of birth defects, where IVF alone has not shown an increased risk in some studies (649). 10 The most important step in mitigating neonatal and long-term pediatric risk associated with IVF is continued emphasis on eSET (547). Patients should have a thorough history and physical examination performed, addressing any health problems or inherited conditions that could impact future pregnancy (647).

Preimplantation Genetic Diagnosis and Screening

11 The primary indication for PGD is to improve the chances of having healthy infants in families at high risk for a specific genetic disease (650). Following embryo biopsy, genetic testing can be performed on a blastomere (cell from day 3 embryo), polar body, or on blastocyst trophectoderm prior to transferring the embryo (651) (Fig. 36-7). Data favor biopsy at the blastocyst stage over the cleavage stage (575). Aneuploidy in embryos most commonly affects

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1936"></a>

صفحه 1936

chromosomes X, Y, 13, 14, 15, 16, 18, 21, and 22 (651). Fluorescence in situ hybridization (FISH) is a technique that assesses aneuploidy, translocation, other structural chromosomal defects, and sex chromosome content (651). FISH is technically limited by the number of distinct chromosomes that can be evaluated, giving the technique an inherently high false-negative rate. Newer genetic technologies have been developed that have the ability to assess the entire genome using array (Fig. 36-8) comparative genomic hybridization (aCGH) (Fig. 36-9), single nucleotide polymorphism (SNP) arrays, and next-generation sequencing (NGS) (Fig. 36-10) (652). 8 One-quarter of the cases of PGD are performed for single-gene disorders, most commonly myotonic dystrophy, Huntington disease, cystic fibrosis, fragile X syndrome, spinal muscular atrophy, tuberous sclerosis, Marfan syndrome, thalassemia, and sickle cell anemia (651). Because polymerase chain reaction (PCR) is required for single-gene disorder diagnosis, ICSI is performed during ART to avoid contamination from sperm bound to the zona pellucida (651). 8 PGD can be used for HLA tissue matching in an effort to produce a child whose cord blood or stem cells could help an existing affected child (651). Disadvantages to PGD include decreased post biopsy embryo survival, requirements for extended culture with an associated possibility that no embryos will be available for transfer or cryopreservation, false- positive and false-negative testing results, and controversies regarding disposition of nontransferred embryos (650,651).

Figure 36-7 1 Embryo biopsy procedure. A small opening is made in the zona pellucida and depending on the stage of embryonic development the removal of a polar body (fertilized or unfertilized oocyte), blastomere (cleavage stage 8 cell), or trophectoderm (blastocyst) is performed.

Copyright © 2016 Springer Science Business Media New York.)

Preimplantation Genetic Testing for Aneuploidy (PGT-A) PGT-A can be performed in couples without known chromosomal anomaly, mutation, or other genetic abnormalities (650). 8 PGT-A focuses on identifying euploid embryos for transfer, with the goal of increasing implantation rates and decreasing miscarriage rates (652). Embryo aneuploidy is significantly associated with abnormal cleavage, particularly with

تصاویر استخراج‌شدهٔ همین صفحه

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1939"></a>

صفحه 1939

female age >40, while similar aneuploidy rates are noted in younger women, perhaps owing to auto-correct mechanisms (654). A prospective intention-to-treat analysis in patients with a mean age of 32 found a higher live birth rate with the first blastocyst transfer attempt with PGT-A- based selection compared with morphology-only selection (655). Biopsy performed once for PGT-A yields similar ongoing pregnancy rates for fresh embryos as compared to untested frozen embryo subsequently thawed for PGT-A and then refrozen (656). Biopsy itself was not associated with a detriment in neonatal outcomes. However, repeating biopsies in cases when the first biopsy yields no result reads may negatively impact outcomes (656). Embryo grading is still useful to prioritize supernumerary PGT-A euploid embryos for transfer, but even poorly graded embryos have live birth potential, albeit lower than embryos graded morphologically good (657). Poorly graded PGT euploid embryos display defects in primitive endoderm gene expression and cell signaling as compared to good graded euploid embryos, which may lead to higher rates of implantation failure and pregnancy (658). 9 When embryos are known to be euploid, single embryo transfer is universally recommended to decrease the risk of multiple gestation (547). Although it seems intuitive that replacing only euploid embryos should improve pregnancy and live birth rates in patients with advanced age, recurrent pregnancy loss, or implantation failure, study outcomes have not been consistent (650,651,659–662). PGT-A euploid embryo recipients of normal BMI have improved implantation rates compared to obese or underweight recipients, indicating a detrimental uterine impact on implantation from disordered BMI that is independent of embryo ploidy (663,664). One study found a reduced miscarriage rate with PGT-A, but only among patients who retrieved fewer than 15 eggs (665). A significant challenge to the application of pre-implantation genetic screening is chromosomal mosaicism (Figs. 36-11 and 36-12), which arises from mitotic errors during embryo development, and has raised concerns of how well the biopsy sample represents the embryo as a whole, and whether subsequent embryonic self-correction can restore euploidy Mosaic embryos display increased numbers of apoptotic trophectoderm cells, which may underpin differences in outcomes as compared to euploid embryos (653). Embryos designated as low mosaics, having a minority of the biopsy impacted by aneuploid status, seem to have a particularly good prognosis for a healthy pregnancy that approaches that of results with euploid embryos (666). Embryos with nonmosaic segmental aneuploidy, involving a partial loss or gain of a chromosome, may also be reasonably considered for transfer, per an encouraging case series of 12 patients with an ongoing pregnancy rate of 33% with normal prenatal genetic testing at the time of the publication (667). However, there are case reports of embryonic mosaicism persisting into the fetus as demonstrated by amniocentesis; in one case, the persisting abnormality was a low-level mosaic segmental chromosome 1 deletion, while another case involved a low-level mosaic trisomy 21 (668,669). Genetic counseling is recommended prior to consideration of any noneuploid transfers.

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1940"></a>

صفحه 1940

Preservation of Fertility in Cancer Patients Improved cancer treatments, such as chemotherapy, surgery, and radiotherapy, have greatly enhanced survival, such that many cancer survivors contemplate parenthood (669). Unfortunately, those same life-saving treatments can diminish fertility potential in men and women (670). Cancer itself does not usually affect oocytes, but certain chemotherapeutic drugs or radiation damage may adversely affect ovarian reserve and uterine function, particularly in older women (670). Patients with a new cancer diagnosis and future fertility plans should be provided with urgent referral and consultation with a reproductive endocrinologist to discuss fertility preservation. 4 If time allows, prior to commencement of cancer treatment, women can undergo an ART cycle with embryo or oocyte cryopreservation, the current standard of care. Ovarian tissue cryopreservation (OTC) is no longer considered experimental and is the only option available to prepubertal girls. It is also a viable option in patients who cannot delay gonadotoxic treatment for ∼2 weeks to undergo oocyte or embryo cryopreservation (671,672). Though limited, the available data reports pregnancy rates of ∼30% and live birth rates of 23% to 25% after transplantation of previously cryopreserved ovarian cortex. OTC is contraindicated in patients with malignancies that may be present in the ovarian tissue at the time of OTC (e.g., leukemias) because of the concern for reseeding of the tumor with future transplantation (673–675). In vitro maturation (IVM) of oocytes, the process of recovering immature oocytes to try to mature them in the lab, is a select option in specialized centers, but it is not widely available (639).

Fertility sparing treatments may be considered in certain situations, including radical trachelectomy for cervical cancer, progestin therapy in lieu of hysterectomy for uterine cancer,

تصاویر استخراج‌شدهٔ همین صفحه

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1942"></a>

صفحه 1942

and unilateral oophorectomy for some ovarian tumors (669). Ovarian function may be preserved when ovarian transposition is performed prior to radiation therapy (639). It is unclear whether ovarian suppression with GnRH analogs during chemotherapy or radiation is protective for fertility and it is still considered investigational (639). GnRH analogs, such as Lupron, should be considered for menstrual suppression in patients with thrombocytopenia as a result of chemotherapy used during stem-cell transplantation. There are data to support the use of GnRH analogs in premenopausal patients with breast cancer to reduce the incidence of POI but should not replace other fertility preservation methods; it can be used in conjunction with oocyte/embryo cryopreservation or OTC (675,676).

In men, cancer directly affects gametogenesis and cancer treatments cause more fertility damage when given at younger ages (669). 3 Semen and sperm cryopreservation prior to cancer treatment, when feasible, is the standard fertility preservation method in men (639).

ART Access and the Provision of Care Over the past four decades, ART live birth rates have increased while complications such as higher-order multiple births and OHSS have decreased. At the time of writing, 10 million babies have been born from ART worldwide and ART contributed to ∼800,000 births globally and or 80,000 births (or 2%) in the United States annually (676–678). Excellent safety has also been achieved with multiple birth rates below 10% (US, UK) and at 12.7% (EU) (676,679–681).

As the definition of clinical infertility becomes more inclusive, indications for ART have also broadened to provide fertility or family-building care to one in six couples in need. In addition to couples unable to conceive after at-home attempts, ART is also used by same-sex or LGBTQ couples, single women pursuing planned oocyte cryopreservation, families wishing to prevent hereditary, disabling genetic diseases from passing to their progeny, couples needing third-party reproduction and patients wishing to preserve fertility prior to sterilizing medical therapy for cancer or other diseases.

Globally, ART access and success vary widely. Although more countries (e.g., many EU countries, Singapore, China, Japan, parts of Canada) are receiving increasing government funding for ART to support health equity, sustain national populations and combat declining fertility rates, most countries, including the United States, Sub-Saharan Africa, and low- and middle-income countries (LMIC) in LATAM, South Asia, and East Asia do not have national government mandated or funded ART. Specifically, in LMIC, despite the prevalence of infertility is 3.5% to 16.7% and 30% to 40% in Sub-Saharan Africa, ART access is particularly limited, and the ART live birth rates are generally lower than in the United States and EU (682,683). A full summary of global ART access and success is beyond the scope of this Chapter (676,679–681,683).

The ART access and success landscape in the United States is complex, requiring in-depth investigation and the testing of a variety of solutions. ART is vastly underutilized (684). Problems of high financial costs, inadequate or lack of insurance coverage, emotional stress are made worse by provider and embryologist shortages (685–687). Despite increasing coverage for fertility services, most Americans do not have ART coverage. While CDC reported that 238,126 patients had 413,776 ART cycles performed at 453 reporting clinics in the United States in 2019, it has been estimated that 2 million IVF cycles should be performed annually in the United States to meet patients’ needs (685–688). A variety of innovative solutions are being tested. For example, advanced practice providers (APPs), nurses, and general obstetricians-gynecologists

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<a id="page-1943"></a>

صفحه 1943

are training for and performing many functions that were practiced solely by REI physicians in the past. The adoption of technology may also support more efficient and streamlined patient communications, such as patient education, counseling and informed consent, to improve patients’ experience and reduce staff burnout.

Especially concerning is ART access and success disparities experienced by Black and Hispanic women in the United States. The adjusted relative risk of failing ART treatment is 1.24 for Black race in an age-adjusted comparison. Black women have a higher prevalence of uterine and tubal pathologies, and they have 25% less ovarian reserve compared to White women matched for age. Overall, Black women have 29% lower ART live birth rate and 35% lower cumulative live birth rate compared to White women (689,690). Seifer et al. proposed the US racial and ethnic Disparities in ART (DART) hypothesis, in which a framework of four to six overarching factors—delay in obtaining ART, higher patient dropout rate, provider factors, and patient biologic factors—exacerbate poor utilization and outcomes of ART for Black women in the United States (689,690). A gap exists for Hispanic women and, to a lesser degree, Asian women as well (691,692).

Ironically, the medical profession itself has been criticized for not being supportive of women physician and physician parents in pursuing fertility care and family building. This problem is gaining more attention and various efforts, such as implementing work–life balance during residency and fellowship training and provision of funding to pursue planned oocyte cryopreservation, are in progress (693–695).

These serious problems affecting access to ART and treatment success have been exacerbated by decades of underfunding in women’s health services, reproductive research and advocacy for both. Medical professionals and health care providers have an important role in leading and supporting efforts to remove disparities and to educate the public to realize the potential of ART to help more people have a family (684,696,697).

## Chat

🌿

## Novaks

42 sources·Jul 9, 2026

متن پیش‌رو به بررسی جامع **سلامت جنسی زنان** و عوامل گوناگون موثر بر **اختلالات عملکرد جنسی** می‌پردازد. این منابع با تکیه بر جنبه‌های **فیزیولوژیک، روان‌شناختی و ارتباطی**، چرخه‌های پاسخ جنسی را تحلیل کرده و تاثیر بیماری‌های مزمن، سرطان‌های زنان و تغییرات هورمونی را تبیین می‌کنند. نویسندگان بر اهمیت **ارتباط پزشک و بیمار** برای شناسایی مشکلاتی نظیر بی‌میلی جنسی و دردهای لگنی تاکید کرده و الگوهای درمانی مختلفی را ارائه می‌دهند. همچنین، نقش **مداخلات روان‌درمانی** مانند درمان‌های شناختی-رفتاری و ذهن‌آگاهی در بهبود کیفیت زندگی جنسی مورد بحث قرار گرفته است. در نهایت، این نوشتار با نگاهی به **مراحل مختلف زندگی** از بارداری تا یائسگی، راهکارهای مدیریت اختلالات را برای کادر درمان بازگو می‌کند.

چگونه عوامل روانی و کیفیت رابطه بر میل جنسی زنان تأثیر می‌گذارند؟

مهم‌ترین ملاحظات و آمادگی‌های لازم پیش از انجام جراحی‌های ژنیکولوژی چیست؟

تغییرات طبیعی پاسخ جنسی در دوران بارداری و یائسگی چگونه مدیریت می‌شوند؟

42 sources
